“THE BULLETIN" BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps (of any province) equal to the price stated (which is published price with
postage added), Books and Periodicals in the subjoined lists will be forwarded to any Australasian address.
SOME RECENT BOOKS.
Two recent volumes in Ward, Lock, anc
Bowden’s Colonial Library are Guy Booth K
" Strange Company ” and Outrara Tr
tram’s “Dead Gallant” and “King o
Hearts ” (bound together). The excelle
printing and binding, and the admirab
illustrations of this series, make some of ti
older “ colonial ” libraries look almost mea
by comparison. For there is an evolution :
libraries as in other things, and the imitate
is frequently able to surpass the invento
“In Strange Company” is compounded o
crude imagination and clumsy contrivanc
The characters—a banker with brains enoug
to embezzle £250,000, which he is foo
enough to cart about in barrels of specie
a commonplacfe villain who at one moment:
chased by a mob, and in the next poses
chief spider of a world-wide web ; an ii
tellectual villain who flees from the spider i
a schooner with Tahiti painted on the stern
and sails straight to Tahiti that the spide:
may catch him, though he has money enoug
to buy 200 schooners ; an honest fool wh
carries the price of a fortune in a locke
round his neck, ready for robbing (as didth
intellectual villain), &c.—need a high!
uncritical audience to swallow them. Outrar
Tristram is anew historical romancer of ver
creditable calibre, and his two readable tale
perhaps approach as near to Weyman
(among recent writers) facile-princeps qualit
as anything which can readily be quoted.
Two splendid-value books are the collec
tion of weird short stories “Aut Diabolu
aut Nihil” (35., 45.) and Kipling’s “Bar
rack-Room Ballads ’ (35., 4s.) —Just out i
grateful “colonial library ” form.
Here is the dramatic close of Louis Becke’
new book, “ His Native Wife ” (Is. 2d.):
blowly -Barrington opened his eyes and gazec
stupidly around him. A raging thirst and a sounc
of someone sobbing had roused him from hi
death like sleep, and in a faint voice he called fo
water.
1 hank God! ” murmured Helen, and raisin
his head on her arm she placed a young cocoanu
ko his lips.
He drank, and then with a heavy sigh sank bac
on the rolled-up mat that formed his pillow, anc
dosed his eyes again.
She knelt beside him for a few moments wit
her hands clasped tightly together, and then ben
down and kissed him—for the last time.
Then came the sound of crunching grave
outside and the doorway of the house wa
darkened by two figures, but she neither heard no
Sr w them as she sobbed out her heart over the un
conscious man.
“See, Nader, ec thy white husband and the
Christ-woman for whom he hath cast thee off,’
and tfi n the old «oman slipped a knife into the
girl’s hand.
As Nadee sprang forward Helen raised her face
and then the knife flashed and sank deep dowi
into her heart and stilled it for ever.
A wild, shrieking laugh made Kate Trenton
and Jose Herrera spring to their feet, to see a
hideous old woman with long, snow-white hair
standing at the door of the chiefs house ; and the
next moment a young girl, as fair-skinned almost
as Kate hoiself, stepped outside.
Again that awful, screeching laugh rang out,
and the hag took the girl by the hand and led her
tut in full view of the village. Then she spoke.
‘‘.See, O men of LSsap, the red hand of
N T ftd e. Hold thou it up, my wood-dove, and let
;h m see the blood of the Christ-woman who stole
:hy lover from thee with her strong witchcraft.”
And Nadee, with blazing eyes and panting
•osom, held up the bloodied knife.”
, At sunset that night the whale-boat, with Kate
IVenton s head pillowed against her lover’s bosom,
evas fifty miles away ; and Jack Barrington with
i glad cry awoke to find bending over him the
jalm face of his native wife.

COUGHS, GOLDS ON THE CHEST, BRON-
CHITIS AND ASTHMA RAPIDLY
CURED BY Hearne’s Bronchitis Cure.
Those who have taken this medicine are amazed
at its wonderful influence. Sufferers from any
form of bronchitis, distressing cough, difficulty of
breathing, hoarseness, pain or soreness in the
chest, experience delightful and immediate relief,
and to those who are subject to colds on the chest
it is invaluable, as it effects a complete cure. It
is most comforting in allaying tickling and irrita-
tion in the throat, and giving strength to the voice,
and it never allows a cough or asthma to become
chronic. No house should be without it, as, taken
at the beginning, a dose is generally sufficient,
and a complete cure is certain.

and 59 other leading residents.

announcements deserve, but in this they would
make a great mistake,

by taking notice of what we have already done
and can do, as proved by thousands of grateful
acknowledgments of our skill which we hold, it
may Save Your Life and that of your dear ones.

WE HAVE CURED ALL DISEASES,

IHB ORDER OF THE BATH.
This is an Order of Knighthood, much prized by
people who have a PENCHANT for distinctive
initials and TINSEL decoration. The circum-
stances under which it is conferred are various.
Sometimes its acceptance is involved in “ A
NEW WAY TO PAY OLD DEBTS," the
recipient being content to wipe off old scores.
On the other hand, the distinction occasional y
falls to the great and brave, to distinguished
soldiers, colonial Premiers, and wily politicians.
Many a man would give ANY MONEY for the
privilege of C. B. after his name, and there are
authenticated cases of enormous sums having
been paid, in one way or another, when there was
a chance of becoming a 0.8. or a G. 0.8.

“ In this world we honor—and we rightly
honor—the strong, the successful, and the rich.
We despise the weak, the dependent, and the
poor.”— Walter Besant (lately knighted) in the
Argus.
The young Duke of Marlborough, like his
shady father and pugnacious uncle Randolph, has
captured a rich ’Murkan wife—Miss Vanderbilt,
the most gilded girl in the market (dowry,
£2,000,000). The bridegroom is to have a
£600,000 settlement. Love of money runs in the
blood. The original Malbrook, who plundered
the public exchequer in his prime, was a notorious
sponger upon the ladies in his early days.
The recent Melb. bench-packing allega-
tions recall the old story -of the Queenslander
charged with “ illegally using,” who got a few
friends on the bench and then pleaded shortness
of sight, unable to see brands. Discharged with-
out a stain on his character. And, to prove that
the verdict was genuine, he immediately after-
wards for a wager wrote the Lord’s Prayer on a
sixpence.
The Bulletin committed the wickedest
error any journal can be guilty of when it described
Miss Jennings-Oarrnichael as the original Vic-
torian girl to obtain a trained nurse’s certificate.
That misstatement touches the lady’s age very
deeply. Even a poetess, amidst her strivings after
an immortal fame, retains a sweet, womanly objec-
tion to seeming about 20 years older than her birth
certificate. Miss Carmichael was not the first of
Victorian trained nurses, but the first pupil to
take a certificate from the nurses’ training-school
at the Children’s Hospital, Melb. This explana-
tion should enable the bardess’s devoted admirers
to breathe again.
The branches at 283 George-street, Sydney,
and 586 Collins-street, Melbourne, of the Silverton
(Eng.) India-rubber, Gutta-percha and Telegraph
Works Co., worthily represent a world-famous
institution. This co., besides being the boss-
manufacturer of all rubber goods, is, perhaps,
the largest submarine cable manufacturer and
layer extant—its fleet of steamers has a carrying
capacity of 12,000 tons. The co’s Silverton works
cover 15 acres; employ 3000 hands ashore and
400 afloat; pay <£loo,ooo annually in wages alone;
use 2000 incandescent lights in their works ; pro-
duce 1,250,0001 b. of rubber goods per annum. The
co. is prepared not only to supply Australia with
all the rubber goods she demands, but to teach
her that there are other rubber goods which srie
should have—for instance, how sadly the bushrnan
wants educating who prefers rheumatism to the
co’s ground sheet 1 Sporting requisites include
tennis balls and shoes, cricketing shoes and pads ;
fishing stockings and. trousers; billiard cushions.
Druggists’sundries are waterproof sheeting, rubber
bandages, air and water-beds and pillows. Cloth-
ing takes in rubber boots, shoes, waterproof
collars, cuffs, overcoats, ponchos, capes, coatings,
ladies’ cloaks, etc. There are door springs, door
stops, carriage-springs’ bumpers, wringers’ and
mangles’ rubber-covered rollers, railway-truck
buffers, rock and brick drill machines’ buffers.
There are also wires for electric lighting, torpedo
apparatus, a Pacific cable, canvas and rubber
belting.*
A curious problem is suggested by the cir-
cumstances connected with the suicide of the late
Constable M'Phee, of Cranbourne (Vic.). It was
stated at the inquest that he had, when a young
man, been thrown from his horse, sustaining a
fracture of the skull from which he had suffered
internally ever since. The coroner’s jury, taking
these facts into consideration, found that deceased
shot himself in a fit of temporary insanity which
they believed to be the result of said injury.
Problem : If, instead of killing himself, a man
whose brain was fractured 15 or 16 years before,
but who during many of those years was con-
sidered to be sufficiently sane and responsible to
be a policeman, killed somebody else —what view
would gaol-doctors take of his action, and what
would be the verdict of a jury (not necessarily
charged by Judge ) empanelled to try him
on a charge of murder ? Eor, after all, it is a
mere toss-up whether homicidal mania ends in
murder, or suicide, or both.
In Sydney D.T. office lately Mr. Frank
J. Bevan put up an Australian record on the
linotype machine. In 15min,, under the stop-
watch, Bevan set up 78 lines of solid nonpareil
matter, without lead or rule, or headline of any
kind, amounting to 4056 ens. This performance,
which equals 16,224 ens per hour, is, remarks the
Telegraph, all the more remarkable when it is
remembered that Mr. Bevan has had less than six
months’continn.; i-notice as an operator. The
previous record « .. .fe by Mr. Philip Balls, an
American exp i came to Australia to take
charge of th,- b ■ *at the office of the Mel-
bourne Eveni.n \.ld, and who, on his arrival
in Sydney, on June 18 last (when just off a long
sea-voyage and with a machine new to him), set
up in the Telegraph office 67 lines of solid non-
pareil in the same time, viz., 15min., equal to
13,936 ens per hour.
The old Hobart firm of A. M'Gregor and
Co. has been wound up. Largely identified
with shipping and mercantile pursuits, the name
of Alick M'Gregor, who for years was one of the
representatives for Hobart in the Leg, Council,
was widely-known. Besides owning a number of
whalers, of which only two remain fit for active
service, the firm had several clippers engaged in
the London-Hobart trade, and some smart inter-
provincial tenders—the now Sydney-owned Loon-
gana, a handsome craft, being one of the last built
for them. During the last year or two they have
disposed of most of their fleet, and the last of
their “deep water” ships, the trim Harriet
M'Gregor, passed into Danish hands recently in
London. The selling of these ships has been a
decided loss to the port of Hobart. Advancing
years and declining health have caused “old
Alick ” to relinquish business and retire to his
superb mansion at Battery Point, which, com-
manding a view of the city and harbor, is often
mistaken by strangers for Govt. House.

E beg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “ Allen and
(winter’s Straight-cut No. 1,” “Cameo ” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called Pure Gold ” and “ Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
, supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“ 17m2.EE CASTLES” and £c C-A-IP STRICT.’ 5
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the nterest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co., Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills’ “Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people
should or should not smoke.

66 Yankee Boodle Mended with Polities .
The above is, of course, a caricature of the famous American national picture, which will be
given later on.
“YANKEE DOODLE” is everywhere spoken of as the finest plug-tobacco that has evei
appeared here, and it has won greater success during the short time it has been on the market than
anything previously. This is no idle boast, and, to prove it, any smoker can test the statement, for
there are few towns in N.S.W. where “YANKEE DOODLE” is not known and sold. Its sale
has this year increased nearly one hundred (100) per cent over that of last year. And why is this?
Simply that “ Y ANKEE DOODLE ” has struck the popular taste. In the first place, it comes, after
years of careful study, from a combination of certain choice Virginian tobaccos, so blended and
exquisitely flavoured, that any smoker who can stand a tobacco heavier than the pure gold-leaf is
forced to admit that he has at last discovered something far and away better than anything he has
been accustomed to. DIXSON & SONS have taken over half-a-century to accomplish this; and, as
their leading business has been built up on their reputation for QUALITY, they could not afford tc
make the foregoing statement if any doubt existed. DIXSON & SONS, who use more American leaf
than all the other Tobacco Factories combined, are noted for several high-class brands“CON-
QUEROR,” “ SIR ROGER,” “ CHAMPION,” and “DOLLAR;” but “YANKEE DOODLE’
ranks supreme.

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 19, 1895,
The Dean Case—Revised Version.
The confessions made within the last few
days by George Dean, by his solicitor
(Meagher), and by Smith, the North Shore
chemist—in short, the confessions made
almost simultaneously by almost everybody
directly or indirectly connected with the
famous Sydney poisoning-case—have sud-
denly cleared up one of the most mysterious
criminal problems of this decade, and ruined
the Dean fetish beyond all redemption.
The confessions which will probably be made
by one or two other persons as soon as the
police can find them are likely to supply the
few details that are still wanting. And
after its manner in such cases, the great,
illogical public which, a few weeks ago, was
in a state cf unreasoning adoration of Dean
—such unreasoning adoration that it would
probably have put him in Parliament if it
had had the chance, and actually did put his
blatherskiting solicitor there, by way of
honoring Dean at second-hand—has now
rushed bo the opposite extreme. It is pre-
pared now to “ canonise ” Dean’s much-
wrungod wife, whom it hated and loathed a
little while ago, despite the fact that no
direct evidence had ever appeared against
her, any more than it had then appeared
against Dean himself. A section of it
is almost even prepared to canonise his
mother-in-law, who still stands out con-
spicuously as a most unfit person for such a
purpose, and is also prepared to canonise
Judge Windeyer, whose method of con-
ducting the trial was the principal cause
of the after-trouble. And for the mo-
ment it has almost unanimously decided
that interference with any legal sentence
is a mistake. In short, it is in such
a mood that for the next few months it will
be prepared bo acquiesce in the hanging of
anybody on almost any evidence, no matter
by what unfairness the verdict is obtained.
The general decision of the press is that
Judge Windeyer s impartiality and legal
acumen have been fully vindicated. And in
the midst of this popular frenzy it is worth
while to point out that the original trial of
D. an is just as unfair, as lopsided, as unjust
and as utterly unjustifir*' 1 - now as it was
five or six months ago. Of all the execra-
tions which The Bulletin heaped on that
famous burlesoue it takes back not one word,
and all the recent revelations do not alter the
situation as it was last April in the least,
* * •
When Dean was originally convicted no
one had seen him poisoning his victim ; no
one had found him in possession of any
poison ; and no one brought forward a
scintilla of evidence to show that accused had
ever obtained poison from any source what-
ever. There was no motive, or shadow of a
motive, alleged to show why he should want
to get rid of his wife. Despite most
strenuous efforts to drag her in, the cus-
tomary “other woman” never appeared in
the case ; no one attempted to prove that
the prisoner was jealous of Mrs. Dean, or
had any cause to be jealous of her,
or had anything to gain by her death.
The medical evidence, as understood by
the jury and narrated by the reporters,
was absurdly and hopelessly contradic-
tory. It tended to show, in the first
place, that Mrs. Dean had taken enough
poison to kill half-a-dozen women. Then
being confronted with the difficulty of
explaining how a woman who was fatally
poisoned six times over, didn’t die, or even
come within measurable distance of death,
the Attorney-General took refuge in the
glaringly absurd assertion that the two
poisons administered were antidotes to each
other. That ridiculously inaccurate state-
ment remains still as an ugly blot on the
acumen of the prosecution ; it helped to
bring a guilty man within a short distance
of the gallows, and it might quite as readily
have brought an innocent man there instead.
Even Mrs. Dean did not assert directly that
she saw her husband putting poison into her
food and drink ; she merely detailed certain
suspicious movements on his part. Her evi-
dence was corroborated to an appreciable
extent by her mother only, and even her
corroboration was limited; moreover, it pre-
sently transpired that, instead of being the
spotless person which the police (notwith-
standing their knowledge to the contrary)
tacitly represented her to be, Mrs. Seymour
was a lady whose long and dismally ugly past
made her testimony a thing to bo received
with much reservation.
The evidence which was then before the
court was not sufficient to justify any Judge
or any jury in regarding Dean’s guilt as
legally proved. In fact, there was no proof
at all, in the legal sense of the term. Yet
Judge Windeyer summed up strongly against
the prisoner, and declared, almost in so
many words, that his guilt was a certainty.
Also, when even his very “straight” sum-
ming-up failed to bring the jury to a decision,
he took extraordinary means to drive it
to the conclusion at which he obviously had
himself arrived. He sent for the jurymen,
who were supposed to come to an absolutely
independent opinion, uninfluenced by any-
thing outside the evidence, on a matter of life
or death, and expressed his astonishment
that they should hesitate at all on so simple
and obvious a matter. He told them that
they had a painful duty to perform, and, as
the acquittal of an innocent man is not in
any sense a painful duty, this certainly
implied that the duty in question was to con-
vict the prisoner. He pointed out to them
that they might recommend Dean to mercy,
(mercy for Dean, who, if guilty, had cold-
bloodedly poisoned his wife in childbirth !)
and, as a recommendation to mercy can only
attach to a verdict of “guilty,” this was
another definite intimation as to the kind of
verdict it was their duty to bring in. He
denounced the theory of the defence as
“ monstrous,” and he intimated to the jury-
men that unless they came to a prompt
decision—in other words, unless they left off
weighing the evidence and hurried to a con-
clusion —they would necessarily be locked
up till Monday morning, it being then late
on the evening of Saturday. Under, or at
any rate after, all this pressure, the jury,
which had then disagreed for over seven
hours, arrived at exactly the sort of verdict
which the Judge had so distinctly suggested,
and did so in the short space of seven
minutes.
# • *
The people who were responsible for the
memorable agitation which followed were :
(I) The police, who failed utterly to trace the
sale of poison to Dean ; (2) the Crown
lawyers who, according to Julian Salomons,
failed so Utterly in the same direction that
the witness Smith was never even asked
whether he had sold the poison ; (3) the
Crown lawyers again who put forward Mrs.
Seymour as a reputable witness, whereas the
public presently learned things concerning
her which seriously injured her apparent
credibility ; (4) Attorney-General Want in
particular, who tilled in an obvious gap in
the medical evidence by adopting a theory
that was wholly untenable, and which
should have at once struck any man
with any experience of legal-medical
questions as untenable ; (5) and, above
all, Judge vWindeyer, whose method
of bringing pressure on the jury roused an
almost unanimous howl throughout the
press, and led even the usually placid and
always law-abiding Argus to draw a parallel
between his remarks and those of Judge
Jeffreys when he drove a jury to an unjust
verdict in the case of Alice Lisle. All the
confessions and revelations which have
transpired since cannot alter the situation as
it was then. Judge Windeyer had no pro-
phetic knowledge of vvht.'> was to be revealed
a few months later. All he had to go upon
was the evidence as it stood last April, and
on that evidence the verdict of “guilty”
was wholly unjustifiable. The fact that Mr.
Windeyer arrived by the wrong road at the
right conclusion does not prove his accur-
acy or reliability of judgment by one
iota. He arrived at the same conclusion
in the Mount Rennie case, and there is the
very strongest reason for believing him to
have been wrong in essential particulars.
He arrived at the same conclusion in the
Butler case, and he was undoubtedly
wrong. He arrived again at his too
frequent conclusion in the Dean case, and
he was right—as it happens. In every case
there are necessarily only two possible
alternatives guilt or innocence. The
man who backs one alternative out
of a possible two must be right sometimes.
The man who tips one horse out s of a
possible two, or who always prophesies
rain in a climate where it rains three days in
the week, or who tries persistently to pick
the higher of two cards, must win now and
then, but because he wins he doesn’t neces-
sarily show foresight or legal acumen or im-
partiality, or any other notable quality.
• • •
Moreover, Judge Windeyer, even apart
from his remarkable conduct at the trial,
was the principal cause of the agitation, by
the fact that he was Judge Windeyer. The
public had—and will have again when the
present feeling has died away —an instinctive
distrust of the Mount Rennie Judge and his
methods of conducting criminal trials. Had
any other Justice on the N.S.W. Bench ob-
tained or recorded the same verdict, even on
the same miserably insufficient evidence, and
expressed his conviction after—not before—
the jury’s decision that the verdict was a
just one, George Dean would very likely
have been hanged and forgotten long ago.
In Mr. Windeyer’s case, under the same
circumstances, such a thing was impossible.
The public had come to regard him as a per-
sonage whose idiosyncracy it was to enforce
his own views of a case upon the jury, and
it acted accordingly. It was, and still is,
fully justified in its action. All that has
since transpired does not alter by one single
fraction the fact that if George Dean had
been hanged or imprisoned on the evidence
as it was then, or on a verdict obtained
under the circumstances that characterised
the Dean verdict, it would have been
a blot on civilisation. The medical tes-
timony on which the prosecution relied was
scouted by the two expert doctors, F. Norton
Manning and Sydney Jones, who were ap-
pointed to the Commission on the ground
that they were among the most eminent and
experienced members of their profession in
Sydney. And even the third Commissioner,
Rogers, Q.C., who sent in an adverse
report, did not venture to assert that the
evidence was satisfactory. He merely stated,
as the sole reason for his verdict, his
childishly unsophisticated belief that a
young woman just on the threshold of
life could not have poisoned herself—and
as a third party might, on the evidence
then available, have been the real criminal,
or there might have been no criminal at all,
this impotent statement was wholly irrele-
vant. If the methods of the Dean trial are
allowed to continue because in that case the
right man was found guilty by accident, then
almost anybody may be found guilty of
almost anything, and the law cannot always
strike the right man by accident.
* * •
It has been a frequent assertion, within
the last few days, that a foul imputation has
been removed from the administration
of justice in New South Wales. Another
frequent assertion is that the evils of trial
by public sentiment have been fully exposed.
Both statements are utterly false and foolish
and illogical. The foul imputation upon the
administration of justice is that Dean was
found guilty of poisoning when no poison
had been traced to his possession ; when no
one had seen him administer the poison ;
when the medical evidence, as recorded in
the Judge’s notes, was wholly impossible
and was bolstered up by a ridiculous mis-
statement on the part of the prosecution ;
and when the jury gave its verdict under
extreme pressure from the presiding Judge,
instead of being left to form its own un-
biassed conclusions. The further imputations
are that the police (as in regard to Mrs.
Seymour) suppress evidence calculated to tell
in favor of the accused ; and the law spends
immense sums and puts all its machinery
in motion to hunt out facts against the
prisoner and present them in the most con-
vincing shape to the jury, while it furnishes
no machinery at all to dig up facts telling in
his favor. Therefore, only a wealthy sus-
pect who can hire enormously expensive
legal and professional aid ever gets a fair
trial —except by accident. These foul im-
putations remain exactly as they were. The
law in the present instance merely occupies
the position of a man who fired his gun
wildly into a mob and hit somebody, who
was proved long afterwards to have deserved
all that happened to him. But it is also
on record that by the same process the law
has hit the wrong person on previous
occasions, and if it continues the same
promiscuous method it will certainly do
so again. As regards the other assertion,
trial by public sentiment is the only thing
connected with the Dean case that has not
been more or less discredited by that
disastrous affair. Thanks to it, the law has
been saved from the final disgrace of com-
mitting what, on the evidence that existed
last April, would have been nothing short of
State murder. The reputation of the detec-
tive force has been considerably damaged by
the wonderful number of things it failed to
discover at the right time. The Crown Law
Department has not improved its character
by the way it filled one gap, at all events,
in the evidence. The Bench has lost
caste woefully by its method of conducting the
case. The miserable weakness of the defence
at the trial has served as a reflection on the
whole system of Botany Bay alleged justice,
which hires the best legal talent in the
country to present one side of the case, and
leaves the other side to be supported by a
young solicitor working often from tnei’e
motives of charity, tempered by a consuming
desire to get into Parliament and listen to
the wagging of his awn jaw. But trial by
public sentiment has proved itself patient,
equitable, reasonably intelligent, and wholly
honest from first to last, and that is as much
as can be expected of any court in this
world.
Trial by Fad.
The other day a well-advertised N.S. W. Judge
was reported to have said that he “knew”
a certain witness was lying “ because he
shuffled his feet ! ” A trenchant and timely
commentary upon that preposterous criterion
of credibility now comes to hand in the fact
that assassin Dean’s demeanour, under all the
long-drawn dubiety of his chances, remained
consistently that of a man sustained by the
consciousness of innocence. To the very last,
Dean looked at the world with an unflinching
eye. When on his ferry steamer he saw a man
who had acted the friend to him, he went and
placed in that friend’s palm, in token of Grati-
tude, the big, horny, honest hand with which
he had put the arsenic into Mrs. Dean’s lemon-
syrup.
* • #
Mankind, as Le Sage observed, is prone to
imagine it sees what it has expected to see, and
the chances are that 99 persons out of 100 will,
in the case of a suspected murderer, translate
into unmistakable indications of criminality
the self-same physiognomical peculiarities which
would speak to them, in the case of a “just
man made perfect,” only the language of
benevolence and brotherly love. “ Once accuse
a man of a crime,” said Dalley at the trial of
Scott, the Sussex-street murderer, “and people
will be ready to believe him guilty. Place the
most honest and pleasing face behind these
rails, and it at once becomes a face that ex-
presses every passion.” This, unfortunately, is
as inevitable as it is that the “ fluke ” —for it
was no more—of the Judge in the Dean case*
will, to a miserable certainty, incline the Sheep
of the community to dangerous reliance upon
mere “beliefs” of a man who, though he is,
doubtless in strict accordance with the nature
of things, is, as an administrator of justice,
most palpably at variance with the fitness of
them.
# • •
A Judge, like other people, must have his
discriminatory capacity estimated not so much
by its exercise within accustomed technical
lines as by the cogency with which it is applied
to questions more or less outside those lines.
But a Judge—as people are too apt to forget—
is but a picturesquely-upholstered and not
always very distinguished law yer, and the con-
tinued practice of the legal profession, according
to more than one psychological authority, has a
distinctly deteriorating effect upon the “power,”
as Lewes puts it, “of estimating general prob-
ability, as distinguished from forensic.” The
lawyer, in a word —like the parson —keeps get-
ting into that ratiocinative rut out of which it
is the tendency of the remaining learned profes-
sion to keep constantly forcing its exercisers.
In which not-sufficiently-observed circumstance
lies the explanation of the facts that a man
who, within the judicial atmosphere, enjoys
reasonable logical heal Lb may become a hopeless
dialectic wreck the moment he gets outside it.
The distinguished legal minority of the Dean
Commission was unable—or said so—to accept
the theory in which his medical colleagues saw
nothing at all incompatible with a very ordinary
experience. “Medical jurisprudence,” wrote
Maudesley, “ would be a much more efficient
factor in the determination of verdicts if the
average lawyer didn’t labor under the delusion
that he is, ips6 factd, a better reasoner than the
average doctor.”
m * •
The lawyer, at all events, has, for so far,
failed to discover the futility of generalisations
from the outer man about the inner. Physiog-
nomy, demeanor —the externality, generally, of
an accused person —must, perforce, produce an
impression one way or other, but, to demon-
strate the dangerous absurdity of making that
impression a conviction, it is only necessary to
remember that a quarter-inch black chin-stubble
would make Bum Sikes of the Angel Gabriel,
or to weirdly conjure up the collocation, behind
the spikes, of three of N.S. Wales’ best-known
and most reputable lawyers—unwashed, un- 1
shaven and arraigned for the attempted murder
of, say, one W. P. Crick. What a wealth of
physiognomical acumen would come to light
about —distributively—the “ cruel, remorseless
mouth,” and the “ ferociously glaring eye,”
and the “ cunning little monkey-grin ! ” But
Lombroso —probably the most accomplished
anthropologist living—focusses the whole ques-
tion to admiration in a single sentence.
“ Physiognomical and physiological phenomena
unquestionably Speak a language which may
hereafter be found perfectly coherent and
intelligible, but there is at present some
danger that an imperfect acquaintance with
even its alphabet may ccurtribute to miscarriage
of justice by the punishment of the innocent.”
Which utterance of a man who has done much
to disentangle criminological issues The Bul-
letin heartily commends to the consideration
of a profession that has done a good deal more
to confuse them.


PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Vic. Coal Question.
The Victorian Government coal-contract situa-
tion is practically this : Two years ago the
Newcastle companies supplied the Victorian
railways with coal at 14s. sd. per ton. A year
ago, when the Gippsland supply began to com-
pete strongly with the imported article, the
price was reduced to 12s. Now it is offered at
11s. delivered in Melbourne—for one year
only, so presumably this price represents a
desperate effort to swamp the Victorian
mine - owners. Meanwhile, the Outtrim
Co., whose coal seems, so far, the only kind
produced in Victoria—except perhaps that of
the Jumbunna mine—which is fully equal to the
Newcastle article, offers to supply at 9s. fid. at
the mine. This, after paying haulage, will pro-
bably amount to 12s. 3d. per ton at Melbourne.
The difference is considered well spent, partly
because the local supply will furnish employ-
ment to some hundreds of families ; partly
because Govt, can enforce the minimum-wage
clause, which it cannot do in the Newcastle
mines; partly because the new arrangement
will prevent nearly £lOO,OOO leaving the pro-
vince annually ; and partly because it is owing
in the main to Victorian competition that the
Government has escaped from the heavy exac-
tions of the N.S.W. exporters, therefore it
is to its interest to encourage that competi-
tion. Further, the £lOO,OOO a year spent
in Victoria will be liable to income-tax,
while the same amount spent in New
South Wales yielded nothing at all. The
springing up of new •coalmining towns
will create property that will yield taxation
revenue to the shire treasuries. It will increase
the value of land in the vicinity, and thereby
further increase the revenue. It will yield
a small income in stamps on the receipts for
dividends and salaries, and on letters in con-
nection with the business. As coal mining is a
thirsty business the Government, by encourag-
ing it, will help along the drink revenue. When
the shareholder dies, after accumulating a pile
out of his dividends, the Government will get
at him for probate and legacy duty, whereas it
can’t get at the Newcastle shareholder who is
out of its jurisdiction. It will also take small
bits out of that annual £lOO,OOO in a dozen
other ways by direct and indirect taxation,
from the tobacco and dog taxes down to the
licence-fee for the inevitable new pub. where
the new coal-miner quenches his thirst. The
establishment of a big local industry is a thing
of many aspects, and the odd Is. 3d. or a little
less per ton is about the smallest of them.
Civil Service Reform.
The N.S.W. Civil Service Reform Bill, intro-
duced by Premier Reid, is a measure which
may do a lot of good, even though it does not
produce quite all that is expected of it. The
main feature of the scheme is that it establishes
the principle of appointment and promotion by
competitive examination instead of appointment
and promotion by having an uncle in Parlia-
ment, as hitherto. It also classifies the Service
into different grades, with a maximum salary
in each grade, so that unless the civil-servant
can climb into another grade by passing the
requisite examination, influence will, or ought
to be, powerless to help him along beyond a
certain limit. The Service is to be under the
control of three commissioners, and if the three
are strong enough to resist political pressure,
there should be very little of the old corruption
left. Probably a new variety of corruption will
spring up by-and-bye instead of the old sort;
but that must be a matter for future considera-
tion. For the time, at all events, there should
be rather less glaring corruption than hitherto,
and the habit of appointing Maloney should be
hardly so prevalent as it has been. If the new
system is honestly conducted, it should remove
the greatest difficulty in the way of retrench-
ment—the necessity of not sacking any friend,
or relative, or influential constituent, of any out
of the 125 M’s.P. under pain of that mem-
ber’s lasting animosity, as the Commis-
sioners will be, to a great extent, beyond
the reach of any member’s animosity. If
it is conducted with only a moderate amount
of dishonesty, it will mean that the province
will have to find billets for the friends (rela-
tives are barred) of the three Commissioners,
instead of for those of 125 politicians, so that
there will be a distinct gain. And if it is con-
ducted very dishonestly indeed, it will probably
mean that the member for Smith’s Curse will
live on the Commissioners’ door-mat instead of
on that of the Ministry, and that the young
man with influence will generally get through
his examination, when the equally unintelligent
young man without influence won’t, and that
the former will rise to the maximum
salary in his grade a good deal faster
than the other fellow. Meanwhile, nothing in
particular is done with the great problem —that
of the bankrupt Superannuation Fund. All
new appointees, instead of contributing to the
fund, are to insure their lives, but though this
is to be compulsory the Government doesn’t
propose to start a State Life Insurance Depart-
ment, so as to guarantee that the civil
servant whom it has compelled to pay
premiums all his life won’t be left in the
lurch by a “ busted ” office when he dies.
Also, sundry new regulations have been made
concerning the Fund, but nothing is done to
alleviate the awful fact that the said Fund is
burst-up aud hopelessly insolvent, and wants
£2,000,000 or £3,000,000 to put it straight
again. Premier Reid, however, promises that
he will look into this difficulty in a month or
two, or R«xt year, or in 1906, or some time.
The Four Sexes.
The lamentable balderdash talked by the
W.C.T.U. about the moral inferiority of the
“ other sex ” is based mostly upon an obstinate
blindness to the utter futility of quarrelling
with Man because he isn’t Woman ! Which is
precisely why he never could and never can
conform to the old-hen standard of conduct set
up for him by every cheap female apostle who
has cackled herself into confounding a mere
vulgar itch for meddling with a Heaven-
sent mission for the moral regeneration of
the universe. Such a silly sisterhood of
faddists as the W.O.T.U. is only possible
as a morbid product of that civilisation, the
history of which is mainly the history of the
man—not of the sub-feminine anomaly M'ho
is as sexually uncharacteristic as the sub-
masculine woman who makes an ideal of
him—but of the red-blooded, robust fellow
whose vital energy brings him, at every turn,
into conflict with some small conventional
morality or other. It is the male man,
in short—with all the male man’s inci-
dental “ wickedness ” who has, up to date,
run the world and the woman and the
woman-man, and who will probably be found
in charge of that little contract long after
the man-woman has discovered that the only
he who has ever taken her seriously is the he
who ought properly to have been a she, and
who, by acknowledging woman as his equal,
has become, ipso facto, her inferior. But it is
the type—not the aberration—that “goes,”
and it is but the atypical woman who—because
she is atypical—turns herself into a miserable
travesty of man, and succeeds to admiration in
confuting her own contention that Nature has
foregone in her favor the otherwise universal
law of the subordination of the female. And,
not of her, but of the womanly woman who
beholds her antics with astonishment, are, for
the most part, born the men whose vital
activity and intellectual vigor are the dominant
factors in the progress of the race.
The Damp Judge.
The following telegram, printed last Saturday
by Sydney Daily Telegraph, will, in the
opinion of level-headed people, constitute one
of the deadliest stabs inflicted on the dignity
of N.S.W. Supreme Court since the memorable
evening when a puisne Judge solemnly patron-
ised a bogus spiritualistic stance and was more
or less taken in by the chocolate-eating spooks
of “ Cissie ” and “Josephine ” :
MR. JUSTICE WINDEYER IN ADELAIDE.
EULOGISED BY A CLERGYMAN.
THE JUDGE MOVED TO TEARS.
SPEECH BY THE REV. JOHN WATSFORD.
A REMARKABLE AND PATHETIC SCENE.
Adelaidb, Friday.—There was a deeply interesting and
pathetic scene at Government House, Adelaide, to-day,
when his Excellency the Lieut.-Governor entertained at
lunch a small party of clergymen, who had met to
take formal leave of a brother cleric before his de-
parture for Melbourne. Among the guests was
Mr. Justice Windbybr, of New South Wales, who
is passing through Adelaide on his way to Broken
Hill. Sir William Windbybr found himself in sym-
pathetic company, as it was evident that the clergymen
present had watched the tragic course of George Dean’s
trial with keen interest, and that their sympathies were
witli the much-abused Judge.
It was hardly possible that the gentlemen round him
could avoid referring to the events that are the common
talk of Australia. The Rev. W. H. Fitciiett was the first
to give expression to the prevailing felling. He referred
to the presence of Sir William, and congratulated him
upon the complete change in the public feeling towards
him, which had now taken place. He who had been so
abused and vilified was now shown to he in the right, and
the man whom he had sentenced had proved himself after
all an even greater villain than was first supposed.
Eye-witnesses aver that Mr. Justice Windbybr was
moved to tears at these kindly references. When the
time came for him to respond, he completely broke down.
For weeks past, he said, his back had been against the
wall ; the current of public feeling had been adverse to
him; and he had b°en represented as unjust and cruel.
But through it all he had been sustained by a conscious-
ness of his own integrity'. Now that the reaction had set
in, the kind expressions which poured in upon him were
more than he could well bear.
The Judge’s utterance was choked with sobs, and there
was not one present hut was deeply moved. “ The stern
and cruel Judge” of popular estimation was after all
shown to be a man of deep and tender feeling, and as
grateful for the approval of his fellows as others. The
pathos of the scene was increased when the patriarchal
Rev. John Watsford, who was born in Parramatta nearly
80 years ago, referred to Mr. Justice Windeybr’s father,
whom he had known in other days as a man of sterling
integrity and commanding power, who could control
audiences as few others could. Other things were said,
which those who were present decline to repeat, as being
too sacred for the public eye ; but the whole scene is des-
cribed as one which could never b° forgotten.
Before the party broke up the Rev. John Watsford had
all present down on their knees, and gave God thanks for
the past, and invoked the Divine blessings on all, and
especially on the one whose recent experiences had been
so hard lo bear.
It is indeed “pathetic” to find a Supreme
Court Judge reported as having wept with
emotion at a parsons’ lunch-party. His Honor’s
dampness is described as proving him to be “a
man of deep and tender feeling.” Judge Win-
deyeb may be a man of deep and tender feel-
ing, but his recent alleged sloppy aspect doesn’t
prove it by any means. Tears are not neces-
sarily an evidence of “ feeling; ” they often
arise through gratified vanity, male hysteria,
and other causes.
Even before “’Possum” Dean had come
tumbling down his tree of innocence, Melb.
Age (“narked,” no doubt, at the lead assumed
by the hated Argus in denouncing the iniquity
of the trial) joined in the reactionary hymn of
praise to the wigged oracle. “The judicial
acumen and impartiality of Judge Windeyer
is re-established. . . . The Sydney public
will have time to reflect how completely it has
been taken in, and the result will probably be
that Mr. Justice Windeyer will take Dean’s
place in its regard,” sang the Age. Can that
time-serving newspaper show cause why proof
of Dean’s guilt, long after his trial, should be
held to justify Judge Windeyer’s assertion of
Dean’s guilt upon unsatisfactory evidence ?
The black facts of the case, as now revealed,
make very indifferent whitewash for the facts
of the trial. George Dean’s innocence seemed
quite probable at a time when Judge Windeyer
was as sure he was guilty as if he had actually
seen him poisoning his wife. The Sydney
public protested against the Judge’s cocksure-
ness. If the public assumed the certainty of
Dean’s innocence on the absolute certainty that
he didn’t get a fair trial, then Dean’s place in
that soft-headed public’s regard is a suitable
position for Judge Windeyer to occupy. Let
the people who dedicated ballads to Dean
cudgel their brains anew on Judge Windeyer’s
behalf.
Melbourne Punch quotes some examples of
-the salaries paid to Anglican parsons in the
Victorian country districts. One reverend and
hungry wretch gets £B9 a year without a house,
has a large district which necessitates keeping
two horses or cruelly overworking one, anil
three congregations to preach to. Another has
£lO7 a year, no house, four congregations, and
one horse to keep. A third starveling has ten
congregations to attend to and £ll3 a year.
Other salaries are £BO (three congregations) ;
£B9 (four congregations, no residence, one
horse to keep); £ll4 (24 alleged congregations,
no residence, three horses to keep), and so on.
There appear to be a very large number of cases
like these, which are merely a few typical in-
stances. The Victorian Christians let these ini-
quities go on, and take very little apparent in-
terest in the feeble efforts now and then made
to furnish the parson with a living wage, and
yet put up £5OOO a year amid great enthusiasm
for Chinese missions. Consequently, The Bul-
letin is more and more convinced that the
Chinese mission enthusiasm arises solely from
the fact that the Chow is remote and mysterious
and more or less picturesque and the owner of a
pig-tail; and that if he wasn’t all these things
he might die and go to Gehenna without any-
one caring much about him. In other words,
the Chinese mission enthusiasm, and the Soudan
contingent, and the small boy’s desire to be a
Deadwood Dick and scalp the raging Indian,
and the Dean excitement, and the Barker-
Placke prize-fight are all manifestations of the
public craving for something new and extra-
ordinary and containing more or less possi-
bilities of bloodshed—other people’s blood being
shed for preference.
The following letter, printed in Sydney Stock
and Station Journal of Bth Oct., 1895, pur-
ports to bear the signature of Mr. J. C. Ryrib,
of Wemabah (N.S. W.), one of the biggest
squatters in the province :
BREEDING HUMAN BEINGS.
(TO TUB EDITOR “ S.S. AND S. JOURNAL.”
Sir,—l am much pleased with the originality and truth
of your flying memos, in your issue of 24th inst. re breed-
ing human beings. You say truly, “In Australia we are
breeding the best of sheep in the world. Under our
lovely skies we are raising cattle and horses that beat all
competitors, but along with them we are raising some of
the meanest human beings that ever cursed the earth."
Quite true. Then, again: “Some people appear to be
under the impression that the reason we have so many
larrikins is owing to the lack of parental control,” &c.
Nothing of the kind ; it is owing to this cursed Edu-
cation Act, which makes the boys who have gained
a smalterivg ashamed of their parents when they
see the mother go to the wash-tub and the father
tuck up his sleeves to go to work like an honest,
hard-working man. Say they, We are fitted for something
better, and won’t work ; hence springs the agitator, street-
corner man, lounger, and larrikin. This so-called educa-
tion makes the boy discontented with his position in life.
We never had any larrikins before this Education Act
came into vogue ; that is the cause of larrikins. Give me
the working man who can neither read nor write, for then
he will give his whole attention to his work, instead of
devoting his time to noiels and agitation, etc. There are
superiors, inferiors, and equals all over the world, and very
properly so, and when pitchforked out of their positions
hence eruptions and troubles. People wonder at the
depression of the times and its cause, for it applies to
the whole world. It all lies in a nutshell, too many pro-
ducers and competitors, too many people in the world,
swarming like rabbits, and trying to shove each other off
the face of the earth to gain a livelihood. Some 25 years
ago everyone in the country was contented, thriving, and
doing well. Now, no one is doing wed, a hand-to-mouth
arrangement, with ruination slaring him in the face, and
thousands of men walking about the country starving
and not able to find work.—Yours, etc., J. C. Ryrib.
Wemabah, Dubbo, or Trangie. September 26,1895.
The conflict of opinion between Dr. Jamieson
and Dr. O’Connor on the question of lunacy
raised in the trial of Wilson before Acting-
Judge Backhouse at Sydney, the other day,
illustrates once more the incompleteness of ex-
pert evidence. From experts in handwriting
to experts in brain-disease, and from these
again to experts in criminal jurisprudence, the
knowledge and experience of one will, if a
little search is made, be contradicted by the
knowledge and experience of another. Look
at Pilcher and Salomons in the George Dean
case. It does not follow from this that expert
opinion is worthless, or that it should not be
submitted to a jury. But it does follow that
the evidence of a single expert, or two or three
experts, readied up by one side, should not be
allowed to possess the weight the ordinary jury
attaches to it. Chemical theories, for instance,
if dependent on the evidence of one analyst
only, should be viewed with the gravest sus-
picion.
The N.S.W. Justice Department’s recent ukase
that a sick prisoner shall, when he needs alco-
hol, be compelled to take it “ with a drug ”
(i.e., in an unpalatable or even nauseous form),
is revolting to the average doctor, the creed of
the profession being that in sickness all men
are to be treated in the same spirit. The order,
when you come to look at it, is really an order
for the institution of torture, and is in its way
as repulsive to every white man as the depart-
ment’s “ humane gag.” Would such a system
be tolerated for an instant by the honorary
medical staff of a public hospital ? The argu-
ment as to the new regulation being necessary
to stop malingering carries with it its own
refutation ; malingerers form but a small per-
centage of cases treated, and why should the
innocent-in-this-respect be made to suffer with
the guilty? But that it is the creed of the
N.S.W. Justice Department to give no man
the benefit of the doubt needed no additional
demonstration. Malingerers occasionally ap-
pear in every hospital, and it is surely as easy
to detect them in the gaol hospitals as in the
great city institutions.
■
In 1865, says a Fortnightly Review article,
an Italian named Pelizzioni was tried, in
London, before Baron Martin, for the murder
of a fellow-countryman in an affray at Saffron
Hill. He was found guilty and sentenced to
death. In passing sentence the Judge made
the following remarks, which should always be
remembered when the acumen begotten of a
sound legal training and long experience is
relied on as a safeguard against error :
In my judgment it was very impossible for the jury to
have come to any other conclusion. The evidence was
about the clearest and the most direct that after a
long course of experience in the administration of
criminal justice 1 have ever known. / am as satisfied as
I can be of anything that Gregorio did not injlict the
wound, and that you were the person who did.
The trial was over. The Home Secretary would
most certainly, after the Judge’s expression of
opinion, never have interfered. The date of
the execution was fixed. Yet the unhappy
prisoner was guiltless of the crime, and it was
only through the exertions of a private indivi-
dual that an innocent man was saved from
Uie gallows. A fellow-countryman of his suc-
ceeded in persuading the real culprit (the
Gregorio so expressly exculpated by the
Judge) to come forward and acknowledge the
crime. He was subsequently tried for man-
slaughter and convicted, while Pellxzioni
received a free pardon.
The abolition of State aid to denominational
schools is to cost the Westralian taxpayer
■£15,000 in cash. The amount originally recom-
mended (by a Parliamentary committee well-
picked by the Govt.) was £30,000; the Govt,
managed to carry £20,000 by 16 votes to 15, and
then decided to compromise with the Opposi-
tion at £5OOO less. The province has got off as
well as could be expected under the circum-
stances, considering that the whole agitation
against sectarian grants has sprung up within
the last five years, since the arrival of
the hated and enlightened “ t’othersiders. ”
Five years ago nobody dared to talk “ aboli-
tion of State aid to religion ” to the Forrest
crowd ; and the Forrest crowd never dreamt
of talking “compromise” to the complaisant
Opposition. But the world moves—and so
does Westralia and the Westralian Parlia-
ment, thanks to Leake, Illingworth,:
Randall, and similar foes of bigotry. None
the less, the grant of £15,000 is a public scandal.
The priests’ and parsons’ schools, subsidised by
the State that they might attract pupils from
the State schools and sow in impressionable
young minds the seeds of dogmatic error and
religious feud, were not entitled to one penny of
compensation. Rather were they entitled to
congratulate themselves on getting off without
restitution. Westralia stultifies itself by taking
away the endowment with one hand and with
the other donating a sum of money the interest
on which will pretty nearly equal the endow-
ment. The fact that the sectarian schools have
accumulated valuable property under the old
system was impudently put forward as a reason
for compensation—and Westralia has followed
the example of the man who, when his horse
was stolen, advertised that he would be glad to
present the thief with the saddle and bridle as
well. The only consolation is that it is Wes-
tralia, and things might have been worse.
The new Sydney markets, to cost £204,000,
are the biggest single contract of the sort ever let
by aN.S.W, municipality. The work has now bten
going on for 11 months, and will last two y. ars
longer. The exterior lineal measurement ot the
whole building is £ mile 30yds.; and the summit
will be 192. t. above the street.

room in her hospital uniform, whereon up steps

that she cannot be admitted as a guest, as her
uniform was objected to.
Blow, Gentle Meagher .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh, wouldn’t you like the truth to hear of how it
came about—
How Meagher began to blow-o-oh, and Jul-i-an to
shout;
How Kater asked a question clear, how Want
became obscure;
How Patsy Crick unoricked and cricked—it
puzzles you, I’m sure.
But this little thing I would have you know :
The beginning and end and the whole was blow—
Australian blow,
Our own blow,
Not the Brummagem foreign kind—Oh, no !
But good old, genuine, ’possum-and-platypus,
bright Australian blow.
Because, you see, when Dean was clear and
pardoned by the Crown
It seemed a thing and a silly thing to give up the
renown;
To give up the renown, my boys, that lies in cun-
ning law
To prove that black is surely white, and a crack is
never a flaw.
But this little thing I would have you know :
The beginning and end and the whole was blow—
Australian blow,
Our own blow,
Not the spurious foreign thing—by Joe !
But right-down, regular, wattle-and-gum-tree, gay
Australian blow.
For Salomons looms in attorney-land, and thriving
lawyers scan
The eyes and nose (but especially nose) of this
successful man,
And blink and wink as they try to win the much
desired prize
Of looking smart—“ unqualified ” smart-in those
most august eyes.
But this little thing I would have you know
The beginning and end and the whole was blow—
Australian blow,
Our own blow,
Not the Brummagem foreign kind—Oh, no !
But gorgeous, glorious, snake-cit/a-wallaby, grand
Australian blow.
He only meant, of course, to state in strictest
secresy,
If there were need of a clever man—why, a clever
man was he!
It was but inditing a business-card whereon the
vendors state
That they supply superior law at a very moderate
rate.
But this little thing I would have you know :
The beginning and end and the whole was blow —
Australian blow,
Our own blow,
Not the spurious foreign thing—by Joe !
But bang-up, slap-up, whaler-and-sundowner, big
Australian blow.
And now, alas ! our Wallaby Dean confesses all
is true;
Behind the walls of Darlinghurst his conduct he
doth rue ;
The Musman boat is desolate, while Windever
and Go.,
They wink their eyes and slap their thighs—Ah,
didn’t we tell you so? ”
But this little thing I would have you know :
The beginning and end and the whole was blow—
Australian blow,
Our own blow,
Not the Brummagem foreign kind—Oh, no 1
But tally three-hundred, hat-on-the-gold-knobs,
true Australian blow.

A fresh sea-breeze from the back-cloth and
an imaginary smell of salt-horse, born of the
realism of the scenery, attended the revival of
old “ Pinafore,” at the Lyceum, on Saturday.
It is a good many years since this opera was
last presented in Sydney, though sundry
amateur co.’s have committed it at intervals in
the suburbs, with more or less success. The
general impression on its re appearance is that
it is about the brightest and freshest of modern
operas ; the atmosphere from the painted ocean
in the background is a pleasant thing-to meet
with, and'the ancient man-of-war is a work of
art, and perfect down to the smallest detail.
Miss Juliet Wray takes the character of Jose-
phine for the first three nights, Miss Florence
Young filling the blue skirt of that innocent
child of the deep for the second half of the
season. Miss W.’s Josephine was well received,
though the performance, vocally and otherwise,
is hardly equal to her great success as Clairette.
Her acting, which was somewhat nervous, not
to say uneven and bumpy, in the first act on
Saturday, steadied wonderfully as the piece
progressed. Lauri doesn’t put much fun into
the character of Joseph Porter, but he is
tolerably effective, and the rest of the cast is
about faultless. Mrs. BVacy is one of the best
Buttercups The Bulletin remembers. Wal-
lace Brownlow and Tapley are both at their
brightest, and Edward Farley’s Dick Deadeye
is a marvel of angularity and many other good
qualities. Likewise, the chorus is particularly
excellent, and that fascinating little girl, Ivy
Scott, the smallest midshipmite yet seen, was
received in a cordial spirit, and encored till she
was nearly dead from exhaustion. The present
“ Pinafore ” is a beautifully-finished perform-
ance, and the blue sea and the fleet at anchor
in the distance are calculated to make Aus-
tralians feel reconciled, in a glow of nautical
fervor, even to the naval subsidy and the un-
picturesque spectacle of Garden Island, and the
occasional running of the Ringarooma on the
rocks.
** * »
Miss Annis Montague has a farewell
matinee benefit to-day (Thursday), at Sydney
Lyceum. The show is under tremendously in-
fluential patronage, and a good programme will
be presented, with the assistance of almost
everybody who is anybody worth mentioning in
theatrical circles.
** * *
Pro. Kennedy’s latest idea is to put a man
in a mesmeric sleep, keep him in that con-
dition for six days, and waken him at 10 sharp
on Saturday night (the 19th) in the presence of
an enthusiastic audience. The patient will
then sit up suddenly in a dazed frame of mind,
and realise that a whole week has dropped out
of his existence. All through this week the
animate mass of dead meat will be visible to
the press and the medical profession. Relays
of watchers appointed by the Sydney doctors
and others will watch him ceaselessly to keep
him from being eaten by rats, to scare the flies
off him, and to prevent dogs or stray pigs
taking bites out of him. The Bulletin’s pri-
vate opinion is that the police, if they can,
ought to shut down on the wretched spectacle
and show the Professor out of the town, also
that Parliament should enact—as has already
been done in some European countries—that
mesmerism is illegal save for medical purposes
only. It further draws attention to the fact
that if this kind of experiment happens too
often, it will end, sooner err later, in the
watchers going out for a surreptitious drink,
and the rats eating a considerable amount of
the trance patient in their absence.
** * *
The “ Prairie King ” is prospering at Her
Majesty’s. The literary merits of the piece are
at the same low ebb as reported last week, but
King Hedley’s stupendous dive into the tem-
pestuous tank in Act 11., and his horse’s mighty
fall from the broken bridge into the same tank
in Act 111., and the martial array of cowboys
who swim the tank in Act IY., are sufficient to
keep any drama going. The leading horse takes
to the work in a kindly spirit, and seems to fall
through the bridge every night in a perfectly
contented frame of mind. Apparently the
drama will go on and prosper till someone
starts a rival show with a bigger tank and
drops an elephant into it.
** * »
George Leitch’s “ Land of the Moa ” opens
at Sydney Royal on Saturday. The scenery is
announced to include the Pink Terraces of
Rotomahana, the Sulphur Island, the Black
Crater, the Devil’s Blowhole, the Wharepuni
of Rewi (whatever that may be), the Bush of
Tikitiri (whatever that may be), the eruption
of Tarawera, and many other beauties and
horrors. Those two excellent artists, the
Dacres, have joined the eo., likewise Sophia,
the well-known Maori lady who used to act as
guide at the hot lakes, and to wash the aston-
ished and undressed visitor in the mud-springs.
A rumour is already current that the scenery
will arouse her old familiar instincts, and that
she will make a strenuous effort to undress
and wash Leitch in the middle of the drama.
* * * *
Albert Banvard has not yet broken his
famous neck by his “ dive from the dome ” at
Sydney Tivoli, so that stupendous spectacle is
still on view. If he does break his neck, Tim
Bulletin presumes that the other six Banvards
will have to do their reckless performance on
the trapeze up by the roof in mourning tights.
Fred Garnett, a boneless individual, who seems
to be lineally descended from the crinky ser-
pent, has been added to the co. John Coleman,
the English funny man, is announced for
Saturday; and, wet or dry, in spite of de-
pression and drought and the Dean mystery,
the shekels still roll in with monotonous regu-
larity.
Last Monday, the variety co. at Sydney
Empire announced its performance was “ in
the presence of the officers and crew of the mis-
sionary ship John Williams.” This spiritual
recognition ought to be sufficient guarantee of
the merits of the show. The people who go
down to the sea in ships to rescue the poor
heathen are generally good judges of a perform-
ance with legs in it.

The Fitzgeralds’ sensational show, concern-
ing which The Bulletin has said many pleasant
things during its late Melb. season, opens at
Sydney Exhibition Building on Saturday.
* * * *
Mr. John Lawler advertises in this issue
for all kinds of first-class variety artistes for his
new music-hall at 608, 610 and 612 George-
street, which opens shortly. The hall is a
splendid structure, well-built, well-ventilated
and beautifully decorated, lighted by electri-
city, and practically fire-proof. It is capable of
accommodating with ease 1000 people, and
should be found one of the most elegant and
comfortable places of amusement in Sydney.
* m * stf
“The meek shall inherit the earth.” ; Not
yet a bit, for if there is a meek man and a
worthy it is W. J. Wilson, but his share of the
planet will not pay much land-tax. Men over
30 don’t need to be told that W. J. Wilson is
one of the oldest and most honoured figures in
the theatrical world. As scenic artist, mana-
ger and impresario his name has stood before
the public for 40 years without reproach. His
lines have not fallen in pleasant places for
several years back, so he is going to take a
benefit next Tuesday. All the stars scintillat-
ing in the Sydney theatrical firmament will re-
volve around him. And though many of the
public may be indifferent to the merits of Mr.
Wilson, or callous as to his position, yet when
it is known that a programme of exceptional
attractiveness and magnitude has been pre-
pared, and that the prices will be “ three, two,
and one,” there will probably be a bumper
house, which is the end aimed at.
* ' * * *
After six months’absence “The Gaiety
Girl ” came back to Melbourne Princess’s on
Saturday, and got a louder approval, especially
from the amphitheatre, than was ever given to
the handsome novelty in its early struggling
days. This increase of popularity for “The
Gaiety Girl ” performance seems fully propor-
tionate to its falling off in point of artistic
merit. Louis Bradfield now takes the place
of Monkhouse as Doctor Brierley, and this
necessitates a new Bobbie, neither change
being a cause for rejoicing. The departed
fat man was a comedian with a humour "of his
own. He lent considerable drollery to a rather
dull character. Bradfield, who has no natural
humour, but only a clever, mechanical vola-
tility, plays Doctor Brierley on Lonnenesque
lines, and the chief effect of his industrious
mumming is to make Monkhouse respected
by many people who didn’t think the
stout person funny until his successor
challenged comparisons. The same people will
regret that Elton wasn’t called upon to fill the
gap (and he would, The Bulletin fancies, have
fitted it neatly), if only for the sake of avoiding
any further alteration of the original cast.
Miss Decima Moore and the gorgeous Widow
Hobson both suffer from the new order of
things, but Fred Kaye is still more palpably
comic than before ; and Palolta puls a little
earnestness into her bewitching picture of the
demon French girl, and the show is the co.’s
best all-round display, anyhow. “ In Town ”
next Saturday. The Saturday after that—the
Deluge. They sail for Europe per the Oruba.
O, woe is us !
* * * *
“ Joseph of Canaan ” is doing well at
the Royal. The first six nights looked healthy ;
the seventh was like unto one of the fat oxen
that stalked out of the river ; so Mrs. Putiphar
may be expected to have a guilty secret for
Joseph’s ear of scorn until further notice.
Parson Walters’ drama gains dignity and polish
as it grows older. The offspring of Jacob and
the Egyptian son of Erin with a brogue, are
leaving more footprints on the desert sand, so
to speak ; also, Pharaoh is less unduly civil to
his subjects than of yore. The scene in Court,
where George Rignold passes judgment on the
royal nightmare, has such a strong hold of
the audience that the prevailing silence
would explode and blow the roof off if
Ata were to strike a match. Just here
it may be remarked that Pharaoh’s dream,
although ordained by Heaven, was produced
by natural causes. When the Chief Baker was
flung into prison, a second-rate baker took
charge of Pharaoh’s pastry, therefore the sod-
dened undercrust sat heavy on ruler’s chest
and indigestion set in. And the fruity wines
which the temporary Chief Butler placed on
the rich man’s table did make him bilious.
Miss Maud Williamson, Scot Inglis, Miss
Claudius, and J. W. Hazlitt are happily placed
in this grave spectacle. Likewise the High
Priest’s daughter, who runs wild among the
clergy of the period, and needs a mother to
look after her, is an interesting young person in
white.
«« « «
Fitzgerald Bros.’ uncommon show having
put in its appointed time at Melb. Exhibition,
hurried away to Bendigo on Monday, but may
return to the big metropolitan building sooner
or later, for another short season. Graceful
Madame Antonio, diver Peart, the contortionists
and Mahomet stand out as the astonishing
features of a select company, but the lady on
the rolling globe and a trio of male acrobats give
valuable support. Peart’s blood-curdling per-
formance fetched a warning letter from the
police department after the second night. His
platform was then fixed higher than his record
in London, where he had, moreover, a bigger
tank to aim at. Ultimately the management
reduced the Professor’s dive to a distance of
about 110 ft., and the authorities breathed more
freely.
** * *
The Fitzgeralds’ talking horse, Maho-
met, is just a wonder, or rather his trainer’s
imperceptible trick of telling the horse
what to do, and when to cease doing it, is
wonderful. If an attendant, way down among
the audience, holds up a slate in a particular
way which denotes the figure written on the
other side, the horse raps the correct number,
but not out of his own head, as your “ Poverty
Point ” paragraphist seems to think. Trainer
Probasco looks at the slate, having previously
explained to the audience the attendant’s
system of announcing each particular number,
and he (Probasco) appeals to Mahomet, who
gives a prompt answer. If a 0 has been
figured on the slate, Mahomet doesn’t rap at
all, but merely shakes his head. The oftener
one watches the performance with one eye on
the tutor and the other on his four-legged pupil,
the more extraordinary it seems. Probasco
talks to the horse as though it had a series of
answers to his sentences stored in its memory,
and the animal never hesitates about replying
with a nod or a shake of the head. For in-
stance, he asks “You like kissing the young
ladies?” (Mahomet nods.) “ You like kissing
the old maids?” (Mahomet shakes a No.)
“Is one kiss enough for the young ladies ? ”
(No, from the horse.) “You like more than
one ? ” (Mahomet nods.) “ How many kisses
do you think they ought to have ? ” (Mahomet
stamps his hoof again and again until told to
stop.) And when asked to show the audience
how he kisses the girls, Mahomet promptly
turns his neck and puts his muzzle to Pro-
basco’s mouth. An educated horse —educated
apparently to understand hints so cunningly
given to him that alert lookers-on can’t detect
any means by which he is prompted. Mahomet
doesn’t need to be stationed at a considerable
distance from his audience. Occasionally he
walks into a newspaper office, climbs upstairs
to the editor’s sanctum, and goes through the
business there.

** * *
The Victorian Artists, who, in spite of
their youthful mannerisms and bilious eccentri-
cities on canvas, deserve to flourish, are about
to hold a Carnival at Melbourne Exhibition in
aid of their banking-account. Long ago these
sanguine dreamers built an Art Gallery on
“ tick,” and the procee Is of the coming Carni-
val will go towards obliterating the debt so
rashly incurred. The promoters have sassiety
at their back and splendid prospects of success
before them. About GOO ladies, together with
an influential mob of male persons, have formed
committees all over the place for the purpose of
working up a boom, Melbourne is strewn over
with hand-painted show-cards, mainly the
clever work of certain “ cannibal ” caricatur-
ists, advertising the Carnival, and the noble
Brassey will perform the inaugural ceremony,
supported by lady B. and suite, probably on
bikes. Wednesday, Ocr,. 23rd, is the date fixed
for this unique event. It is to be a carnival of
infinite variety, extending over several days,
and the full programme measures half-a-tnile in
length, or a trifle less- Indeed, if the com-
mittee and the people wlo have promised to
take part in the entertainment had taken up a
unanimous collection for the good cause at the
outset, they might have wiped out the artists'
debt without holding a carnival.

Louis Bradfield is genuinely funny when a
newspaper reporter comes along to interview him,
and the delicate question of Arthur Roberts crops
up. It appears from a recent interview that
“ Mr. Bradfield was glad to be able to create
‘ Gentleman Joe ’ for himself here, as it had nob
been produced when he left London. As he.has
only seen Mr. Arthur Roberts play three times in
his life , he can hardly be said to have copied
his Captain Coddington of ‘ln Town.’” Cer-
tainly not. The resemblance between the two
performances was no doubt a mere accident-all
the same it was a lucky accident for Bradfield.
The great fault of Bradfield’s “Gentleman Joe”
is that the cabby seems a comparatively original
creation. People would have preferred another
lot of accidental reminders of Roberts. Yet
there’s no serious fault to be found with Braddy
“on his own.” He’s a very good “singing
waiter ” sort of comedian, although not a natural
humorist. Not one actor in a thousand is an
original humorist in any department, for a humor-
ist must be able to laugh at his own weaknesses,
and it is hard—O, it is hard—for the man who
laughs at himself to take seriously to an actor’s
life. Why is acting the one profession, or art, in
which women are at least equal, if not superior, to
men? Because the ladies are lacking in sense of
humor. But this is moralising.
** * *
The attitude assumed by professional
musicians towards newspaper critics is, after all, a
good deal more absurd than the airs which critics
give themselves. Little Mark Hambourg, for
instance, says : “ I never look at what they write.
Who are they ? ” If it comes to that, who are the
people responsible for Mark’s treble recalls and
“unparalleled ovations?” The general public,
devoid of musical feeling and artistic sense, for
the most part. They cheer Mark for his excep-
tional ability to strike a given number of notes
correctly within a given time, but they would be
more impressed by an automaton smiting the
same keys at a higher rate of speed. Firework
pianoforte-playing appeals to the order of intellect
that prefers an acrobatic show to a grand opera.
*# * *
Mind-reading ? Ah ! Pin-finding 1 Pshaw 1
That represents the logical attitude of one of the
latest Sydney showmanisms. The conditions of
pin-finding have never been above suspicion, i.e.,
if the performer intends people seriously to believe
that he enacts his feats by mind-reading. Muscle-
jerking is another so-called scientific wonder. So
is “shuffling the feet in the witness-box,” accord-
ing to a learned Judge. None of the feats per-
formed were any better, and most of them were
not nearly as good as those performed by the
Steens (Prof, and Mrne.), who both owned up that
they had a secret oracular code that merely de-
manded from them constant practice and uninter-
rupted alertness. If a pin-finder can find a pin by
mind-reading, then he can disclose the pin-hider’s
thoughts on anything. Let that test be made,
but let it be made under conditions that will abso-
lutely be above all suspicion of collusion between
the thinker and the reader.

Melbourne playgoers would like to see a slimmer Joseph
in Canaan. George Kignold would make an excellent
king—fat, Pharaoh, and fifty. Seeing Joseph as he is, it
is hard to believe in those seven years of famine.
When “Joseph of Canaan ” was in rehearsal, Rignold
is said to have had a terrible time keeping Sweeney from
acting his part in Irish. This was a mistake. A Hebrew
with a brogue would have supplied the comic relief sadly
wanting in the piece.
It was Henry Arthur Jones who once said that he
wouldn’t want to go further than the Bible to find plots
for new plays. There’s the story of Daniel: the yarn
about Elijah (or was it Elisha?), and the leper Naaman,
and the covetous servant who “ tapped ” Naaman for
handsome presents and was doomed to take over the
warrior’s discarded leprosy along with 'em. And Jonah
and the whale. Ye gods ! what sort of a marine spectacle
would Bland Holt make out of Jonah and the whale !
David would he a good line ; and Samson ! Think of
George Rignold in the live-act melodrama “S mishit,”
concluding wilh the polling down of the Philistine temple
and the death of everybody !

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION.
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback- the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and diligent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who makes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.

Green, the clerk who was so much hunted
for in re the Dean alleged conspiracy-case,
gave his address to the police last Tuesday.
During the 18 days he has been wanted
he had been living within a few yards of
the Sydney detective-office. If he had remained
there he might not have been found this year.
The man who lives near enough to the detectives
is hardly ever found.
With the arrival of Lord Brassey and
suite there is to be an unprecedented bicycle boom.
The question of a becoming rational costume has
hitherto been a stumbling-block to the popularity
Of bicycling among the fair sex, but apparently
demand has now created a supply and the diffi-
culty is solved. Messrs. Hicks, Atkinson & Sons,
of Oollins-street, Melb., exhibit the latest styles
of costumes, such as are worn in the Bois de
Boulogne in Paris, where every pretty woman is
a wheelist in the present year of grace.*

An Insurance Swindle,
A rather good story appears in the Havannah
Trade Journal, devoted to the interests of
tobacco. It appears that an ingenious, but econo-
mical smoker, with a weakness for good cigars,
purchased a thousand of the famous B.L. Corto
Rothschilds and forthwith insured them. Then
he set to work to smoke them, and having con-
sumed them all, demanded £25 from the insurance
company, on the ground that the whole of the
insured stock had been destroyed by fire. The
court decided in his favor, whereupon the com-
pany brought an action for conspiracy, accusing
him of having intentionally set fire to the cigars,
and obtained his committal to prison for three
months. THe story may be true or it may not.
At any rate, the B.L. Corto Rothschild cigars are
the best value ever imported into Australia. They
are a delicious smoke, and sold by all first-class
tobacconists at 6d. each.*
