Dear BCLLRTIN, —Archaeological criticisms on “Joseph”
at Melbourne Royal may savour, ns the Age has it, of
pedantic ism.” But correct Hebrew pronunciation in a
Semitic play 1* easily obtainable in Jewish Melbourne.
“Canaan ” is voiced by the Chosen ns spelt “Can-a-an.”
The Egyptian name “Ata” has the accent on the last
syllable, and not on the first, as the Rignold co. declaims
it. There ore other similar mistakes.—Yours, Philistine.
Dear Bulletin, —Now that the subject of Juliet Flegel-
(auh is getting worn out—down to the bone as it were—
how would a story about old man Flegeltaub go? It was
when Juliet was at an age when she little thought of the
free ads. the yet unborn Bulletin would dish up for her,
that I had a benefit in the frosty city : to make it boom
(the benefit) I advertised gifts, and amongst the articles I
bought a gorgeous clock from Mr. Flegeltaub. It had a
marble stand, a crystal cover, and seemed to be built of
solid gold angels and cupids. Well, the show was brought
off successfully, and, singularly enough, the clock was
won by the printer, to whom a bill was owing, hut that s
another story. When he got the clock he wanted a key
to wind it up, and as none of the keys in Ballarat seemed
to be nude for that build of machine, lie came and
bothered me for one. I, in turn, went to Flegel, who
said he would soon supply the deficiency. A day or two
passed, but no key could be found. At last Flegeltaub’s
patience wore out, and with a comical smile he asked
me “Vat de deffel do you vant mitderkey? Doyou really
oxpect so glorious-looking a concern to go?”—P. ABBOTT.

The Rev. Walters, in his letters to the Melb. papers, re
the authorship of “Joseph of Canaan,” piously resenis
the insinuation of Ansuin George that his dialogue, as it
Stood iu the original* was calculated to harrow up the
sensibilities of those good church-goers whom George was
most anxious to inveigle into the Royal, and asserls that,
on the contrary, the management made the temptation-
scene much more templing than it is in ins version. The
reverend dramatist thinks Joseph is over-tempted in
Kignold s representation of the scene, and the cast-iron
propriety of Joseph, under the circumstances, certainly
does lend to the situation an air of improbability.
Danvers—poor old Danvers—died the other week in
some dull corner of Hmgland, paralysed and forgotten.
He was a burlesque actor, and a droll fellow in the long
ago, when such toney ladies as Mrs. Bancroft and Mrs.
John Wood were glad to dance “break-downs” in his
company. There may still he found in ancient theatrical
albums a few photos, of little Marie Wilton throwing her
legs at Danvers, and Mrs. Bancrott.jit is said, never misses
a chance of buying up those souvenirs of her frisky past.

MA MIE ROSETTE.
Ma Mie Rosette, one cannot blame
The jovial Henri’s amorous flame,
For, though you spoke most virtuously,
You looked— Weil, somewhat differently 1
Ma Mie Rosette 1
I’m sure you thought it quite a shame
You could not have your little game,
But some great fool must come to see,
And from her royal lover claim
Ma Mie Rosette!
So vanished all your chance of fame !
You might have been a noble dame—
Marquise or Duohesse of degree—
Then things which now may naughty be
Would have another nicer name—
Ma Mie Rosette.

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney,
October 15, 1895,
My Dear Moorabinda—
It was not at all disappointing to mo to find the
French ball, in aid of the Bienfaisanco Francaise
(at Sydney Town Hall last Monday evening), just
the facsimile of the balls that we’ve been having
all along, because French and English mixtures
never do effervesce. Asa usual sort of charity
dance, then, it may be said to have “got home ”
—good gallery and floor attendance, up-to-date
music, nicely-trimmed basement supper, and the
usual gloomy cavern for the patronisers, which is
called the dais. Ball committees seem to have a
solemn rule not to throw any more light on
their bell-wethers than can be helped, and it is
probably the murky atmosphere into which
these centre ornaments are plunged which
makes them dress so weirdly sometimes. The
best dress on the dais was worn by pretty
Madame Blanc: it was the shade of Parma
violets in satin brocade mingled with ivory satin,
and as the wearer fluttered .about the dark dais it
looked like a Francisca blossom blowing about a
coal-hole.
The Acting-Governess, in black satin brocade
with gaps of oyster-white brocade (by the way,
the A. V.R. habit of holding up the tail of her
gown when she promenades, though strictly
economical, is scarcely complimentary to the
broom-and-duster committee), was hardly distin-
guishable in the Cimmerian gloom of the dais ;
and the Major-Generaless also in black, with a
spray of natural roses, was merely an outline in
the darkness. The president (Lady limes) was
more visible in yellow brocade with violets and
violet satin ribbons. Miss Granville sported a
quaint garb of cream and mauve and striped silk.
The two youngest A.V.R. daughters were rather
limp in floral frocks.
Madame Tauveron (one of the hon. secs.) wore
a fetching confection of mauve and green silk
crepe, which made a Frenchy ensemble—but there
was plenty of sternly English Locking to dilute
the artistic effect of the one or two light and airy
confections on top. On the whole the toggery
was not smart —it’s too late in the season for
ball-frocks. Mark Harnbourg, whose Town-hall
concerts are a glittering success, turned up in a
basin-crop. Like the swan who should never be
seen walking, so Mark Harnbourg should never
be far off a piano-stool, for it is borne in upon
one that this brilliant and lionised youth could
not attempt to catch a pig in a passage, as the
saying goes—also that his hips are as wide as a
woman’s. Another celebrity, standing in conver-
sation with a real live poet, was Grace Rebellious
Susan Noble, who looked very captivating in blue
brocade and violets.
Talking of actresses reminds me that the
nuptials of Mr. Alf Marks (of Darling Point) and
Miss Claire Leighton, of theGee-Gees, werequietly
performed in Melbourne last week, with Mr.
Stuart Wallace as best man. The newly-married
pair didn’t rush away to hide their happiness,
but went to the races after the ceremony, the
bride appearing in “The Shop Girl ” at night.
Miss Leighton is the first Gee-Gee (so far as I
remember) to make a more or less society match
in Australia, for Letty Lind and her silver king
came to cross purposes and matrimony didn’t
eventuate.
The concert given last week at Sydney Cen-
tenary Hall by M. Napoleon Boffard—a recently-
unpacked tenor from Paris, with a somewhat
soprano quality about his top-register—was a
success of three —the tenor himself, Miss Edith
King, and Edgar Strauss. The rest was chaos;
for Kowalski, billed to appear, did not show.
Financially the concert was “Jack Frost.” Syd-
ney has become so inured to charity concerts
where the artists give their services, that the
common sort of concert, to which you go for
music’s sake alone and not to buy John Smith a
cork leg, never gets a show. And strange
vocalists, who willingly lend their services to
charity concerts, at first with an eye to biz (as they
vainly imagine), quickly make the starvation dis-
covery that Sydney musicians are people who go
to great expense beforehand to perfect themselves
in their art so that, apparently, the services they
are obliged to give away for nothing will be the
best to be obtained locally. One of the best ways
for a musioian to attract a paying audience in
Australia is to die a horrible and well-advertised
death, leaving a fascinating widow not over 84,
and a large and influential committee to work up
a ben. on her behalf.
The S.A.O.S. concert in Sydney Town Hall last
Thursday week was a well-rounded-off show, with
Sig. Hazon in the bird-cage threatening the per-
formers with his shillelagh, and a good spread of
audience in front. It was a great success for the
orchestra, though the vocalist of the evening—
Miss Flo. Eidaile—was naturally too comic
operatio in her style to sing Wagner s music
acceptably. The tuft on top of the pop*
cert was Beethoven’s concerto for violin
and ’chestra. Mr. Allpress played the fiddle
part in a manner which did equal credit to
his soul and his muscles, for the concerto is a tour
de force, lasting about three-quarters of an hour;
and the orchestra boomed and twanged, and pom-
pora’d in strapping style. I didn’t so keenly ap-
preciate the Sakantala overture —whicli suggested
a bit of particularly ferocious tapestry
set to music. . >y 1 "’ .
Apropos of music, the newest idea in religious
circles is to have an overture played for a
quarter of an hour before the agony com-
mences. It was announced last week that
the newly-arrived-from-Tasmania lady-organist
of a psychologically-inclined Sydney church,
which would be eternally staging problem-
plays if it ever turned inside-out and became a
theatre, would play the organ for a quarter of an
hour prior to the service—introducing, the worldly
might imagine, the principal airs to be heard dur-
ing the performance, with a chastened waltz-
movement illustrative of the deacon going round
after ttie threepenny-bits. Soon religious ads.
will run, “ Overture at 7.3 o—commence at 8.”
A particularly awful example of the horrible
effects of the Trinity College musical exams, is
the Queensland waltz, by Jean Lamond. Tins
awkward composition somehow reminds you
of a hash of ancient drinking-songs which are not
pursued far enough to be absolutely glaringly
“reminiscent,” but their ghosts hover o’er every
page most noticeably in the main air. And any-
body who could dance to it would also be able to
waltz to the tender strains of Chinese opera.
From Gordon and Gotch I have 'received anew
and revised copy of “Still Apart,” in D. A
dash after the. D would fill a long-felt want.
Titipu society now possesses two Mrs, Mala-
props moving in the highest circles—which is the
safest place for them to be if they really want
their uncertain English to ese.ape detection. 'lhe
latest from Mrs. M. No. 2 (whose society nick-
name is “Mrs. Partington”) was a story of how
she was driving to see a friend recently when “ I
nearly had a severe accident, for the horses shied
at a gladiator lying on the verandah.” Shemeant
alligator, of course, the same as she meant “ in-
flammation ” when she said about somebody’s
wound that “amputation” had set in.
Talking about alligators as verandah-ornaments
reminds me that the handsomest and most original
of lamp-stands is a tripod of emu-legs (from the
knee down and including the toes), which have
been thrown into a tan-pit just as they were cut
from the birds and left to tan for a year or so. So
far, the only part of an emu which has been made
into a drawing-room ornament is the large,
green egg, which is extremely monotonous as an
object of vertu —is never useful —and is almost as
common as woolwork flowers under glass. The
legs—so long overlooked —are rarer and naturally
more fashionable and expensive, for, while the
emu’s laying capacities are infinite, given a fair
field, there are seldom more than two legs to each
bird.
A Sydney medico had a rather unusual experi-
ence the other day, which almost induced him to
believe that women are only oysters in petticoats,
in the absence of satisfactory explanation. A
patient came to him suffering from a tumor in her
right ear, and, when the surgeon had operated and
removed the swelling, it was found to contain a
small pearl! The only explanation that occurred
was that the patient must have been sleeping with
her cheek on the ring when the pearl had got
loose and slipped in. This is another of the awful
consequences of being engaged to be married, and
is also a good yarn for an impecunious Romeo _to
remember when he wants to get over giving
jewels to his Juliet. “Take this plain gold ring,
my ownest. I love you too well to give you any-
thing that could drop down your ear.”
And talking of disappearing pearls somehow
reminds me that an Australian girl swallowed
four artificial teeth the other day. What a treat
it would be to see an Australian woman clad in
her own teeth and not girning at you with blue
horse-fangs in a yellow face, or vice-versa. Asa
rule an Australienne’s teeth are as false as her
tongue, only the teetli are safer. You can tell the
shop-made grinders at a glance, while you only
find out the falsehoods bit by bit.
Looking up the statistics, the other day, I was
interested to see that a foundling-asylum in Russia
receives 12,039 wild oats every year, of whom all
the boys are trained for the navy. Giddiness > is
hereditary—and this may account for the weird
proclivities of a large number of .naval officers
who wear tan boots and look quite harmless seen
publicly in a civilised city, although the denizens
of remote lands tell other tales. I hear, for in-
stance, that a certain gold - buttoned contin-
gent who went recently cruising amid the
innocent coral isles had a distinctly gorgeous time.
One of the games was a water-chute—each gallant
Oapt. Corcoran being deftly guided to the depths
below by a belle sauvage dressed in the very ex-
treme fashion of the country. Beyond the fun to
be got out of this sort of thing, the gallant cruise-
abouts don’t seem to have come back with any
very startling information. I hear that one
spoffish lieutenant has learnt how to get a good
buttercup gloss on his tan feet by means of banana-
skins—but that seems a little thing to go all those
weary miles after.
The Society of Artists* children*s-party last
Friday evening, at York-street Kink, was a clever
idea cleverly carried out. The novel programme
by Tom Roberts and Arthur Streeton)
was most artistically compiled from every point
of view, and the several hundred infants, rang-
ing from well-grown 14 to very small fours, had
a joyous time, what with the awful tragedy of
Punch and Judy, singing, dancing, funny sketches,
and absolutely unlimited “pastry” to crown the
whole and encourage the colic. The evening was
an amusing novelty for the oldsters, too, who
came in full war-paint, though the crowd was too
great for “ grown-up ” furbelows to make a hit,
and besides, it was most emphatically the nursery
first and the rest nowhere. Sydney society cer-
tainly has a fair share of pretty children. One
child in white, with blue rosettes and golden hair
d la Nellie Stewart’s “Paul Jones’’ wig, was a
veritable bundle of dimples—but what struck mo
most about some of the bantlings was their strik-
ing likeness to tlieir proud papas. You could ,
place almost all the society olive branches at least
without looking at the catalogue, so to speak, i
because of that strong likeness.
The Art Society’s annual conversazione, held at
the rooms, Pitt-st., on 9th inst,, was the usual
well-dressed crowd being entertained with vocal
and band music and long welcome intervals for
copious refreshments. Acting vice-royalty turned
up missing, and the lengthy brand-new president
of the society was detained at the Legislative
Assembly. Considering the new president’s indies,
it is possible he bad caught his hair in a Parlia-
mentary skylight and could not be extricated in
time to turn up. However, he sent the presi-
dentess and a daughter as his proxies, and,
generally speaking, the highest compliment a man
of the world can pay any function is to stop
severely away himself and send his wife and
daughter. The Art Society's conversazmne made
the usual success, and calmly performed its old
sleight-of-hand trick of getting more crowds into
a given space than would seem to be arithmetically
possible.
Talking about festivals and f£tes —the Sydney
bigwigs who attended a recent naval tea- party
were hurt in the tenderest part of their etiquette
at the attentions paid by the host to a veteran
politician and the lady whom he convoyed—report
having it that the lady’s white attire was a dress-
rehearsal for the bridal robes which she will
shortly assume. The veteran was reverently
seated in the front row, so to speak, all day long,
to the disgust of the Bigwig par excellence, who
is said never to have sent him a card for even the
most unflinchingly official functions.
“ H.M.S. Pinafore ’’ revived briskly at Sydney
Lyceum last Saturday. All the same, I have
heard and seen better Josephines than Juliet
Wray, who came like a blue coot —a burlesque
Josephine in a short satin redingote, a blue cap,
and red hair hanging down her back. Captains’
daughters don’t dress like that even in comic-
opera, and so her change to a long skirted bridal
gown and her hair (golden this time) in a teapot-
knot, was quite a relief. But most people won-
dered why Flo. Young wasn’t put up for the first
night, if only for her vocal abilities ; truth to tell,
Miss Wray’s voice was somewhat distressing at
various points in “Pinafore.”
Wallace Brownlow (and I wish to state, in
answer to several correspondents, that Wallace
Brownlow is his real name, and there is no fear
of him turning out to be William Brown) cut a
dash as Captain Corcoran, and balanced his
cocked hat as if to the manner born ; neither did he
catch his heels in his sword. The Captain is one
of W.B.’s picture-parts, evidently. Tapley made
a very pretty tar—indeed, I think “Rafe ” Rack-
straw is one of Tapley’s picture-parts, too—but
the she-sailors who danced the jig with flags in
the second act were not so picturesque, taking
them all round—only so many bounding argu-
ments why women shouldn’t wear trousers on top.
Lauri’s Sir Joseph reminded me of Captain Cook
as seen in'Hyde Park —especially one attitude —
but slow-running parts are not Lauri’s best vein.
I suppose we have at last arrived near the
denouement of the Dean case, and one can’t help
feeling glad that the whole bag of tricks was
revealed before any more women made exhibi-
tions of themselves in deifying the wily
ferry captain and showing a disposition to
twine flowers in his hair. A lot of
women have occasion to feel very small indeed
over this affair. 1 wonder, for instance, how that
excited female feels, who tore off her glove to
shake hands with the idol of the hour before he
toppled over, and then enthusiastically kissed the
place where his horny hand —the one with which
he poisoned Madaine’s tea—had pressed ? For
my part I must confess I thought the youth
falsely accused at first, but felt some doubts
about his innocence when I read that canting,
ultra-pious letter of his, concocted after his sen-
tence to death —a letter brimming over with
mawkish expressions of piety. Innocent people
are not always religious ; on the contrary, an easy
flow of Scripture is the trade-mark of calculating
guile in 99 cases out of a hundred. It is signifi-
cant that since the cat got out of the bag not a
telling text has escaped the prisoner’s lips—a cigar
and a newspaper, both profane things, are more in
his line apparently, which shows conclusively that
the man who turns the whites of his eyes up to
Heaven on the shortest notice has generally a
great deal to conceal.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

The most wholly criminal aspects of the
Dean case were that Crick made bis Parliamen-
tary statement about Meagher’s guilt on Tuesday
night, when this paper was just going to press,
and that Dean made bis confession on Wednesday
afternoon, when last week’s issue of The Bulle-
tin was nearly all printed. Considering that The
Bulletin has spoken not unkindly of Mr. Crick
on many occasions, it regards his conduct in con-
fessing on Tuesday night as black ingratitude. All
lawyers are requested to confess in future not
later than Monday afternoon.
• # #
The downfall oi Dean arises solely through
the desire of every weak man to breathe into the
capacious ear of somebody the dreadful secret
which is too large to be humped around alone. So
Dean breathed his story into the huge ear of
Meagher, and Meagher, built on the same plan,
promptly started breathing on bis own account.
If Dean hadn’t told Meagher, Meagher wouldn’t
have told Salomons, and Salomons wouldn’t have
told Want, and chemist Smith possibly wouldn’t
have told anybody, and the whole problem would
most likely have lain quiet till the end. A man
who knows how to get rid of the body on scientific
principles, and who lias no accomplices, and doesn’t
keep a diary, or talk in bis sleep, or breathe into
the large, convoluted ear of any person whatever,
is the ideal murderer. Therefore, every intended
assassin should take this for his motto: “Don’t
breathe.” If he must mention the dreadful fact
to ease his mind, then he should go outside the
Heads in a small boat at midnight and mention it
in Latin to the dog-star, and even then be should
do it only when a strong westerly is blowing.
# • •
Dean’s confession was a plain, business-
like statement of alleged fact with no frills on it.
Meagher’s was an elevated document with great
chunks of high-toned eloquence—the work of a
vain little solicitor posturing to the last. “ I only
wish I could put buck the universe again to the
Photo. by Kerry.
Mrs. DEAN and BABY.
day I made my first error” (quotation from the
“Silver King” slightly altered and badly im-
proved : “Oh, God,” says Wilfred Denver, “put
back Thy universe and give me yesterday ”). The
idea of Meagher putting back the universe is very
fine. “ And as there is a destiny that shapes our
ends rougb-bew them how we will ” (Shakespeare).
“Sir Julian Salomons became from me the re-
pository of the secret.” Even at this crisis
the fallen attorney doesn’t simply say, “I told
Salomons, Q.G.” Again, “I am determined to
endure mental torture no longer nor to stifle the
voice of truth.” “ I became aware after Ids con-
viction of his guilt, aud, coming from Darling-
hurst in the tram, I was torn asunder,” &c.
This is partly eloquence and partly bathos; picture
a police-court solicitor being torn asunder in a
tram ! And then there is the peroration :
I trust with my young life—-yet under 30 years of age—
I may in some other portion of the globe lead the life of
truth, and use my humble faculties in the small sphere I
shall move in for the promotion of the happiness and win-
ning the esteem of those I come in contact with in a
strange country, and that in the land I love my acts may
in days to come be forgiven and forgotten.
“ The land I love !” Patriotism, the last refuge
of an attorney ! The saddest thing about Meagher,
after all, is the eloquence of bis downfall. He fell
like G. Rignold, in the centre of the stage, with
the limelight on him and his eyes fixed on the
gallery.

It is notable that the S.M. Herald made
no reference whatever to the weeping-and-wailing
S.A. gubernatorial-clerical lunch-party at which

and at which “ all ” went down on their marrow-

developing a sense of humor? The latter alterna-
tive, however incredible, is preferable to the
theory that the Herald didn’t know about the
affair. That the clergy should turn dog on the
Herald is absolutely inconceivable.

Except M‘Millan, M.L.A., the committee
and officials of the Miry Dean fund are all
lawyers. Mrs. Dean is deserving of all sympathy,
for she has been cruelly injured, and the most
strenuous enquiries, fair and foul, have utterly
failed to disclose a single spot on her character.
But let there be no mistake about the fund raised
on her behalf—it is designed by, at any rate, the
non-legal subscribers, from whose pockets most of
it vvillnorne, solely as a means of placing her in
comfort, and not as a medium of whitewashing
any functionary whose failure to properly perform
his duty has been at least the indirect means of
enabling a cold-blooded intending assassin to
escape the full penalty of his crime.

# * *
That the proposal to revoke the royal
pardon granted to Dean on the report of the Com-
mission should be seriously discussed in official-
legal and political circles is a lamentable evidence
of the pettifogging style of the N.S.W. powers
that be. To revoke a royal pardon would be, as
it were, to debase, to “smash,” the legal cur-
rency. It would be like a bank dishonoring its
own big note in order to get level with a cat's-
meat man. It would be as though the Archangel
Gabriel, after sounding the last trump, told the
dead to get back into their graves for a
few minutes, as the Deity hadn’t yet finished
breakfast. The proposal to revoke so tremen-
dous an instrument as a royal pardon shows
how many grocer-souled wretches are in the
Botanv Bay legislature, at the Botany Bay bar,
Photo. by Newman.
DEAN, the Poisoner.
and on the Botany Bay press. Let us hope none
of them are on the Botany Bay bench.
The official who could talk of revoking a royal
pardon given to the lying master of a cockle-shell
ferry-boat, would be capable of pearl-creaming in
public the whisky-blue nose of bis Royal Master
or Mistress.

When in the condemned-cell, Possum
Dean wrote the following beautiful letter to
Parson Bennett-Anderson :
Darlinghurst Gaol,
April 13, 1895.
Dear Friend—
It gives me great pleasure to write these few lines to
you, trusting it will reach you with God’s speed, for
the comfort which you gave to me on your visit to
me on Thursday. Likewise the sermon which you left
with me, enHtled “ Death Overcome," which (/ires me
yreat spirit when / read it, w'lieh / am doinj daily, as I
have, to prepare myself to meet my Maker. Bat, thanks
to Oof, I wi|l he able to meet Him a pure and innocent
beiny; and I also hope the Lord will forgive the ones
which have brought this trouble on me, and that it may
please him to forgive mine, enemies, persecutors, and
slaiKlerers, and to turn their hearts, which has been
against me. I also hope to see you again when an oppor-
tunity affords you.
Trusting with God s will and wish,
I remain,
Yours innocently,
Cav. George Dean,
Darlinghurst Gaol.
1i.1.P.
When Possum got out he told the Norwich
Chambers meeting that—
He would do anything in his power to uphold his repu-
tation in his future life. He had led a very good life
before and trusted in God to continue to do so. He
would shun any traps that might be laid for him.
But he didn’t shun the traps enough. They
grabbed him—twice —on the steamer.
* * *
If any really keen, forceful young barrister
cuts bis throat anywhere in N.B.W. within the
next six months, The Bulletin will know that he
did it because lie had lost the one great oppor-
tunity of bis life—the chance to rise in one act to
the top of bis profession by defending (whether to
acquittal or conviction) George Dean. If that
misguided harbor mariner hadn’t confessed, but
bad lied to the bitter end, he would have afforded
somebody a splendid opening. His barrister could
have risen up and pointed out that his client was
a brave and noble-hearted young seaman (here
Dean would smite his hairy chest in the dock);
that he had gone through a great ordeal and was
yet strong in his innocence (here Dean would
assume the upward expression of Moses when lie
strikes the rock in the Sunday-school lithograph).
Then he would explain that his client hadn’t
fled from the country after his release like one
driven away by a guilty conscience (here Dean
would assume the attitude of G. Washington in
the darkest hours of bis fortunes). After that he
would allude to the discrepancies in the evidence
—the fact that Meagher said he fell on the floor
when he heard about the alleged discovery of bis
guilt, whereas a warder was present and could
assert that he didn’t fall on anything; the fact
that the chemist denied supplying the strychnine,
so that it was wholly unaccounted for; the fact
that witneseses who swore against him had
previously declared the exact opposite of what
they now asserted, and were as unreliable in one
capacity as in the other (here Dean would pass his
brown band through his back hair and look like
Sir Walter Raleigh when he was going to be
executed on a false accusation). Then, when he
had spread himself on these and several dozen
other discrepancies, he would point with a
trembling forefinger to his client’s previous good
character (here Dean would again pound on his
chest, like the cheerful African gorilla) and throw
in a peroration. And, instead of all these glories,
the fallen mariner, who is evidently quite devoid
of all artistic-legal instincts, made a common,
vulgar confession with common, vulgar perspira-
tion slopping off him as he did it, and his head
smoking with agitation and moisture.

The New Woman has progressed from the
psychological to the bike-ological.
The Church of England is
now cutting its own throat with
the pig’s-foot of divorce. It won’t
recognise as a churchman or a
churchwoman anyone legally
divorced, unless he or she had
been sinned against adulteronsly.
It won’t remarry anyone divorced,
unless he or she has been divorced
for that one reason only, and is
himself or herself free from the
allegation of adultery. Of course,
it can’t prevent these other lies
and shes from getting re-married
elsewhere and elsehow, but it holds
them, in the Church’s awful eye,
as living in a state of sinful
non-wedlock. This seems “bad
business ” for the church.

The report of the last South
Sea Islands cruise of the mission
steamer, John Williams, is, in
places, exhilarating reading for
the unregenerate. At Tan, in the
island of Manna, the Queen de-
livered a sermon ; the maids of
honor, “in gala dresses,” chap-
eroned by the Queen’s cup-bearer,
visited the steamer —it may easily
be unofficially supposed that,
after the way of women, they
mashed whomsoever they would
—and “a special official reception
to do honor to the ship took the
form of a grand kava-making
ceremony, at the conclusion of
which there was a feast.” In
other words, a grand drunk on
stewed and brewed kava-musb and
maidens’ spittle ! And afterwards
a gorge ! There is evidently much
hope for gospel-missions that in-
clude “ kava-makmg ’’ among
their recognised and patronised
institutions. Kava and roast pig !
Who wouldn’t be an islands mis-
sionary ? Queen-shepherds and
maids-of-bonor ! And a royal
cup-bearer! Almost do they per-
suade us to be a gospel buccaneer.
The Melbourne Y.M.C. A., having arranged
for a festival at a local cricket-ground on 26th
mst., and Lord Brassey having promised bis vice-
regal presence, the Christian masters of the cere-
monies made a circular appeal as follows to mem-
bers of the pious bong-tung : “The members’
balcony has been especially reserved, as well as
retiring-room, in which afternoon tea, &c., will be
provided [‘ etcetera ’ in the retiring-room sounds
mysterious], and as the balcony will only hold
about 100 people, and the management desire to
secure the presence of suitable society to associate
with his Excellency, a limited number of tickets at
one guinea have been issued. If you are able to
accept the enclosed ticket, to admit yourself and
a lady, please reply at earliest convenience.” Cer-
tain less exalted Christians, who, although Saved,
are not suitable for Brassey, seem to have taken
affront at this circular. Which is sad indeed :
The Y.M.C. A. professes a socialistic religion
which reverences poverty and systematically curses
the rich, but its idea of what constitutes “suitable
society to associate with his Excellency ” is—the
fat person who can spare .£1 Is.
Such disgusting grovel is typical of a city in
which, almost above every other city, they have
always a welcome for the Golden Calf and never
one for the Prodigal Son.

A fact not hitherto stated in connection
with the disgraceful struggle of the loyal rabble at
Melb. Govt. House, on the occasion when the
visitors’ book was opened, is that in the
fight the Madden book, which was also lying on
the table, was knocked off, trampled upon, and
kicked about the floor. It is a bulky tome, and
the women who found it getting mixed up with
their legs ami petticoats, stamped on it furiously.
Finally, it was kicked out into the middle of the
entranoe-hall, where it lay until it was picked up
by a servant. And the people who did this were
those who, only a few days before, had been eat-
ing Madden’s cakes and drinking Madden’s claret-
cup ! The indecent rudeness of a loyal “ push ”is
only equalled by its ingratitude.

That disgustingly-sycophantic body, St.
Kilda (Vic.) Municipal Council, which lately re-
duced its charity-vote from .£2OO to £lOO on plea
of bad times, and which at the following meeting
reinstated a mayoral allowanceof .£l5O to provide
swill at the council meetings, is now engaged in
flashing around the ratepayers’ money on the pier
and esplanade, where several triumphal arches
and other curiously-shaped piles of decaying veget
able matter are awaiting Brassey’s arrival.
• • *
A correspondent enquires what has become
of case No. 1862 under a letter near the com-
mencement of the alphabet in the books of
N.S.W. divorce-court? Also, whether it is possible
to get a divorce privately in N.S.W. ? The Bul-
letin cannot answer either question though it
has heard (possibly unfounded) rumors cqnoern-
cerning such things. As the books of the divorce-
court cannot, by rule of court— i.e., by a purely
Judge-made law—be inspected without an order
from a solicitor on one side or other of a case, it
would seem that where a divorce-case doss not
appear on the published list, and has been heard
at a time when reporters cannot be expected to be
present, it can be kept perfectly dark as long as
ever the respective attorneys engaged like to keep
it dark.
# * *
Melbourne’s Mayor-elect is one Strong,
who, when the Council had chosen him, as per
previous arrangement, to succeed Mayor Snow-
den, “could not find words to express his feelings.
He was neither proud nor vain, but he thought he
had every reason for feeling proud, in being elected
by the unanimous vote of the council, to be chosen,
as it were, by the voice of the people, whose repre-
sentatives they were. It had been said that the
king could do no wrong, and he supposed that
implied that the sovereign must be right if guided
by the counsels of his advisers. So it would be
with him (Or. Strong).” The Bulletin will watch
the career of Strong with much interest. An
immediate successor to the reviled Snowden who
can fancy himself an infallible monarch, elected by
the Voice of People, ought to be well-worth watch-
ing. A tout will be hired to rise with the lark
and see Strong gallop.

An Italian cruiser, the Cristoforo
Colombo, is now in Australian waters. She has
on board Prince Luigi of Savoy, a 23-year-old
nephew of King Humbert. The Australian fair
sex has strong reason to remember the last lot of
Italian officers, with their dark eyes and their
pink champagne.

# • *
Major-Gen. Hutton declined to allow the
Permanent Artillery band to play in connection
with the Sydney Eight-hours’ Day, on the ground
that the celebration was a political one. A
doubtful reason. Six months ago, he ruled that
the band might play for hire wherever asked, so
long as no conflict with duties was entailed, and
gave as his ground that such bands enjoyed such
liberties all over the world. This, again, is in-
sufficient as a reason. But withal, he is more
consistent than the Eight-hour people, who first
objected to the band playing in competition with
unemployed musicians, and next applied to have
it play at their own ceremony.

America’s adoration of titles. The aristocratic

E. Reynolds, five-tnne
amateur cycle champion of
Australia, is a sinewy,
limber, 22-year-old Maori-
lander, who believes that
bicycling has made a man
of him, for he began with
aconstitution nonetoo good.
Since ’9O he iias raced 130
times being placed 100
times and scoring 30 wins.
His best-remembered feat
is his sensational perform-
ance in Auckland (M.L.)
one afternoon two years
ago, when he won the one,
two, three and five mile
races from scratch, although
the limit-man in the five
miles had a half-mile start.
He has raced all distances
from half-a-mile to the 20-
mile road-race, and beholds
the Australian amateurhalf-
mile record (Imin. 7sec.).
Recently he established the
mile record (2.24 J) on the
Sydney track, and ran his
rivals right off their legs in
the five-mile race. He rides
a machine supplied by the
Austral Cycle Agency.

There is a watchdog at an Adelaide brewery
who, at night, flies savagely at every intruder not
attired in policeman’s uniform. A perfect stranger
in that guise can pass without raising a whimper.
A CHARACTER.
N.S.W.
I.D.
He lay upon his riches, like a toad,
And glared at all creation. Rain and sun,
Kinsmen and neighbours, seemed to him at one,
Planning his ruin. “ Let there not be owed
One pious rite,” he said, “lest I who sowed
So well, be ere the reaping quite undone
By floods, or drought, or fraud too fine to shun !”
Forthwith he reckoned all his ledger showed :
Then rose with face demure and gravely took
(First locking fast his gold and books of trade)
From its due place of state the Holy Book,
Chose the fit passage, slowly stept downstairs,
And called the shop-boy and his one small maid
With proud humility to evening prayers.

The Wild - Cat Column .
The Mutual Life Association of Australasia
considers that The Bulletin did it a serious injustice in
its issue of 17th August, and again last week, when it
mentioned that the Association’s “last balance-sheet
shows no reserve whatever ” for depreciation of securities.
Tub Bulletin fails to see where the injustice comes in,
hut it desires to state the circumstances as fully and fairly
as possible. The Mutual Life Association this year wrote
£5270 off the valuation of its assets to provide for depre-
ciation. Last year it wrote off £16,283 for the same pur-
pose, and it makes a larger or smaller allowance every
year, presumably sufficient to cover any falling-off in this
respect. As Tub Bulletin remarked in reviewing the
Association’s last report, it “ wisely makes provision for
depreciation as it goes along.” The Mutual Life Associa-
tion is preparing to put its valuation on a 3J per cent,
basis, like the A.M.P., and unlike moat of its other busi-
ness rivals. It Ins already made large reserves for this
purpose. The fact that the Mutual Life is about lo take
this valuable precaution is much to its credit, and the
sooner other societies do the same the better it will be for
their policyholders.
* * * *
The N.S.W. loan of £4,000,000 went off in
London at £96 18s. 3d. Allowing for the “ accrued in-
terest ” the price is, roughly, £96 Bs. 9d., and after
deducting stamp-duty, and estimating the expenses on
the lowest scale, the province will get something like 195
net for each £lOO. Under these circumstances, the fact
that N.S.W. has floated what is nominally the cheapest
loan ever issued by an Australasian province doesn’t
amount to very much after all. Making allowance for
the loss of £5 on every £lOO at repayment 40 years hence,
the cost of the loan is £3 ss. Bd. per cent. The province’s
4 per cent, debentures with an 18 years’ currency are now
worth £llO 10s. in the Sydney market, which, allowing
for the loss to the holder on redemption, means that they
are worth about £3 2s. 6d. per cent, per annum. In the
case of a local issue, the saving in expenses of manage-
ment, the work being done locally by the Treasury in-
stead of by a bank as in London, and the further saving
in cost of remitting interest, are additional advantages.
Therefore, there seems no reason to believe that the
London market, despite all the glare and whoop about
Mr. Reid’s great financial operation, has done anything
which the local market, with decent management, couldn't
have done better.
• ♦ * *
The new N.S.W. Civil Service Act provides
that all future Stale employes must insure their lives.
But it doesn’t provide any State institution or any Stale-
guaranteed and State-audited private institution in which
they can insure. It simply compels them to pay annually
into a possibly-rotten office-for no one outside each
office, and only a few inside, really know whether it
is rotten or not—a considerable proportion of their earn-
ings. If the office bursts at last, and the widow and
children of the deceased C.S. are left destitute, they will
have good reason to ask the Government to refund the
premiums (with compound interest) which it com-
pelled the old man to pay, and which are now so
very badly W’anted. Why not create a State Life
Insurance Department, and compel the civil servants
to insure in that? In the circumstances the De-
partment, under moderately good management,
would be the best thing of its kind in Australasia —the
A.M.P. Society not excepted. It would have, say, 30,000
reliable clients. It would need to pay no commission, or
travelling expenses, or advertising charges to get their
business. It would incur no expense in collecting pre-
miums ; the Treasury would deduct the premium from
each man's salary and send it along. It wouldn’t need
any stupendous office to impress the public with. It
could invest all its money in State and good municipal
securities, in its own office property, and in loans on its
own policies, and thus save the vast amount of clerical
and legal wmrk involved in investments on mortgage, and
also most of the big losses through “ depreciation,” which
come along every now and then and make such ghastly
holes in the profits.
With all these expenses saved the State institu-
tion, under a clever insurance expert, should get along by
spending something like 4 per cent, of its income in
expenses. The A.M.P. Society spends nearly 9 per cent.,
and other Australasian offices anything up to 30 per
cent. And though the Government denartment, by
avoiding the somewhat shaky mortgage business, would
probably not realise more than £4 per cent, on its funds,
whereas the other offices average £5 Bs., the saving in
expenses and in depreciation should enable it to pay the
biggest bonuses in Australasia. With its compulsory
civil-service business it would be independent of outside
support, but it would take as much outside support as it
could get without paying commission to agents. The
public would gradually learn of its existence, and come
to it with their proposals ; the large profits and the State
security would be sufficient advt. If Premier Reid lets
this chance slide he will have missed the biggest oppor-
tunity of his career.
• * » *
Maoriland Government is going to transfer its
London financial business—inscription of loans, payment
of interest, flotation of new loans, &c. from the Bank of
England to the 8.N.Z., and thus the annual payment of
£14,000 a year, instead of going to a purely English insti-
tution, will go to assist one that is at least partly local.
The other provinces should follow this example, if they
are not prepared to conduct the business at the Agent-
Generals’ offices, and thus a great many thousands per
an. would go to support local concerns. The 8.N.5.W.,
National of Australasia, Bank of Victoria, Bank of Ade-
laide, Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney, Commercial of
Australia, Q.N.8., and many others have London offices
where they could represent their respective provinces
with as much dignity as is needed and scoop in the com-
mission in a thankful frame of mind.
• * • •
The Federal Bank liquidation is getting on
slowly. When it closed its doors the late Munro institu-
tion had £202,214 in hand, and it has since collected
£320,419 from its creditors and only £42,348 from share-
holders, making £564,981. After paying notes and the
Government's preferential claims the creditors have had
£210,000 in dividends, and ttiere are £1,250,000 still due
them. The remaining assets are nominally £1,500,000,
but if they realise half that amount they will do about as
well as can be expected.
• ♦ • •
The Mutual Life Association issues a new table
under which it is possible to insure by paying only half the
usual rate for the first five years. This is a great con-
venience to those who want to get the largest amount of
insurance for the smallest immediate cost. Other offices
do the same variety of business, hut the Mutual Life
Association has extended the principle further than
usual by applying it not only to whole-life, but also to
limited premium and endowment assurances.
» * * •
The blank folly of the Victorian bankruptcy
system, under which any person may set up in business
as a trustee in insolvency, and b“ entrusted with the
winding-up of estates, and do very much as he pleases,
was badly shown up in a recent application for the re-
moval of one of the rapacious tribe. This individual had
charge of the estate of a bankrupt importer. The assets
realised £497, and as the trustee charged £533 for col-
lecting them there was nothing left for anybody save
himself. As a rule, no single creditor takes the trouble
to move in such a case, and the trustee has a pleasant
time, but the present instance moved an aggravated
bank to action. The trustee got into the box and pro-
tended to explain. He paid one clerk a salary of £2 10s.
a week, and charged the estate £6 a week for his services.
He paid one of his sons £l, and another 15s. per week,
and he charged the estate £6 a week for their combined
services. Whether the estate got their whole services, or
wheiher they were being also charged against other
estates at the same tim», didn’t appear. According to
the trustee “it was the custom” to do those things,
which assertion is probably true. An ndvrUsng
account of £l4 10s. was charged, by “ a slip of the pen,”
as£2o 4<. Finally, the trustee had taken the a-counts
aw-ay with him to a remot- part of Gippsland, so that the
estate and all information connected therewith practically
vanished. Yet the court only disallowed the more glaring
Items in the bill, and made an order for £lO 10s. costs.
Another Melbourne trustee has just been re-
moved from the charge of a large insolvent estate for the
simple and beautiful reason that he has disappeared
altogether, and no one knows where ’e are. How the
estate, or any other estates in his charge, had got on
under these conditions wasn’t explained. If the other
departments of the law were run in the same helter-
skelter fashion, and it became necessary to appoint a new
judge every few days, on the ground that an old one had
disappeared with a prisoner’s bail-money, what exciting
times there would be in the land of £d. in the £ I

* * * *
St. Joseph’s Investment and Building Society,
of Sydney, has also arranged to reduce deposit interest
from sto 3 percent. A rather desolate report was pre-
sented by the chairman. St. Joseph’s principal asset
consists of £256,000 lent on mortgage. Mortgagors repre-
senting £57,000 are still paying up interest and instal-
ments of principal as they become due. On about
£40,000 the society is getting interest with tolerable
regularity. The other £156,000 represents foreclosed
properties. Therefore, St. Joseph’s is loaded with un-
saleable land and buildings which yield little revenue,
and does its second reconstruction in a depressed frame
of mind.
• * • •
The Haymarket Permanent Land, Building
and Investment Co. (Sydney) is following th“ general
example, and arranging to reduce interest on deposits
from sto 3 per cent, per an. The time for repayment is
extended indefinitely, payment to be made by instalments
of Is. in the £ as the society’s properties are realised.

Stamp-selling in Sydney when the commission
was 2J per cent, used to be a rather good game. One or
two people who went, into the business extensively used
to sell thousands of pounds’ worth on mail-day and
quarter-day ; the big offices being canvassed for orders
and the commission whacked.

“Jimmy” Mirams’s libel-suit against the
Argus for £lO,OOO form d the subject of an application
before Justice Williams the other day. His Honor
declined to hear affidavits with the remark, “ I am nn un-
fortunate party in the society mentioned.” Mr. Williams
Game out of the Premier Permanent smash with something
over five figures to the bad. Some recent strictures, no
doubt, moved him to adjourn the Mirams action with
scant ceremony, “in order that it may be taken by
another Judge." This formula has become a regular
thing now. Every Melb. Judge is more or less unfitted
to adjudicate upon any financial case without the bias of
personal circumstances, or a suspicion of it, creeping in ;
so A “passes on” to B, and ihe effect secured by the pro-
cess is, perhaps, a little worse than if he hadn’t.

The Abrahams brothers have not starved them-
selves all in vain. Now that the Greener gun firm is
trying to get from the Abrahams’ estate the damages
awarded by a Melb. jury, it seems Chat brother Emanuel
is not strong enough to give ei id> nce. He has malingered
himself one degree and a-half below normal temperature,
and the enquiry as to what has become of the firm’s
alleged wealth must stand over until December. This
Abrahams business threatens to give Australia’s com-
mercial credit another nasty little shaking.

Two Melb. Stock Exchange seats lately sold at
£lOOO each, and five more are likely to be quitted at
aforesaid figure. For the last three or four years mem-
bers have had to ante-up an annual call of £25 each.
Exchange now numbers 200 members.
• • * *
The Yic. Assembly has gone back on the sweep-
ing reductions it made a few weeks ago on coir and jute
matting. The same old reason is assigned. Amemberin
the importing interest told the House that these mattings
were not made in Victoria. The House, in the vastness
of its ignorance, didn’t know any better, and made the
reductions. After a while some one learned by the merest
accident that the articles in question were manufactured
in the province, or else he thought he learned it, and the
House put the duty up avain. The blank—not to say
blanky—ignorance of the Victorian M.L.A. about the
industries he legislates for is a great circumstance.
• * * *
Hardacre urged the other day in Q. Assembly
that the Sugar Works Guarantee Act provided insufficient
security, inasmuch as sugar-lands, from continuous crop-
ping, declined in value. A planter advocate then arose
and assured llardacre that there wasn’t the least danger,
inasmuch as sir a -lands became the more productive the
more they were cropped. Let the waste lands on the
Mary, once smiling plantations, answer the saccharine
Ananias.
• * * *
Prahran (Melb.) Council called for tenders for
scavenging. Lowest tender, with clause fixing minimum-
wage of ss. per day, £3444; without minimum wage
clause, £2987. Council resolved on doing work by day-
labor. bossed by its own officials, at minimum-wage rates.
Cost for financial year just closed, £2854, or a clear saving
of £590 on minimum-wage tender, or a saving of £133
over non-restricted tender. Instructive.
• ♦ » •
It is a fatal thing for a paper to be ruled by a
board of directors. Look at the dreary piles of figures
which prosy prolix Pinschof, chairman of Melb. Herald
directors, has been shooting into the columns of that
devoted organ for weeks past. There lives no editor who
can edit his chairman.

once-partner, merely for the use of deceased’s name.

last month.

To date, a down south syndicate has spent
£20,000 over its Bingara diamond properly. It is still
hopeful.
♦ » » »
The bi-metallic controversy is cropping up in the
Solomons. The currency there consists mainly of porpoise
teeth. Certain traders have recently tried to introduce
gold as a medium of exchange, but the Solomon Islander,
foreseeing disturbance of trade and depreciation of the
Government reserves of porpoise teeth, scornfully rejects
the new currency and clings lovingly to the old one.
• • • •
Melbourne compositors feel sore over the pre-
sent practices of big Southern drapery firms, which, by
getting their season fashion-circulars printed in England
and posted therefrom to Melb. householders per aid of a
dir dory and stamp, rob the local P.O. revenue and
deprive the trade of employment.

It wotdd only be in accordance with the eternal
fitness of things if a Collingwood man, with only a 6-foot
hack-yard, were to win the ostriches in St. Alban's sweep ;

• * •
It is suggested that one of the luckiest Westra-
lian scrip-holders might do worse than pay up the many
shillings in the £ which his numerous trade creditors
lost over him when be went bung not many years ago. It
would mean all the difference between poverty and com-
fort to one honorable, straight-dealing old trader who
was ruined by giving credit to that mining speculator
when he was in business.
• * * *
In one Australian liquor-concern, four banks are
interested to the extent of a million.
* ♦ » ♦
No one ever sees worn sovereigns in Australia.
They all get away to dear old Hingland to pay interest
on Australia’s many debts before they begin to show signs
of wear and tear. You can tell a deeply-indebted nation
by its gold coinage ; it is only a creditor country which
circulates battered sovereigns. The land where the
sovereigns are new and the bank-notes old and dirty is in
a bad way.
♦ ♦ » *
Tasmania shipped last season to England 120,000
cases of apples which realised £60,000, out of which
£42,000 went for freight alone, the balance remaining to
pay other charges and to recompense the exporter.
4s. 3d. per case freight was charged, but shippers have
struck, and it has been shown that 2s. 6d. freight will pay
the shipowners.
* » • ♦
A Melbourne lady has sued the N.Z. Loan and
Mercantile Agency for her reconstructed deposit of £3700
and £371 interest, and got a verdict that the coin must be
paid over. The decision is based on the fact that the co.
did its reconstruction in England only, the Australian
creditors not being consulted, and that the arrangement
is therefore not binding on them. This opens up a large
question, but it isn’t likely to cause any serious trouble.
Very few people w’ant to get their money out of a reliable
4J per cent, investment these times.
* * » »
That prosperous concern, the South British In-
surance Co. of Maoriland, has finished anolher remark-
ably good year. The fire and marine premiums and in-
terest amount to £219,062, and the net profit is £32,674.
Also, there is a balance of £6239 from last year, making
£38,913 available. Of this £20,000 is added to reserve-
fund, £2500 to reinsurance fund, £674 to depreciation
account, £9694 goes to pay a dividend of 3s. per share,
and £6044 is carried forward.

Huddart, Parker and Co.’s success in competing
with the Union S.S. Co. for the Maoriland business lias
aroused envy in the trade, and another shipping co. is

will start again more violently than ever, and fares from
Sydney to Auckland will presently get down to ss.

The Ocean Accident and Guarantee Corpora-
tion, a British concern, has started business in
Sydney. The Ocean has a capital of £lOO,OOO, and
a reserve fund of £135,147, so it offers excellent
security to policyholders, and it issues insurances against
infectious diseases as well as against ordinary accidents.
The Sydney office is at 58 Pitt-strcet.
• • » •
The Australian Star publishes a lovely story
of the fortunes made by Adelaide men in Weslralia.
“ One young man, well known in the city, left two years
ago for Coolgardie a poor man, and to-day is the holder
of 450,000 Great Boulder shares, and is worth a million.”
Now, there are only 175,000 Great Boulder shares al-
together. Another Adelaide citizen, writing to a friend,
says: “ You can congratulate me. I am worth £150,000
in cash and shares and on paper.” Yes, but how'much
on paper? True, Adelaide men are doing very well in
Coolgardie, but they are not doing quite as well as that.

For the week ended Oct. 5 the output of ore
from the Broken Hill mines was 14,220 tons, of wdiich the
Proprietary contributed 7828 tons ; Junction, 1500 ; Block
14, 1585; North, 523; South, 865 (under the average);
Block 10, 1447 ; and Central, 572 (much under average).
* ♦ ♦ ♦
According to N.S.W. Government Analyst
Hamlet, a cloud of lead weighing 15 tons is thrown off
from the chimneys of the great silver mines at Broken
Hill every 24 hours. The fumes kill even the local cats.

nung’s buyer for £9OO, has been unearthed by Kenneth
Grant on Block 3, White Cliffs (N.S.W.). The Hoffnungs
seem to be monopolising the opal trade.

An English syndicate lately applied to S. A.
Govt, for tiie right to lease 50,000 sq. m. of the Northern
Territory at 6d. per mile, and undertook to spend £lOO,OOO
in developing it. It also wanted a right to buy after

Peremptorily declined. Fat Man now' indignant with
Kingston for “blocking the progress of the territory."
• * * •
B.H. Proprietary director Muecke, after visiting
the mine, admits, semi-puhliolv, that the fire in Block 11
is not out. He pretty accurately describes the situation
when he says that the old stopes, filled with partly-burned
timber, have been changed into charcoal beds. The most
serious recent effect is the stoppage of ironstone supplies
to the Port Pirie furnaces. This ironstone, got from the
open cuts, besides being a good flux, is rich in silver ; and
as only ironstone without silver can be bought locally,
the output is likely to show a still further, though slight,
falling off.

The 523 tons of B.H. North sulphides treated
by way of experiment at B.H. South Concentrators, gave
170 tons concentrates assaying 69 per cent. lead, 18oz.
silver. Manager Paul and the directors have been in con-
sultation in Melb. as to the best form of mill to erect.

Kurragong, Wyalong, has crushed 307 tons at
the local battery for 3270 z. gold. Concentrates assay
17oz. to the ton and tailings loz. 12dwts. The reef is
now showing 6ft. wide !
Bolte and party’s returns from 6J tons, treated

The Paddy Lackey mine, Sunny Corner,
N.S.W., has been placed under offer for, it is said, £lB,OOO.
This mine has been worked in a primitive way for over
ten years, and has a large body of stone, which has, so
far, averaged nearly an ounce to the ton.
» * * »
Considerable improvement has taken place
lately in the Lake George United, N.S.W., by the opening
up of a new’ body of payable Copper and silver ore.
“The Golden Gully mine, N.5.W.,” writes a
Bulletin London correspondent, "did not go at all well
on the market. I am told the promoters ‘stuck,’ and
did not get 100 shares taken up. They then tried to make
a market; and, although they struggled hard to work off
the shares through the jobbers, so far they have not met
with mueh success. They are now going to try and place
some on the continent.” Cables received about the float-
ing of this mine had created an impression amongst the
local shareholders that what they had sold for a few
pounds had been sold in London for thousands.
• • • •
The mining-frenzy in Adelaide seems to be
almost as big as when Broken Hill boom was at its height.
Half-a-dozen brokers are said to have made £lO,OOO each
in commissions alone. Outside speculators have profited
considerably. M’sP. have been unable to resist the
temptation, and many women have entered the mining
arena, determined to share the wealth of the Golden West.
All very well at present.

Lake View, Hannan’s (fortnightly crushing),
195 tons, 581 oz. This is the second W.A. mine which has
established its consistent richness, but the small amount
of divs. it has paid indicates the great cost of mining in
W.A.
• * * •
The scarcity of water is again being felt in
Conlgardie district. One month we are told of heavy
rains filling all the dams ; the next that all thecondensers
are again at work, but never an allusion to the fact that
the bulk of the water is absorbed, owing to the spongy
nature of the soil.

A sample Coolgardie boom wire : “ The Kings-
ley Hill mine, Broad Arrow, has been sold to the London
and South African 00., of which Mr. Cecil Rhodes is
managing director. Mr. H. Rice has also a large interest
in the lode”—[We know Rhodes, but who is Rice?] —
" which is 53ft. wide, and four different assayers in Cool-
gardie gave results varying from IJoz. to 30oz. to the
ton.” The variation is considerable, but if we strike the
mean we get an average of over 150 z., and, bear in mind,
the reef is 53ft, widel One nan quite understand Cecil
Khodes’ action, and see the reason of the recent
slump in South African shares. True, they turn out there
over 200,0000 z. a month, but they have about 3000 stamps
at work, and what would this mine turn out with 3000
stamps? Say (allowing 120 tons weekly for each 10 head)
540,0000 z. a week, or tivo millions sterling—£loo,ooo,ooo
a year. The Bulletin is tempted to speck ten bob in
that mine.

• » * •
The famous Madame Berry mine has put up a
world’s alluvial div. record of £900,000, while close on
£150,000 has gone in a 7J and 9 per cent, royalty to the
Seven Hills estate owners, chiefly the defunct Martin
Loughlin and W. Bailey, Ballarat’s “Scotch” horsey
man, whose annual cut tots up £lO,OOO. Although
Madame seems to be on the wane—the last block of the
rich southern gutter was lately taken out—the chances
are that the Ristori West run of wash will be met with in
the small section of untried ground now being pros-
pected. In tier palmy days Madame paid 10s. a month
per share. Biggest holder, A. J. Sprague, a Melb. man.
Altogether Madame has been kind to original scrippers,
each 17s. 9d. paid-up share having returned £46 odd.
Berry Consols, 95th div. of 2s. Gold won,
136,3840 z. ; divs., £237,000; royalty (74) paid, £43,000.
Long worked rich ground at No.’l shaft nearly exhausted,
but the bores.abotit a mile away have proved a big body
of wash to exist there. At No. 2 shaft, near where, in
deep ground, it is thought the Australasian lead has been
located, the wash is turning out well. This lead spreads
out to a tremendous width. A big area yet to work with
decidedly favorable prospects.
Another 40ft. driving should land the Berry
Consols Extended in the gold-bearing country which
dipped underfoot from a rise from west main level.
B fore dipping the gutter panned out 12oz. to the
machine, and if it lives up to that richness when located
again shareholders will not regret their long call-paying
era.
• * • •
Working Miners Homebush Co., at Mary-
borough, is the first alluvial Vic. property into which the
Britisher has ventured. Old co. paid £34,500 divs., and
it will have a sixth interest in the new co., which furnishes
£25,000 working capital.
• • • *
New field between Orbost and Bendoe, in the
Croajingolong district, promises well; Govt, mining in-
pector Sharpe names it as the best Gippsland find since
Long Tunnel, and it extends overa wide belt of auriferous
country. Chief mine, Ace of Clubs, has a 4ft. lode,
picked up a few chains off a 7in. lode discovered by learn-
ing. The Bairnsdale R.C. priest has pegged out a claim.
At Caasilis, near Omeo, several shows are turn-
ing out 3oz. stone, with an occasional break of Boz.
thin, and until deep level permanency assured, public
hold off this as well as the Glen Wills field.
Some smart Melb. men have taken up big slices
of alluvial country at Drouin. Good gold got in the
shallow', and bores will shortly prospect for the rich deep
gutter supposed to exist.
• • * •
Wired that “miners, mechanics, and laborers”
are in demand at Coolgardie. No doubt a repetition of
the Broken Hill hoom experience, when hundreds of
W'orkmen took to stock-jobbing. While shares mounted,
of course, they did well ; after that—well, they had to
slink back to work in the mines. And then the’eompeti-
tion began. It may pay Eastern miners to remember
that.
* * ♦ *
Mailed from London to Bulletin :—“ The
market here is still very good for W.A. mines, and things
in the Hannan’s district are now- on the boom. Oiher
districts are not so much sought after—notably, the
Murchison. It would be a hard matter lo place a Mur-
chison mine here now, even with the present boom.
People do not care for that region, as the bat teries are not
realising anticipations. It is hard to tell how long the
boom will last, but it looks to me as if there ought to he
an end to it soon, as people are getting filled up with this
sort of stuff, and when the time comes for unloading there
will be a colossal scramble. Everybody is bull now, there
are no bears in the field, and it is not safe to sell, no
matter how bad a thing is. People buy the bad stock
because it is cheap, and raise the price on you should you
undertake to bear it.”
* * * •

Dr. J. E. Neild and Prof. Halford are the
only surviving members of the original teaching
staff of Melb. medical school. Both were born in
1824, and are therefore 71 years old.
George R. Ashton is bringing out a volume
of “ Australian Sketches,” through Bell and Co.
Sketches by himself, text by ex-Gov. Hopetoun,
Max O’Rell, late Dr. Pearson, and others.
One prominent journalist on a Melb. daily
was particularly incensed at Trenwith’s recent
allusion to the average pressman as a “literary
prostitute.” He regarded it as a personal reflec-
tion on himself, and a vile punning allusion to his
name.
Premier Kingston has got a cable from the
London directors of the S. A. Co. apologising to
him for the assault committed by Sparks, their
Adelaide manager. Kingston expresses the hope
that they will not deal harshly with his late
opponent.
W. J. Trickett, M. L.C., at Sydney Town

he should have had no mercy, but should have
been hung, drawn, and quartered.” Some men
will never obtain what others think are their
deserts.

Mark Twain was so delighted with Marcus
Clarke’s “For the Term of His Natural Life”
that he devoted himself to getting all Clarke’s
books. He has got a complete set with
the exception of “Long Odds,” which is at
present out of print. “ Long Odds ”is the book
in which Clarke —who knew nothing about sport-
ing matters —made a filly win the Derby.
Justice Williams (Vic.) has answered the
uproar of criticism of his new religion, in a little
lecture on Mrs. Grundy. His Honor abuses the
old lady, and again held up his noble exemplar,
George Higinbothara, as a man who followed the
right and dusted his boots with the sacred robes
of Mrs. G. But how does Hartley Williams,
K.C.M.G., account for his bauble, his Imperial

and husky blessing are a joy and comfort to his

It is averred that George Rignold, when
first he thought about producing a Biblical play,
asked several friends, who professed to have read
the Bible, what particular part of it would be
appropriate. The story most strongly urged upon
him, ere lie fixed on Joseph, was that of the
Prodig d Sun. George didn’t quite know what
the Pr idigal Son did in particular, but he asked
questions, and ascertained that the principal
character would have to live for a time amongst
swine, and eat the husks which the swine did eat.
That killed th ■ project. George barred associating
with pigs, but why didn’t he play the Fatted
Calf?

It was endeavored to get up a press recep-
tion to Mark Twain in Adelaide, but the reporters
objected either to advertise themselves at Mark’s
expense or to advertise him at their expense.
When Fronde visited Adelaide the solitary person
to receive him was “the sandhill savage,” Capt.
Dick Jagoe, who from time immemorial has been
shipping reporter for both Adelaide dailies.
The Sketch on Parkes’s sonnets :
It is a valiant little book, full of fresh, youthful enthu-
siasms, and evidence of mental and physical health. Will
any of our age-worn, weary young poets ever be able to
write an autobiographical sonnet called “Four Score,”
and in this temper?—
I count the mercifullest part of all
God’s mercies, in this coil of eighty years,
Is that no sense of being disappears
Or fails.
Apropos, there is a shriekingly-funny lie current
just now about the evident non-failure of one
sense of the virile old gentleman’s being.
Mr. A. W. Eales, who was bom at Duck-
enfield, Morpeth, N.S. W., barely 22 years ago, ia
incomparably the best shot of his age in Australia
and at present the best in N.S W. He began
shooting when 13 years old. He was elected a
member of the M.S. W. Gun Club in ’93, and was
put on the 26yds. mark. Since then he has won
more trophies than any other member, and has
never drawn less than £5O out of an open handi-
cap. Last Dec. he killed 66 out of 69 birds off
29yds.; last April, 50 running off 30yds.; a week
later, another consecutive 50 off 31yds. ; in
Sept., 42 off 30yds., and the same day
54 out of 55 off 30yds. Last week he
eclipsed himself by winning N.S. Wales
Gun Club cup, with nine kills off 32yds., and by
directly afterwards killing 16
birds off 34yds. This last dis-
tance is the new club limit, and
Mr. Eales is the first man in Aus-
tralian pigeon - shooting to merit
the distinction of deserving it. Carver,
Brewer, and Clark, never shot in com-
petition behind 32yds. Another feature of Eales’s
last brilliant performance is that he deserved
every bird he brought down —there was no luck
in it —only hard, clean shooting. It has often
been said that every morning, at Duckenfield, Mr.
Eales kills his 25 birds in practice, but he denies
ever shooting at pigeons during the last six months
except in matches. He has considerable field-
shooting, however.
“Possum” Dean, the poisoner, had his
fortune told recently, and the fortune-teller’s in-
criminating document was found in his pocket
when he was arrested. He had received it the
same day, and it ran thus :
Reading from a photo, of George Dean, Esq. : —The face
shows you to be active, energetic and industrious. You
have the powers of grasping an idea quickly, and putting
it in lucid form. lam afraid your trouble is not yet over.
You are true hearted, and a very loyal friend. You will
succeed very well in life. Although you have had sorrow,
much brightness will yet come into your life, for you are
honorable, upright and brave in all your dealings. One
who would scorn to be guilty of a mean action either
for popularity or gain, who would respect your elders and
be courteous to all. You are fond of animals, and love
fresh air and exercise of games. You have a strong will,
and are extremely independent.
Had Dean been able to “grasp an idea quickly”
he would long since have grasped the idea that he
ought to be in Chili.
John Haynes is the most robust humorist
of N.S.W. Assembly. Long ago something was
said in debate about a bucket of unpleasantness
having been thrown over an individual to whom
the Govt, of the day had been making a grant or
giving a billet, or something. “ I remember the
occurrence well,” said Haynes, “ and it was nob
unearned ‘increment,’ either.” Only he didn’t
say increment.
Gov. Hopetoun used to twit Murray Smith
with his alliance with Max Hirsoh, the Single-
taxer. “ How can you, Mr. Smith,” the shaven
Gov. used to say, “ stand on the same platform as
a man who advocates robbery?” Smith used to
squirm and feel awkward. He says himself that
it made his relations with Hopetoun uncomfort-
able.

Interprovincial and country visitors wishing
to view the arrival of Lord and Lady Brassey
should secure rooms at the Beaconsfield Hotel,
St. Kilda, from the towers of which palatial
hostelry can be seen the St. Kilda fleet of yachts
waiting to welcome the new Governor of Victoria.
One thing is certain : the obliging hostess, Mias
Starr, enhances the surrounding beauties of
nature by providing for the most trivial wants of
her patrons.*

“WHEN THE STORM CAME.”
The Curse of Protection Again.
, “ Melbourne five or six years ago
was erecting buildings, offices, and
so on. THEN GAME THE FAIL-
URE OF THE BANKS, and fol-
lowing this the destruction of all
confidence and great falling off in
the volume of employment, although
the population remained the same.
Five years ago work was so plenti-
ful that in some trades the men
thought of reducing the hours of
labor from 8 to 7i per day. But
a very different state of affairs
existed now. I '—Graham Berry.
“The accurate statement of the
facts narrated by Sir Graham Berry
is that in the day of trouble, when
the BTOE M struck the colony,
the artificial and subsidised trades
failed miserably. When the test
came they proved to bo utterly
wanting.’’—" Argus’’ Comment.

Just Ice—N.S.W. Minister Gould.

preferable to the aristocratic ideas of the demo-
cracy.”

will have to retire for a time under pecuniary
■tress.
S.A. National Ass thinks of running
Symon, Q.C., against Wood, the weakest of the
Labor Ms.P., next April,

Sydney Daily Telegraph
So inextricably are politics interwoven with the adminis-
tration of justice.
But why should the Attorney-General be a politi*
cal officer ?
Dr. MacLaurin? M.L.C., opposes the Land-
tax in N.S.W. because the Maoriland tax, as he
asserts, costs .£30,000 a year in expenses of collec-
tion. As a matter of fact, the M.L. Land and
Income-taxes cost, at the highest estimate, about
iG17,000 a year for collection, including the charges
of the periodical assessment.
“ French vanity has been gratified by the capture of
the capital of Madagascar.”—Agk.
French vanity, our papers say,
Doth move the gods to wild surprise.
In English, though, this little trait
Of grabbing niggers’ country, they
Behold in their divine survey
As noble British enterprise.
“Chinaman” Dibbs: “How about the
Reid Chinese regime? Looking in the window
of a Sydney Chinese cabinetmaker’s, the writer
lately saw a Chinaman affixing addresses to furni-
ture for a N.S.W. Govt, charitable institution.
Have the suppliers a direct contract with the
State, or is some Govt, contractor getting a cheap
line by subletting to the Chinese firm ? ”
