If the minimum for N.S.W. Income-tax
be fixed at £l5O, it is unlikely, despite a general
opinion among M.sL.A. to the contrary, that
their Parliamentary allowance of £3OO will
remain untouched. Several members hold that
electioneering expenses, direct and indirect, when
deducted from the £3OO, will leave them still out-
side the taxation area. But if this plea be set up,
it will undoubtedly be taken to the courts and dis-
allowed. In Tasmania the Chief-Justice has ruled
that electioneering expenses cannot be allowed
against an Income-tax claim.
N.S.W. Justice Stephen’s original six
months’ leave on full pay expired on Aug. 12.
Does he still draw full pay? Judges’ liberties
and privileges appear to awe every class of Aus-
tralian politicians.
Taverner, Vic. Minister of Agriculture,
about the best-abused man in the Turner Ministry,
got level with his political antagonists the other
evening thus : “ When the Patterson Ministry
were in power they went about the country eating
turkeys; I export them.”
As Angus Cameron says, re the vaunted
N.S.W. Public Service Regulation Bill: “Ecclesi-
astical and social influence are as noxious as
political influence if allowed to affect appoint-
ments in the public service.” There are thousands
in the public service who owe nothing to the poli-
tician, but who came over the wall of “faith ” on
one side or eminent social intercession on the
other.
Of late, the uncompromising-single-tax
party has been little heard from. Two years
ago no newspaper-controversy ever started with-
out the single tax people chipping in and nointing
out that the remedy lay in Georgeism. Had the
Dean affair occurred a couple of years since, the
whole thing would have been settled by a refer-
ence to “Progress and Poverty,” signed “Yours
truly, W. E. Johnston,” or “Percy R. Meggy,”
or words to that effect.
It didn’t take long to thrust Garrick out
of Q. Agent-Generalship, in which Acting-A.-G.
Dicken is a mere warming-pan ; but Govt, hasn’t
yet appointed a successor to Judge Harding. The
obvious course is to remove Chubb or Cooper from
Townsville to Brisbane, and give Paul, or Noel,
or Miller a step up to a Supreme Court at Rock-
hampton. But it isn’t the course that appeals to
Kanakaland diplomacy. Besides, Minister
Philp’s Townsville seat is shaky now, and a lioness
robbed of her whelps is nothing to Townsville
robbed of an official.
N.S.W. Legislative Council, though it
may mutilate Reid’s direct taxation proposals,
presents no such hostile front to such measures as
it has done in the recent past. Ten years ago it
contemptuously threw out Jennings’ Bill for a
land-tax, two years later a similar Bill sent up by
the Parkes Government, and Dibbs’ Income-tax
Bill of a couple of years ago. On these occasions
it was impossible to get more than half the mem-
bers of the Council to attend. For the Parkes
Bill only seven voted, while for those of Jennings
and Dibbs only seven votes stand recorded.
Maoriland, like Victoria, has been wallow-
ing through the long, dismal job of revising the
tariff. The result isn’t satisfactory. A lot of the
members know something of one industry only.
A lot more have no practical knowledge of any
industry whatever. Sometimes a whole batch of
duties would be fixed when the only man who had
any practical knowledge of the subject was away,
and when the member who professed to know
about it, but who had got all his information
upside-down, was unhappily present. Then the
other man who thought lie knew about it also,
but who had got his information inside-out, would
arrive later on, and supply a lot more valuable
ignorance on the subject, only a different kind of
ignorance from the previous lot, and get the item
recommitted and altered, so that instead of being
bow-legged as it was before, it would become
knock-kneed instead. And when all the solicitors,
squatters, farmers, financial-agents, merchants,
and auctioneers had finished telling each other
about industries they had never seen, and about
machinery they had never heard of, they fixed up
a tariff which is about as good as might be ex-
pected under the circumstances.
South Australia
commenced a systematic
plan of planting and con-
serving forests 19 years
ago. The expenditure up
to date has been £129,000,
while the revenue has
been .£115,000, and the
forests now existing are
reckoned as being worth
<£loB,ooo. The deficit
was confined to the early
days of the system, and
now the annual revenue
exceeds the expenditure
by =£3ooo. N.S. Wales
has had a forest conserva-
tion system for 22 years,
and in 1893 (the last year
for which statistics are
available) the revenue of
the department was £9546
and the expenditure
.£19,459, salaries and
travelling expenses alone
coming to nearly£ 14,000!
When <£50,000 has been
‘ blewed ” in one year
for thinning, how on
eart/i will the account
stand ?
The anti-vaccination
movement is growing in
Victoria. In that pro-
vince vaccination is com-
pulsory; in N.S.W. it is
purely optional. The
result in 1892 was that
there were no deaths from
smallpox in Victoria,
while in N.S.W. there
was only one. Vaccina-
tion is a dirty and dan
gerous practice, which
often results in the trans-
mission of loathsome
diseases, and the results
of the compulsory system
apparently are not worth
the risk. It probably
prevents smallpox to a
considerable extent, and
therefore it was useful
and valuable in the in-
sanitary past; but small-
pox, like most other epi-
demics, arises mostly
from dirt and stupidity, and with a little atten’
tion to cleanliness it has become almost extinct.
Vaccination now-a-days seems to be a habit, or
a species of Joss, more than anything else.
A Bill now before Vic. Assembly authorises
women to vote, on the same basis as men, at elec-
tions for the Leg. Assembly only. The Council
is to be elected by male property franchise as
heretofore. This suggests the possibility of a
really great experiment. Let the Council be
elected by manhood suffrage, if such a reform can
be brought about, while the Assembly is elected
on manhood and womanhood suffrage. Then give
the two houses co-ordinate powers, and after that
let the person who is responsible for the experi-
ment conceal himself in the hills till he sees how it
is going to pan out.
Some one nearly broke up a Vic. political
banquet by handing in a bogus telegram to the
effect that the Melbourne market had collapsed,
burying many people in the ruins, and in its fall
had carried away the viaduct, thus causing the
wreck of a passenger-train and horrible carnage.
Another train had fallen into the gap on top of
the first one, and its boiler had burst, and then
the ruins had taken fire, and a sickening odor of
burning passenger had spread all over the city.
Unluckily, the hoax was discovered, so this well-
meant effort to shut off the usual dreary speech
about the great future of Victoria failed of its
purpose.
A cheeky British person named Evans,
who collects stamps, has lodged a complaint with
Chamberlain, Secretary of State for the Colonies,
that Postmaster-General Cook is reprinting old
N.S.W. stamps and selling them. Therefore the
market is becoming over-supplied with ancient
N.S.W. stamps, and the value of specimens,
which were once rare and valuable, is seriously
diminished. P.M.G. Cook tersely replies that he
is doing so for the good of the N.S.W. revenue,
and apparently intends to continue at the work.
Consequently the British person named Evans
will have to simmer down. N.S.W. can go on
issuing its own stamps as long as it pleases.
Not one of the Freetrade-party in Vic.
Assembly holds his principles consistently. Every
man of them makes an exception in favor of some
industry carried on in his own constituency.
Frank Madden (Eastern Suburbs) is a Freetrader
except as to pipeclay ; Bowen (Wangaratta) seeks
special protection for Victorian-grown tobacco;
Mackenzie (Broadford) advocates protection on
strawboard ; Le.vien (Barwon) tries to protect fish
and bananas; Squatter Staughton (West Bourke)
advocates stiff duties on explosives ; J. S. White
(Albert Park) goes in for protecting hat-nets ; and
so on. The Victorian Freetrader would clap 25
per cent, on Sin if the Devil had a factory in his
electorate.
“A distinguished member of the bar in
a neighboring province—a gentleman who has
held the highest official rank in that province”
(Madden? Way? Griffith?) —“has written com-
plimenting Salomons, Q.C., and expressing the
opinion that he was fully within his professional
rights in divulging the statement made to him by
attorney Meagher re Dean. “ I was so sorry to
see the Commission granted in the first instance,
but your brave conduct and the resolute action of
Mr. Want in backing you up have brought good
out of evil.” And there you have the cat out of
the bag ! All the lawyers are so sorry that the
eccentricities of “ legal ” justice should have been
reviewed by laymen. Hence many things.

far the best of the bunch. Meagher was a Protec-
tionist, and that practically settles the case. That
fallen solicitor might have done one good turn to
his party by joining the Reid crowd when he felt
the earth slipping beneath his feet; then he would

mental attitude of many voters is simply : “Pro-
tection ain’t no good ; look at Meagher ! ”
Another Redmond Barry story. A sailor
speaking very low in court was told he would be
committed for contempt if he didn’t speak up.
Sailor responded in speaking - trumpet tones.
Barry, thinking he was being guyed, committed
the sailor forthwith. It took counsel a full quarter-
hour to smooth Barry’s ruffled dignity and explain
that sailors’ voices have no middle-register.
Visitors to the Spouting Spring at Heli-
don, 70 miles from Brisbane, are always astounded
at the quantity of water they can consume at the
Spring. Ten or 12 tumblers in an hour or so are
taken with comfort. The reason is that the
tissues of the body are absorbing the water all the
time. Something beneficial is always added to
the system by every glass of Helidon Spa water
consumed. Messrs. Tooth and Co., Kent
Brewery, are the Sydney agents.
In these days, when people in Sydney a*e
positively pestered with photographers advertising
by means of phrases well calculated to startle and
to attract those who run as they read, it is well to
remember that, after all, the value of what is
artistic is certainly not gauged by quantity or
measurement, and, as photographers should be
possessed of artistic merit, their true value depends
on the quality of their work. At the present
juncture, Sydney is highly favored, as we see
photographic galleries charging rates for work of
a class which, in any city of the old world, would
command a price double that charged here. The
work of several firms, notably Charlemont and
Co., of George-street, Sydney, is known and
highly appreciated all over Australasia. The
prices charged are such as will certainly cause to
be distributed through the community photo-
graphs of a high order of merit. Charlemont and
Co.’s rooms and studio are all that is to be
desired, and make easy the despatch of a large

More rushes in N.W. Westralia. A Chinaman
having reported he had found 900 oz. gold on the Annesley
Ranges, great excitement followed. It turned out that
John had three horses which he wanted to sell; he
thought that by causing a rush he could force a deal. He
managed the deal—but, at latest, they were looking for
that yellow man.
Another rush out West followed on a spinifex
squatter reporting that one of his niggers had found 2dwt.
gold. When they asked the nig. where he had got it
he told the diggers that his master gib it to him and to
tellim white pfeller you find him alonga bii“h. That
spinifex squatter had a lot of old ewes, and charged 15s.
a head for them while the rush lasted.
* * * *
Two prospectors named Northmore and Doolette
report a sensational find near Niagara, in the Coolgardie
district. From a small surface-trench they took out
200 oz. of gold, one piece weighing 9oz. The formation is
ironstone, with quartz and schist intermingled.
* • • *
A practical miner, once at Hillgrove, writes
from Bardoc, W.A. : “I am working in the Excelsior
mine, which is a fine battery show. Two reefs, one 3ft.
an I the other 18in., both show gold freely. A trial-crush-
ing from the big reef went 7oz. and one from the small
reef 140 z., and, in each case, I know the stone was taken
just as it was broken down. These reefs, tapped at 100 ft.
level, are as good there as at surface. Machinery is on
the road. Rosstrevor, who also used to work in Baker’s
Creek, has likewise got a very good show.”

The Tasmania G.M. Co. pays Tas. Govt. Geo-
logist Montgomerie £250 for his report on its property.
Dally Bros. (5), original owners of the great
Tasmania mine, after sluicing the decomposed portion of
the reef and grassing all intruding solid quartz, sold out
for £5OOO and an interest. The ore won by the Daliys
yielded £BOOO to the purchasing co. This, the co.’s first
streak of luck, was followed by the cheap acquisition of
the Golden Gate and Florence Nightingale, two rich West
and adjoining sections. After_ this, on the East, the
Lefroy, whose crosscut was within three feet of the reef,
sickened, and was taken over by the boss 00. Also
Daily’s United (now a valuable property) fell into its
hands ; and last, not least, the Phcenix, with a big lump
of underlay, which she was too poor to reach, amalga-
mated. Further, the Tasmania leases cover all alluvial.
The Tasmania is a pie-biter, and the bigger the pie the
more it's hers. William Dally, discoverer, holds no
interest. Also some of his brothers have parted. Such
is the luck of the co.—which, so far as is known, has
every bit of the reef.

Dear Bulletin, — “Tag. miners do not love
John. They had enough of him on the E. Coast tin-
fields,” &c. Your remarks imply censure—not all
deserved. John ousted the tin-miner by organisation
and dogged perseverance. He worked hard for his
money, but had to go without credit for his virtues
because he had vices and was yellow. True, John smoked
opium and played fanian and ran lotteries day and night,
but there was method always in his madness. He never
neglected his work. He didn’t work spasmodically for a
fortnight, and then drink and wallow in all manner of
filth for the balance of the month, and go back to his
camp to inveigh against every man not out on strike
while he was on the spree. No ;he fed well, supported a
large percentage of non-producing Chows, paid tribute to
the Europeans, and had money to spare 1 Honor to him,
though we “chuck him out, the brute.”—Yours in the
great crush, Theta.

Castlemaine (Vic.) has been a most disappoint-
ing country for the quartz miner. Reefs which shed the
gold that made Forest Creek famous have not yet been
found. Amongst the many theories advanced to account
for this is the one that possibly the hill-tops, now denuded
and worn away, were immensely rich and full of leaders
that did not live down.
* » * *
The wire-rope question is troubling the Northern
miner. Defective ropes have recently caused several acci-
dents. It is said that the iron or steel crystallises as a
result of rapid or continuous bending. This process takes
place particularly at the portion of the rope just clear of
the pulley and at the shoe—especially in shafts using
buckets. It is suggested that ten or fifteen feet be cut off
the rope every few months —thus removing the most
defective portions.

Dear Bulletin, —I am a New England selector
and do more than a bit in shooting wallabies and trap-
ping opossums. There is something rotten about certain
of the salesmen-agents in Sydney. The prices quoted in
the papers are merely delusive to people like me. There
are no thicker-furred ’possums in N.S.W. than up here in
this severe climate. ’Possums here don’t fool about,
sitting in the forks of trees. Consequently, “rumpers”
are few. But they abound in our sale-accounts. Last lob
of 'possums I sent in—some hundreds—l culled severely.
Everything which could possibly be fancied to be
“rumped” I kept back, and tanned for rugs for own
use. My account-sales came along all the same. Fifty
rumpers—exactly 50, mark you—at less than Is. per doz.
Top market quotation, 10s. per doz. Of my lot, a re-
markably even parcel, less than 50 fetched Bs. lid. per
doz., then so many 6s. 9d., and so on in batches of 70, 30,
5, &c.— so very exact, you see, down to 3s. per doz.
Then I sent, say, 50 wallaby skins. Got an account sales
of 50 skins right enough. Top market quotation, 18s. per
doz. at the time. Half-a-dozen, say, of mine “fetched ”
15s. 6d. per doz., and little batches down to 2d. each. One
excellent feature is that a couple of doz., say, are set down
at nominal prices as being “ shot-holed.” Singular, con-
sidering that I don’t use a shot-gun. Every wallaby was
shot by me with a rifle! Per contra, I didn’t send a
single kangaroo or wallaroo skin, that lot, but I got a
return-sales for just my right number of wallabies, divided
into kangaroos and wallaroos, at bigger prices than
wallaby skins would bring. Then, again, I am
charged “rail” expenses for a parcel sent by sea.
I have tried two different firms, and my returns
have always been flagrant humbug and glaringly
“faked.” But what am I to dot Selectors are
completely at the mercy of these auctioneer-agents.
The direct commission charges they make are but 5 per
cent. Nothing can be clearer on the face of some of my
accounts rendered for small batches than that these
ostensible charges don’t pay for the clerical work of
making out the documents. I tell you that according to
my experience there is crooked work in the alleged prices
which the skins fetch. If ’possum-skins are quoted in
reports furnished to the papers by Sydney selling
agents at 10s. 6d. for top-greys—the best sort—and 1 send
in a picked lot of 50doz. of the finest greys that New Eng-
land produces, pegged out in tip-top style, all trapped
and not a shot-hole in a skin, and every skin the least
shade thin-furred in any part thrown out when making
up the parcel, I am lucky if I receive ss. per doz. on the
all-round average for the lot. My name is Smith, and my
address Snooks’s Place. 1 daren’t give my real name,
lest every one of my next batch be rumpers and shot-
holed.—Smith.
George Dibbs is the first N.S.W. ex-
Premier to take a subordinate post under Govt.
One, James Martin, took a Chief-Justiceship; and
two, Charles Cowper and William Forster,
became Agents-General, but although the Agent-
Generalship is in the Civil Service, the three ex-
Premiers named passed on to positions of respon-
sibility and influence rather above than below that
of a temporary Premiership. This shows the
natural modesty of Dibbs, or proves the truth of
the old adage that “ needs must when the devil
drives.”
The boyish-looking Earl of Shaftesbury,
one of Brassey’s aidekongs, is a grandson of the
noted philanthropist of that ilk. One of the most
touching episodes in the life of the latter was
when the Oovent Garden costers, overflowing
with gratitude at the efforts of his lordship to
ameliorate their lot, subscribed among themselves
and presented him with —a donkey, flhe youthful
earl should feel a responsive thrill when he visits
Sydney Zoo and sees the donkey that cost the
N.S.W. taxpayer £300,000.
It is a somewhat startling fact that Mr.
Darley, in his dual capacity of N.S.W, Chief
Justice and Acting-Governor, draws the third
highest amount in the way of salary among
officials, excluding royalties, in the entire British
empire. The highest salary is that of the Gov.-
General of India, £25,000; the Lord-Lieut. of
Ireland takes £20,000 ; then come the English
ambassador at Paris with £lO,OOO, and the Lord
Chancellor with £6OOO for his chancellorship and
£4OOO for Speakership of the Lords. There are
other salaries of £lO,OOO ; but, with the exception
of the two named, the little N.S.W. potentate
leads the empire.
Electrocution having proved unsatisfactory
in U.S.A., it is now proposer! to try the lethal
chamber. While the condemned is sleeping, after
a secretly-administered tasteless opiate, his room
will be quietly filled with carbonic acid gas, and
he will painlessly pass from life.
A rising Australian poet (not Sladen), who
had allowed his hair to grow en voyage, and whose
pockets bulged with MSS., waited on editor Ash-
down, of Black and White, and, after presenting
his card, proceeded to read one of his little things.
Ashdown, who stands about 74in., and is thin in
proportion, gradually reared himself up to his
full height, like a hop-pole, or an anaconda
balancing himself on the tip of his tail. “Would
you mind if I turn you out? ” said the lanky one,
suavely, as he elongated himself. Whereupon
the wild-eyed poet fled, leaving his MS. behind.

Julyan, will be a godsend to the famous loanster.

nection with the N.Y. Life. The new appoint-

London, in return for certain services rendered by
Julius, and at the urgent request of Baron
Schroeder and other big men on ’Change.
When in office as Chief Secretary, Alfred
Deakin used to lunch regularly with his econo-
mical colleague, the late Prof. Pearson, at a six-
penny restaurant in Little Collins-street, where
lentii-soup was a specialty. John Nirnmo, when
Vie. Works Minister, generally carried his lunch
to the office in his pocket, and might have been
seen about noon alternately carving a slice of
bread and a piece of meat, or a raw onion, with a
pocket-knife, and washing it (the food, not the
pocket-knife) down with a draught of cold water.
Nirnmo gave a lot to public charities, including a
heap of good advice and a fund of blessings, not
to mention several volumes of departmental litera-
ture for which he had no further use. Gillies
lunched at the Athenaeum sumptuously, and
seldom dined without fizz. Munro, in his best
days, lunched at the full-and-plenty table of one
of his own coffee-palaces.
A good many Melbourne people didn’t
know that Sir Righteous Wrixon was an Irishman
until (for his piety is verra Scawtish) he took
the chair at Mrs. Andrews’ recent lecture on
“ Woman’s Revolt. ” When Dr. Pat. Moloney,
proposing a vote of thanks to the chairman in a
long, discursive speech, had called him the finest
lawyer and the noblest statesman under Heaven,
there was no question as to Wrixon’s nationality.
This was clearly a case of one Irishman but
tering another Irishman. Wrixon felt called
upon to suggest that his admiring “fellow
countryman ” had piled it on rather too thick,
though, indeed, Dr. Pat. hadn’t said much more
than the average Scotchman says of a brither
Scot on any similar occasion. When will Eng-
lishmen start a mutual-admiration society for
offensive and defensive purposes ?

JGOOO. Moreover, there is a great deal in a name,
Juliet to the contrary notwithstanding, and that
of Perceval, recalling a tragical historic event, has
a vaguely-aristooratio association.
Mrs. “ Robert Elsmere ” Ward was born
a Sorell, and her Tasmanian relations used to say
she had the Sorell semper. Once her parents sent
her on a short visit to her godfather, then Arch-
deacon Reibey (now Tas. M.H.A.), and there
came a morning when the future novelist was very
naughty. “You mustn’t make faces at the
table,” she was told. “Then I’ll go out and
make faces at the juniper tree,” she threatened.
“Very well,” said the archdeacon ; “but if you do
the tree will throw water over you.” “It won’t,”
said the child, and ran away to carry out her
threat, while a housemaid, by her godfather’s
instructions, set out with a bucket of water by a
roundabout path to hide behind the tree. The
little girl made faces at the tree to her heart’s
content, and turned to go, when souse came the
water over her. She ran for her life to the house,
but meeting the archdeacon she stopped and
clenched her little fist, and stamped her little
foot at him, with “ Godpapa, I hate you ! ” But
when that child grew up she had her revenge.
Souse came a bucket of water over Archdeacon
Reibey’s church, which in its turn stamped an
indignant foot and said, “Mrs. Humphry Ward,
we hate you.”
When the question of Deeming’s sanity
was raised by attorney Lyle, Dr. Shields, the Vic.
Govt, medical officer, who has recently been much
in evidence over the Abrahams case, was deputed to
make tests as to the soundness of the murderer’s
mind. The doctor, notwithstanding his long asso-
ciation with convicts, and in despite of the Italian
proverb, “Five doctors, four atheists,” is most
pronouncedly orthodox, and frequently administers
to the soul of the criminal as well as to his body.
“What do you think of the Ten Command-
ments? ” was his first question to Deeming, when
probing for a weakness in the murderer’s intellect.
“Not much,” said Deeming, philosophically.
“You see, Moses lived a long time ago, and
catered for a very different class of people from
what are on the earth to-day. We require a new
order of fiction now ; though, I believe, if Moses
had lived in these times, he would have been found
equal to modern requirements as a writer.”
“ Don’t you think it wrong to rob your neighbor ? ”
was the doctor’s next question. “Well, it all
depends,” said Deeming. “If my neighbor
happens to have something which he doesn’t want,
and which I do want, where is the harm of my
taking it from him?” “Don’t you think it
wrong to take life?” he was then asked. “You
don’t mean to say that you have murdered any-
body?” he enquired, looking the doctor pity-
ingly in the face. “If you have done anything
like that, I am afraid you are beyond any
power of mine for good. Truly, murder is a
terrible thing.” Shields, whose amiable placidity
was proof even against this impertinence, merely
rejoined, “ Well, sir, you are a philosopher, what-
ever else you may be.” That afternoon the doctor
reported to the Executive Council that the mur-
derer was perfectly sane.

Sixty percent, of all telegraph operators die
of some disease of the respiratory organs. Of these
three-fourths —or nearly 50 per cent, of the whole
profession—die of phthisis, otherwise known as
consumption or tuberculosis of the lungs.
Melb. University Medical Museum has the
contents of the stomach of a lunatic with ostrich
like tastes, including a clay-pipe, an iron dinner-
fork, some nails, pebbles, and an assortment ot
hair. Another human ostrich lately swallowed an
iron L-shaped gate-bolt, with a six-inch shaft and
Ifin. handle-part.

Warra : In the gully by the creek lay the
carcase of an old mare. Not far off stood a lean,
lank foal. Leading from the carcase to the top of
the range was a track of beaten mud and under-
growth. The mare, exhausted by the drought and
her natural efforts to preserve the equine race, the
last straw being the cold weather, had fallen on
the ridge. Then began the vain struggle tor life,
the alow agony of death—down, down, down—
the foal following the progress, which lasted maybe
a fortnight, and terminated at the creek. Yet,
knowing the horror of the horse’s last days, there
are men who, to save themselves the thrill of
shooting an old favorite, “ turn him out to die 1 ”
It is said, “ He giveth his beloved sleep,” The
noble horse is evidently little loved, for his sleep
is long, long a-coming.
Melbourne women almost universally wear
their boots and shoes much narrower in the tread,
and an inch or an inch and a-quarter higher in
the heel than Sydney women do. The boot-trade
has a Melbourne last and a Sydney last, which
are altogether different. The Sydney taste doesn’t
differ from the Melbourne in color, but Melbourne
is the home of narrow tread and high heel. At
present the Sydney woman’s heel is from lin. to
the Melbourne woman’s from 2in. to 2|in.
If a bootman put the Melb. boot on the Sydney
market ruin would follow, just as it would if he
put the Sydney boot on the Melb. market. The
boot-authorities point out that Sydney’s hills are
probably the cause of the local wide tread and low
heel, while Melbourne’s flatness admits the narrow
tread and high-heel boot with comfort. But it is
not that. It is on account of the rivalry between
the two cities. Melbourne must distinguish itself
from Sydney, even if it is done with corns and
bunions and the sad ingrowing nail.
Most of the London publishers are deluged
with literary productions from “ the colonies.”
The success of Rolf Boldrewood and a few others
created a temporary boom in Australian stories
and sketches, but it soon died out. The method
adopted by some of the editors of London maga-
zines and weekly papers in dealing with “colonial”
MSS. is peculiar. They divide them into three
classes those written by authors who have
made a reputation, those written by men whose
names are less known, and those written by
others, the majority, whose names are not
known at all. This is the order in which the
MSS. are read by the editors, so that it natur-
ally happens that obscure writers stand hardly
any chance at all. As a London editor put it,
“ It is the brand on the blacking” that takes with
the public. Marriott Watson almost starved for
a year before “ The Web of the Spider ’’made a
lucky hit. “Iota” (Mrs. Caffyn) received but a
trifle for her first book, and Fergus Hume only
makes a moderate income.
Editor Bulletin, —It is charged against
Australian fiction that it smells of the charnel-
house, and delights in abattoir adjectives ; that it
revels in the morbid and sanguinary, finding no
story complete without the services of an under-
taker. This chamber of horrors is becoming only
less wearisome than the inevitable wedding-bells
and half-a-dozen brats that are found on the last
page of the British-made article. It was the cus-
tom of ancient builders to place under their
monuments a holocaust of slain enemies ; perhaps
the edifice of our literature is being built on a
foundation of “stiffs,” tuck-pointed with blood
and tears. But surely the morgue must have
limits, the day of slaughter must be near its end ;
and if we cannot hold a concert, then why not a
wake on the poor lamented defunct? When
writers have wiped their eyes dry over the graves
of the bushman, the swagman, and the prospector,
they may find that human interest can be aroused
with many other chords than those of the requiem;
which is only a minor air in the vast range of
melodies open to their choice. We want, by pre-
ference, the history of the real men about us ; who
neither die in the bush, make sudden fortunes, or
pass unchequered lives with their wives. Men
who finally die in their beds, much to the relief of
the sorrowing family which quarrels over the
insurance-money. And, above all, with the
tragedy of life so present to us, we want in our
reading moments to find in fiction what is so often
absent in fact—a little comedy and joy.—H.F.
J, H. Witheford, having floated several
mining go’s in the London market, thought it
would be easy to induce the Admiralty to establish
naval workshops in Auckland (M.L.) harbour.
With some trouble he obtained an introduction to
the First Lord, but was told that he would be
fired out when the interview had lasted exactly
3min. by the clock. He put on a top hat and a
frock coat, stuck a bunch of primroses in his
button-hole, handed in his pasteboard, and was
ushered into a gorgeous apartment where the
exalted personage sat at a huge table. The Auck-
lander, a man gifted with some cheek and readi-
ness of resource, walked briskly up to the great
man, seized his hand, wrung it with the vigor of a
blacksmith, and asked him in a cheery way how
he was. My lord turned pale and stared through
his eye-glass at the eccentric visitor, w ho proceeded
to quote voluminous reports, stacks of figures,
and newspaper clippings in support of Auckland’s
claims to be made the naval station of the South
Pacific. My lord held up a deprecatory hand,
but this merely acted as a stimulus to Witheford’s
eloquence. When at length he paused for breath
his lordship drawled out: “ The only thing I
know about New Zealand is that you have bor-
rowed a great deal too much money.” For a
single instant Witheford was taken aback, but,
recovering his presence of mind, he again grabbed
his lordship’s hand, and exclaimed, in a tone of
fervent admiration: “Right you are. But as I
was about to remark when you interrupted me ”
He got no further. Melud made a convulsive
dart at the electric button, and was saved by the
prompt entrance ot his secretary, who showed the
visitor out.

THE RABBIT PEST, AND HOW TO
GRAPPLE WITH IT.
In dealing with this question, the ingenuity of
thousands of our colonists has been exerted to
find some means of stamping out the rabbit
plague. Fencing, trapping, poisoning, all have
been employed to arrest the increase of this pest,
but unfortunately the plague still makes progress,
and is surely extending through Queensland in
spite of all efforts to arrest the advance. Few
people realise the enormous annual outlay of land-
holders in checking the increase of rabbits. The
sum spent every year exceeds .£1)00,000 sterling.
New Zealand is the only colony so far that has
thoroughly reduced the plague. The new poison
employed there, called “ TOXA,” is the agent
through which bunny is destroyed by the million,
and tlie New Zealander through it has practically
made rabbit-destruction a paying business. The
action of this poison is instantaneous. Once a
rabbit touches it he rarely runs 20 yards, and all
the tkius are secured, which more than defray the
lal or and cost of the poison. The National Wool
,C< mp ny of Melbourne has introduced the poison
to Australia, and, where tried, it hasgiven excellent
results ; so much so that the company has decided
to manufacture in Melbourne, in order to reduce
the price of the poison as low as possible.*

ONLY FOUR TO MAN THE PUMPS.
Dear, dear I When you come to think of it how
closely related things are ; how one thing brings
up another. Ideas are like a lot of beads on a
string, aren’t they ?
A letter I have just been reading makes me re-
member what happened to me one winter about
twenty years ago. The story is too long to tell
here, so 111 merely give you the tail-end of it. I
was supercargo on a bark bound from London to
Rio. -v tremendous gale, lasting five days,
wrecked us. Forty-eight hours after it ceased
there j* ere four men and no more loft on the
vessel. The captain had been killed by a falling
► pa , three of the crow washed overboard, and the
rest of the -hips company (save us four) went
away in the long-boat with the first and second
mates. We were taking in water through a leak
at the rate of six im hes an hour. Working with
all our might the four of us could pump that out
in forty minutes, but we must do it every hour.
It was awful wo<-k. For two days we kept it up,
without sleep Then we stopped, took io the
quarter boat and shoved off. The sea was quiet
—no wind. While we lay to within a mile of her
the ship threw up her nose and went down stern
first. We were picked up the next day by a
Danish b ig.
Now the odd thing is that the letter that re-
minded me of that experience has nothing what-
ever to say about ships. Please help me to find
out the association.
The lady who writes the letter says that in
July, 1881, she got a bad fright. Exac ly what it
was she doesn t tell. I wish she did. Anyway it
bo upset her that she didn’r get over the effects of
it for nine years. After that her appetite fell off;
she lost all real relish for food, and what she did
eat only made ti ouble instead of nourishing her.
j t gave her pain in the pit of the stomach and
(curiously enough) between the shoulders. She
says her eyes and skin presently turned yellow as
a butce cup. Her face and abdomen swelled, and
her feet the same, the latter so much so that she
was obliged to have her shoes made larger.
“ I got little sleep at night,” she says, “ and was
in so much pain 1 hid to be propped up with pil-
lows. For weeks together 1 could not lie down in
bed. I had a dry, hollow cough, and bad night
sweats. Then diarrhoea set in and my bowels
became ulcerated I was often in dreadful agony
for forty-eight hours at a time. Then I would
have a chill as hough a bucket of cold water were
poured down my back. I KOt so low I could no
longer sew, knit, or do any housework or look
after my children. My siste’ - had to come and
help in the house.
“ Everybody said I was in ™ decline and must
die. What I suffered for eight years tongue
cannot tell. The doctor could do nothing for me.
He said my complaint was complicated and bad to
deal with. In 1886 I wen tas an outdoor pa 1 lent
to the Shrewsbury Infl mary, but only got
uansient relief.”
The writer is in gooa health now, but why did
her case remind me of the shipwreck ? Let’s settle
that first. The a-sociation is easy and natural.
Just see. The ship rank because we four men
hadn’t the stiength to pump out the water as fast
as i. came in. twenty men might have git her
into port. It is the last straw that breaks the
c imel s back ; the last unsupplied need Lira makes
poverty abject and desperate.
These bodies of ours carry the seeds of disease
with them all the time —chiefly the poisons created
by imper ect digestion, made worse by ca eless
habits. But as long as nothing extraordinary
happens we manage to scrape along in a half-and-
half sort of fashion. Yet we’ve got in our blood
the stuff that any of a dozen diseases is made of,
only waiting for something to set it afire. While
the liver, kidneys, and lungs and skin keep us
fairly free —that is, don’t let the load get too heavy
—we say, “Oh, yes, I’m tolerably well, thank
you.” Little pains and unpleasant symptoms
bother us now and then, but we don’t fancy they
mean anything.
By-and-bye something happens. A cold, too
hearty a meal, a night of dissipation, an affliction
through death or loss of property, a fright, as in
Mrs. Bunco’s case, &c. Over we go. The last
straw has crushed us. One loose spark has blown
up the barrel of powder. The crew is too small to
save the ship. The kidneys, liver, skin, and
stomach strike work, and we must have help right
away or perish. All of which means the explosion
of latent indigestion and dyspepsia poisons in the
blood.
There! isn’t it plain why I thought of the ship ?
Now for the conclusion of the lady’s story. She
says : “In 1889 I first. heard of Mother Seigel’s
Curative Syrup. Half a bottle made mo feel
better, and by keeping on taking it I was soon
strong and well as ever. (Signed) Mrs. Ann
Bunce, The Park, Worthen, near Shrewsbury,
February 22nd, 1893.”
If there were only a way to save sinking ships
as certain and trustworthy as Mother Seigel’s
medicine is in the case of sinking human bodies,
what a blessing it would be to poor sailors.*
Colonel Macdonald.
Cor,. Malcolm Macdonald, who is 76 years old,
over 6ft. high, straight as a pine, and in appearance
the beau-ideal of a soldier, received liis first com-
mission in ’37 and spent 15 years in India, where
he commanded a regiment of native irregular
cavalry. In ’57, during the Mutiny, he came to
Sydney to buy horses, got married and stayed
here. For 30 years he was the most active Sydney
patron of swordsmen, fencers, boxers, cricketers,
and athletes in general, and when he contributed to
Sydney Punch, that paper characterised the “noble
art” as the “nurse of manliness.” During those
years he had a boxing or fencing or broadsword
bout with every champion who visited Sydney.
In ’6O, at the Victoria Theatre, lie met Parker
(the Sergt. G°orge of “Bleak House”), a Life-
Guards’corporal, with blunt cavalry swords. Each
swordsman’s only protection was a helmet, and
the prospect of deadly thrust or broken bones
caused the excited crowd, after £290 had been
taken at the doors, to surge over the doorkeepers
and overflow into the building. Parker (who was
afterwards killed by falling from his horse in
Photo. by Kerry.
COLONEL MALCOLM MACDONALD.
Gippsland) received the worst punisliment, but on
points the Colonel let him win. When Mace
came to Australia, he brought with him the
champion swordsman of America, but the Colonel
beat him easily. Foley, who recently beat Major-
General Hutton’s best swordsman, owes his skill
in no small degree to the Colonel’s teaching.
» # »
Black Perry, who introduced the blow on the
point of the jaw, was the most wonderful boxer
the Colonel ever met. A Philadelphian black,
6ft. 2in. high, he would have been champion of
England had not the opposing crowd given him a
dummy £lO-note, then laid information against
him for passing it, and got him “ lagged ” for life
to Botany Bay. Perry, as a ticket-of-leavo man,
was for many years in the Colonel’s service.
There was heaps of fighting in those days (before
and after ’GO), but Perry was easily champion.
He met Huff, the Windsor giant (6ft, 6in.), and
“ cooked his goose ”in a very few rounds. Perry
was a scienced hurricane, and when in battle he
would cow many men with his horrible grimaces.
# * #
Foley, when the Colonel first knew him, was a
bricklayer, working for Sir John Young at the
erection of the old Exhibition Building, Redfern
Park, Sydney. After his work he would come
across to the Colonel’s house in Elizabeth-street —
that house has long been demolished, and the site
is now occupied by Toohey’s brewery. The
Colonel taught Foley the noble art —“ Foley was
never a hard hitter, but he was a most accurate
judge of distance and a wonderful counterer.”
It was Mace, the Gipsy, who could neither read nor
write yet made a “ book;” whose sharp, fixed eye,
strong as the eagle’s, preserved his face from even
one blow in 14 battles, who finished Foley’s
education. The Colonel never saw Foley in any
of his big battles —the one fight of Foley’s that he
saw was an impromptu affair for not more than
a fiver a side. No one dreamt then of Foley’s
White Horse Hotel in George-street, and of
Foley’s £5OOO a year income from his boxing-hall.
* # •
Mace received £lOOO from Wilson’s Circus
for five weeks of sparring during the interval.
It was there that Foley first showed as a scientist.
* m m
Concerning fighting, the old Colonel says
“We must have something to try the muscles of
men’s bodies and the endurance of their hearts.
We have also to fight those who won’t live quietly
until we have thrashed them. Of course, the
world would be better without any fighting, but
then it would not be our world. Still, no man has
a right to indulge in the prize-fight unless he is
prepared to get his face battered and to swallow
half-a-dozen teeth or so. Prize-fighting is prize-
fighting.” Terrible punisliment was given and
taken by the old-timers. A man would come back
to his corner with the red of his lower lip hanging
from the corner of his mouth, and the second would
bite the loose piece off and send his man uja again.
The hollow-chested, long-necked, peak-nosed Lang-
ham. whom no man could strike without being
struck back, blinded Sayers in a few rounds, and
Sayers (with his one sound arm) blinded Heenan.
The Colonel was referee at the Dawson-Burge
contest, when Dawson cut Burge’s face into
ribbons—yet the iron Burge was out next day
enjoying the sun and air. The Colonel also acted as
referee in the Placke-Barker fight, in the presence
of Sergeant Brennan of the N.S. W. police.


Vie. Artists’ Exhibition.
The Victorian Artists’ Exhibition this year, as
of yore, is held under the disadvantage of a big
debt, which sits with soulless and sordid
obstinacy upon the society’s chest. Somehow,
it seems to have cast its baleful influence over
the artists also. The work generally gives the
impression of having been done with the bailiff
in the house; and the landscape coloring, for
the most part, represents Nature as viewed
with jaundiced eyes through pale-brown spec-
tacles. A sickly affectation of a vague some-
thing which never was on land or sea pervades
the large majority of pictures on the line. They
are all of them on the line. The works that
might have been “ skied ” were mercifully re-
fused admission. Furthermore, there are alto-
gether too many studies from still-life —flowers,
boiled prawns, and other class-room subjects.
If these were weeded out, the residue might
make a tolerable show. Of landscapes, there
is a large assortment. David Davies makes
the most ambitious bid for fame in “The
Season of Storm and Stress and Toil,” a
large, ploughed field, tinted with the juice
of stewed prunes; plough, horses, and driver
in the middle of it; a straight, flat wall of
heavy paint standing for a sky background.
The picture is far more suggestive than in-
spiring, and the artist is better represented by
less ambitious work elsewhere. Withers has
got a good result in “ Tranquil Winter,” and
Tucker’s “ The Lover’s House,” a moonshiny
bit, is full of atmosphere and charm—a har-
monious picture. “The Last Watch,” a sea-
scape, by George Pontin, representing two
shipwrecked sailors, the chief figure badly
drawn, on a raft in a wilderness of water, is an
unconvincing attempt at realism, but another
Pontin effort in the North Gallery shows more
merit. Mather’s “Mount Mimda” deserves
admiration, despite its chromo-lithograph style.
• * •
There are not many life pieces, chiefly owing
to the fact that the local artists are too poverty-
stricken to hire the requisite models ; and,
somehow, they can’t, as in Paris, be got for
love, whether because the artists are not attrac-
tive enough, or for other reasons, The Bulletin
has not yet discovered. Tudor Tucker’s por-
trait of one of his pupils is strong, graceful, and
alive ; also, E. P. Fox’s portrait of himself, and
the little girl figure in his “ Art Students,” are
pleasing bits of painting. Bernard Hall takes
the palm for technical skill with his “Portrait
of a Lady,” a small-sized study of feminine
wearing-apparel and costume accessories, with
an unassuming wearer included in the scheme.
* * *
The water-colors are for the most part better
than the oils, and a pastel sketch by E. P. Fox
is, perhaps, the gem of the collection. There
are not a score of black-and-white drawings on
the walls, none of them especially worthy of
note. The etching of Mather’s “ Bathing-place
on the Watts ” is conspicuous as the only bit of
nude work in the collection.
Miss Bessie Way, daughter of Dr. Way
and niece of the S.a. Limit.-Gov., has been
appointed matron of the hospital at Hannan’s.
She was one of the nurses of “the better class”
whom Premier Kingston charged the Adelaide
hospital board with unfairly promoting.
“ Cecil John Wilson” (really Cecil Long,
a member of a highly-respectable English family)
has been sentenced to death at Sydney for
having induced to take poison one Edwin Nelson
Hampson, whom he then pushed over a cliff.
Hampson miraculously escaped death, and Wil-
son, who held .£5OO insurance on his intended
victim’s life, was arrested. The defence was
insanity, prisoner having been an inmate of several
English asylums. Which all goes to show that
there may be much method in madness.
It is now within a few months of 30 years
since Julian Salomons made his celebrated tech-
nical defence of murderer Bertrand. On Ber-
trand’s first trial the jury disagreed ; on the second
trial, before the same udge (the same counsel
being engaged), evidence, by counsel's consent,
was admitted from the Judge’s notes of the first
trial. Bertrand was convicted and sentenced to
death, and was to have been hanged on the same
day as bushranger Dunn 16th March, 1866.
Here Salomons (who had held no brief at either
trial) stepped in and moved the Full Court
on the ground that the notes of the Judge on the
first trial were not good evidence on the second.
The Judges were equally divided on the question,
which was eventually referred to the Privy Coun-
cil. The conviction was upheld, but it was recom-
mended that owing to the lapse of time the
sentence should not be executed. It is a coin-
cidence that Loth the Bertrand case and the Dean
case arose at North Shore, Sydney.
The Lynch rnurder-case, tried at Adelaide
Oct. sessions, and which ended in the conviction
of Geo. Lynch for the murder of Donald Ross,
was, perhaps, the “ biggest ” murder-case ever
tried in S. A. Lynch picked up Ross, a boy of 18,
in Ballarat in ’94, and, after knocking about Sydney
and Brisbane, they walked to Adelaide. At
Balaklava (S. A. ) they quarrelled and parted, but
Lynch openly threatened to “do for” the boy,
and wrote letters to the same effect which were
produced at the trial. One day in Aug. deceased
went to a ford to cart water, and some children,
hearing a gun-shot, ran to the scene and saw
Lynch deliberately shoot his mate with the second
shot. The chief witness was a little girl of nine
years, whose evidence could not be shaken. It is
believed there is something besides the quarrel
which prompted Lynch to put the boy out of the
way. Lynch is 50 years old, fairly well educated,
and was formerly in the navy, and it is a little
doubtful whether he is wholly sane. The last
S.A. murder case was that of Brown, who killed
his mate, Maroski, under similar circumstances.
Judge Boucant, who tried Lynch, lia& been on the
bench nearly 20 years, yet had never sentenced a
prisoner to death before.
The business deadlock that occurred the
other day between Sydney Fat Stock Buyers’
Association and an auctioneering firm, recalls a
comical Adelaide auctioneering incident. A well-
known firm which devoted itself chiefly to selling
wool and stud-sheep, found itself, on the an-
nounced date of an important sale, bound by un-
deniable engagements elsewhere. So it asked a
brother firm of fat-cattle auctioneers to kindly
send one of their salesmen to conduct this sale.
Now, the fat-cattle auctioneers had, of course,
butchers and a rough crowd of clients, and were
not particular in being lialf-an-hour 1 ite ; but the
clients of the wool and sheep-breeding firm were
sedate old pastoral magnates, with big bank
balances and mighty manners always on tap.
Well, the cattle-salesmen’s man, a burly, red-
faced, jolly-coiinteiianced fellow, strolled calmly
up to conduct the wool or sheep sale, lialf-an-hour
late, as was his wont with the butchers, sprang
into the rostrum, read out “Loti,” brought down
his gavel with a thump, and tooted out—“ Now,
then, you blanky, bhmky So-and-so’s, give us a
lurid bid, explode yer ! ” The dignified magnates,
after gazing speechlessly at each other for a few
seconds, stalked solemnly out, one after the other.
They were too paralysed even to protest against
the jolly-faced man’s rude language; while lie
was simply nonplussed, and ejaculated—“ What
the cardinal-tinted Paradise have I done now?”

For God still giretb His tieloved sleep,

A S.M. Herald correspondent asserts
that Erskine-street, Sydney, was named after the
lawyer-lord, Erskine. The street was actually
named after Col. Erskine, who was in Australia
with the 4Cth Regt. in 1816. Druitt-street was
named after the 48th Regt. major of that name,
and Wynyard-sqnare and street were named after
the major-general who commanded the troops in
Sydney in ’47.
A Chinaman bought a horse, saddle and
bridle for £5 10s. On his way home he had to
cross a few creeks which—thanks to the prayers
for rain—were running strong. The last was a
bit stronger than the others, and carried the horse
off his legs, capsizing the Chow. John managed
to get ashore, and, looking round, saw his pur-
chase in full sail towards the river. Overcome at
the loss, he burst into tears and exclaimed : “Goo
by horsey, goo by saddley, goo by blidley ! Go to
hell my fi pong ten.”
Dear Bulletin, —Re “The Breaker’s”
“drooping myalls” and “massy bloodwoods.”
The poet’s logic, as well as his botanical know-
ledge, is absolutely inverted. One swallow doesn’t
make a summer, even if what “The Breaker”
says in reply to rny criticism be correct. There
are, as we all know, botanical freaks in N.S. W.
and in other parts which have puzzled, and
still puzzle, the Von Muellers, and Baylcys,
and Tenison Woodses of Australia ; but because,
for example, tropical flora can be found in Wollon-
gong. Milton, and Bateman’s Bay districts, no
intelligent person dreams of peopling those regions
with all tropical plants, reptiles, or birds. The
impression that “ Breaker's ” “ pome ” was calcu-
lated to have upon ordinary readers was that it
was a common and general occurrence to find
pines, myalls, and bloodwoods growing together,
and this impression would be an utterly
false and misleading one. On Lake George,
N.S. Wales, seagulls—which, some few years
ago, were swept there from the Pacific by
easterly gales—are found in thousands, and are
yearly increasing in numbers. This fact, accord-
ing to “ Breaker’s ” logic, would convert Lake
George into a vast inland sea and people its
waters with whales, porpoises, sword-fish and
tiger-sharks! The bush “ pote ” is, from a
scientific standpoint, a fraud, a curse, and a
delusion—a reckless and lavish distributor of
everything that is scientifically absurd, outrageous
and untenable; and in this respect “Theßreaker”
is far from being the least culpable offender.—
Yours, &c., H. J. M’Cooey.

has been dispell* d. Having won confidence by si raight-

An Advertising Dodge.
You can’t always tell by the heading of an
article what it is. It may be an advertisement
for Warner’s Safe Cure, “a safe, sure, and positive
cure for diseases of the kidneys and liver, and
their developments,” and you do not read adver-
tisements if you know it, but you have read this
one so far, and ought to finish it, as it will only
take you thirty seconds to read the testimonial of
Mrs. Eliza Parkes, who has resided in Gisborne,
Victoria, for thirty years, which is witnessed by
the wife of the H.ev. Mcßride, Wesleyan Minister
of Kineton. She says ; “ About six years ago I
was taken very ill, and was confined to my bed
for several months, being attended by the doctor
who resided here at that time, whom I believe to
be a very clever and skilled physician, and most
certainly a very careful and attentive one. After
doing all he could for me, he gave me no hopes
whatever of my recovery, and stated that I was
suffering from diseased kidneys; the liver, he said,
was also in a very bad state, the action of the
heart very weak, and the blood very thick and
poor. My legs were swollen to an enormous size.
Having heard from friends about Warner’s Safe
Cure, I determined to try it. I bought one bottle
at a time, and f found it was doing me good, so I
continued taking it, and after taking several
bottles was able to get out of bed. Still continu-
ing the medicine, I soon became strong and able
to get about as well as ever. This I consider a
wonderful cure, as my age is now seventy-six
years, and I have not had the least symptoms of
the old complaint since I left off taking the medi-
cine, which was six years ago. I informed the
doctor that I was.using Warner’s Safe Cure, and
lie recommended me to continue, as he candidly
said it was a very good medicine.”*
Bella is a black Yenus at Roundstone
Island (Q.) 28 years of age, sft. sin. in height,

T. Blake, the Victorian cross-country
champion, is a wiry man, 22 years old, sft, Gin.
high, and with no particular development to indi-
cate his body’s endurance or his heart’s pluck.
Yet he is a signal performer over long distances ;
he won the two-miles championship in ’9l; the
three-miles scratch team’s race against Australia
in ’92; the same race again last year ; the Vic.
ten-miles cross-country championship in ’93, com-
ing home in 63min., and over a minute before
second man; and he won it again in ’94, with a
new record of 59min. 44sec., or 40sec. ahead of
second man. He holds the record for every Vic-
torian cross-country event; his time for the five-
miles cross-country championship is 29min. Isec.
He recently met Biggs, the N.S.W, cross-country
hero, in a three-mile race, and downed him.
Blake is captain of the Carlton (Melb.) football
team, with which he has played four years. He
is a good swimmer, too —has won several 100yds.
races. He doesn’t train over much, nor systemati-
cally, but is very careful about his diet. And he
comes of a family of athletes.
What was poisoner Dean’s motive ? He
apparently loved Mary Seymour during their
eight months’ courtship, when he came almost
every Sunday morning on his bicycle to breakfast;
and he loved her for a month or so after marriage,
but after that Mrs. Dean thinks he grew to hate
her, or to love some other woman. Yet, when in
gaol, he wrote his wife a letter of 10 sentences in
which he calls her “dear” or “darling” seven
times, and Mrs. Dean believes that he will
yet send for her and to her alone confess
the motive of his crime. Dean’s protestations
of love were doubtless hypocritical, for had he a
spark of affection he would not have allowed his
wife to sue him for maintenance after compassing
her death and escaping due retribution. No
suspicion of any kind attaches to Mrs.
Dean’s career, and Dean, on his own
statement, knew nothing of Mrs. Sey-
mour’s antecedents until they were unearthed
in evidence. Dean’s temper was sullen and un-
certain ; he would fly into a violent rage on the
slightest provocation and not speak to his wife
for a month after. This characteristic is familiar
to every man or woman who knows him well.
Dean’s father, a police-sergeant, committed suicide
because a fellow-sergeant captured a bushranger
whom both were seeking, and his mother and
some other relatives of his, according to appar-
ently reliable accounts, seem to be in low mental
health, but Dean himself never showed any
erratic tendencies. He was a strictly temperate,
unforgiving man, a hard worker, a faithful ser-
vant, a tyrannical husband, yet the last man,
according to general repute, whom his worst
enemy would have suspected of administering
poison.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
The Parisienne made a decent attempt to bring
off a surprise in the Caulfield Guineas, hut Cook’s mare
was “smothered” by Wallace as soon as Gough asked
him for an effort. Tims far Wallace appears the champion
of his year, and only the fact that Carbine’s lazy son is to
be raffled next week keeps punters from backing him at
even money for the V.R.C. Derby. Ail the same, there
are plenty of South Australians who swear by Auraiia as
firmly as ever.
With Hopscotch in the field, the Caulfield
Stakes might have been a fairly prophetic event. If Hova
can beat Hopscotch he will, with a fair run in his favor,
not lose the Caulfield Cup by much, if he doesn’t win the
race outright. Judging from appearances after their
bumping finish for the Stakes last Saturday, Atlas bad
travelled quite far enough, whilst Hova was fit to go
another half-mile without turning a hair. He is
in great form just now’. Of the horses that
ran nowhere in the Stakes, the writer takes Devon
and Trenchant to have chances for the Cup—
Devon because he is a real good, game animal, capable of
some improvement during this week, and Trenchant
because he is the very sort of little dandy to do well at
Caulfield, though lie looks somewhat stale and spiritless.
Size in the competitor seems to go for a good deal in this
race. Hova is rather tail and leggy for the small course,
W’ith its awkw’ard turns, when there are twenty or thirty
jockeys all trying to best one another. That’s the only
objection the writer can raise against Hova, who has, on
paper, an excellent chance to beat Hopscotch.
* ♦ » ♦
The Admiral has strong pretensions to the Caul-
field Cup, and if he runs for Cook’s stable he will not
start at longer odds than 8 to 1. The Heroine, a beautiful
mare, was talked of as being likely to beat the old Derby
winner at the weights, but she is seemingly short of
work and may not he ripe until Flemington meeting.
The Admiral is a handy horse, favorably handicapped on
his latest winning form. They can’t make any serious
mistake in a trid gallop, and so, taking one consideration
with another. Tub Bulletin will pick The Admiral to
beat Hova, Hopscotch, and Devon, and win the big
stake. But the state of the going next Saturday may
spoil the chances of these four heavy-weights.

Champion sculler Stanhury proposes to leave
for England in Feb. to row' Harding, who has challenged
him for £5OO and undertaken to pay Ids expenses. Toe
champion will take With him sculler Wray as trainer, and
it has been suggested to ex-champion Beach that if he
could get away from his farm and accompany Stanhury
to England it would ensure the success of the under-
taking. Since’9l, when Stanhury beat Sullivan on the
Parramatta, lie has not done any work in a boat, and at
one time iie scaled 17st. During the last year he has been
working hard, and is now in a state that would enable
him to get into proper condition in 10 weeks. By rights
the race should be rowed on rhe Parramat ta, hut, as Hard-
ing earns a good living coaching amateur crews in Eng-
land and on the continent, the idea of asking him to Aus-
tralia was abandoned.

Lovely woman’s muscle is not to be monopolised
by the bicycle, rowing being the latest craze of N.Y.
sassiety. What Melbournian of 10 years ago does not
remember I he summer evenings w'hen the dashing Emilia
Melville paddled down the Yarra? Emilie used to get a
pretty turn of pace out of tier boat, and, as far as
“form” was concerned, could give points to half the
oarsmen on the river. At ttie present time Twickenham
Ferry, Toorak, poss sses a feminine four—sisters named
Mitchell—which turns out at the Yarra regattas in
white flannel skirls and blue silk blouses, and generally
creates more public interest than all the racing boats put
together.

At the dawning of last summer a freelance
pressman who for years had been “ doing " cricket for a
certain newspaper, turned up at the office to arrange for
the forthcoming season. To his surprise he was Informed
that his services woulit be no longer required. Are you
dissatisfied with me?” he asked. “Not at all, on the
contrary, we congratulate you on the excellence of your
work, and we’ll irive you a testimonial to that eflecg but
the fact is ihe office has m ide other arrangements.” A
week or two later the journalist, who lives by the game,
di-coyered what the “other arrangements" were. To
save 80s. a week the work had been given to a civil
servant drawing over £4OO a year, who, for the sake of
posing as “ Snoozer, of the Stiffen,” and travelling round
matches on the nod, was writing without pay.

* • * •
That excellent judge, W. L. Murdoch, considers
Kumar Shri Ranjitsinhji, an Indian prince at Cambridge
University, the finest batsman now in England, not
excepting W. G. Grace. Unable to negotiate his Hindoo
appellation, his brother-players mostly call him “ Smith,”
while the crowd outside the chains has punned his name
into Ramsgate Jimmy. He will probably captain Sussex
next year.

“ Joe Goddard,” says a S. African paper, “ had
little difficulty in disposing of Sullivan ” (a local pugilist),
and “the contest throughout was conducted with rare
friendliness and good temper, the combatants inter-
changing various little pleasantries.” “And, as I was
saying, Joe”—(whoof) —“when we signed articles”—
(thud)—“ if I lost ’’—(smash)—'“ damn it, don’t inter-
rupt’’—(bang, bash, plunk). Time! We fancy we see it
all. Just think of Joj Goddard, boxing in earnest, being
friendly! Anyone who has ever seen J. Goddard with
his hair short, his eyeballs glaring, and blood dripping
from his face, cycloning round a ring, will appreciate the
humor of the situation.
* * •
Texas, U.S., has refused to allow Gorbett and
Fitzsimmons to fight within its boundaries. Texas is the
home of the wild-eyed cowboy of American literature, and
is the place where every year one half of the population
uses up the other half at revolver p actice, and yet they
won’t allow two mild'and peaceable men like Corbett and
Fitzsimmons to burr each other’s edges up a hit for pure
sport. We are deceived in Texas, and have no faith left
in New York comic papers.

Placke, knocked out by Barker in the recent
giants’ fight, continues to improve in Sydney Hospital,
and will, it is imped, be out within a month. At the
fight, one horse-trainer ejaculated in regard to Barker:
“ Aly oath ! ain’t lie long in Lire cannons 1” That’s what
tie is. There’s about a foot of hare bone and sinew up from
the ankle before the calf begins. He’s altogether a queer
shaped ’un and w ill never learn to fight.
But Foley said of Placke after the fight : “He’s
the finest man I ever saw’, and if I had him I’d make him
champion of rhe world. He’s never had a lesson, and yet
look how straight he drove that left most limes, and he’s
tire gamest man ever stepped into a ring."
Placke should get a big benefit. The poor
fellow sadly needs one.

Piles Cured. —Owing to the fact that numbers
of people think piles are incurable, the proprietor
of the new “Cannalin” remedies has given in-
structions that free samples and descriptive
pamphlets are to be sent to all applicants in the
colonies. Those who have obtained no relief,
even after years of treatment or from operation,
are specially invited to write to the depdt for
Australasia, 257 George-st., Sydney, N.S.W.*
W. E. Smith, the well-known Sydney and
Newcastle printer, has issued for the benefit of
his customers the most complete book of type-
faces ever brought out in Australasia. Many a
type-founder cannot show the variety of types
and ornaments which W. B. Smith stocks for the
purposes of his general printing-trade. The book
is a credit to Australian printing enterprise.
It has been decided to have St. Alban’s
lottery drawn on 24th inst. Numerous representa-
tions have been made to Mr. W. R. Wilson with
a view to having the drawing postponed until
after Cup-week, in the interests of ticket-holders
in distant parts ; and these received careful con-
sideration before the decision was made. Mr.
Wilson returned from Westralia on Friday and
was met in Melb. by Geo. Tattersall Adams.
After a brief conference it was concluded that
strict adherence to the announced date was the
course most likely to meet with the approval of
the majority of the subscribers, and this was
agreed to accordingly. The tickets are being
taken very rapidly.*
Every citizen who finds his hair fading
gently away like the beautiful consumptive child
in the Sunday-school book, and is oppressed by
the knowledge that the flies of next summer will
rink gaily on his shining scalp, should seize oppor-
tunity with both hands and try Parker’s Aus-
tralian Hair Preserver. This is a local invention,
constructed to suit the climate of Australia, where
the hair falls out mostly from causes unknown in
England, and which the English hair-restorer can-
not grapple with. It is endorsed by testimonials
from a number of well-known Sydney citizens
whose hair it has rescued from the brink of des-
truction, and snatched as a brand from the burn-
ing, so to speak.*

» • • *
Parsons (Vic.) rode in great form in Adelaida
last Saturday—winning the half-mile and ten miles inter-
provincial cycle-races, and twice healing Megson, the
N.S.VV. champion. Parsons, when he began racing, was a
terribly nervous rider ; the first time he rode in tlie Aus-
tral lie oame to the post “dead” from a night of in-
somnia.
• * • •
Megson downed D. Walker, of Victoria, in the
Adelaide half-mile. Walker is 20 years old, and in train-
ing appears to be the fastest rider in Australia. His per-
formances are sensational except in the races. He is dis-
cussed more than any other Victorian cyclist for his time-
trials and racing "bundle.” His only big win was the
one-mile scratch-race at St. Kilda two years ago. Some-
how lie forgot he was racing—came at the finish with a
splendid spurt and won upon the post. Walker s trainer
is Billy Williams, the Melbourne boxer, who thinks to
make his man a champion.
♦ • * •
Parsons is a splendidly-built 20-year-older who
first took to racing three years ago. It is Parsons whose 10-
mile championship at last Austral meeting, wherein he put
up a world’s grass-record, will he remembered long by all
who witnessed it, and it was he who, a month later,
knocked out Wally Kerr’s previous times and put up
Australian records for all distances from J to 10 miles on
the Exhibition, a 6-lap track.
♦ ♦ ♦ *
The eyes of the cycling world will be upon
Sydney track at the forthcoming Nov. meeting, when
Zimmerman (America), Megson, Lewis, O Brien, Simpson
(N.S.VV.), Parsons, Walker, KuMe (Vic.), Middleton (Q.),
Tom Busst, Davis (S. Aus.), and Boyd (M.L.), will bump
against the’ dark horse who has been waiting for those
two £lOO cash prizes.
Zimmerman races in Adelaide, 26th Oct., in
Brisbane, 9th and 11th Nov.; in Sydney, 16th and 23rd
Nov.; afterwards in Melbourne, Maorilaudand Westralia.
• • * *
Parsons’ time (Imin. 19|sec;) in the half-mile
Adelaide cycling.competition, shows that it was a waiting
race, and that, therefore, Megson hasn’t the finishing
sprint that Parsons and Lewis have. Megson was
“ pocketed,” but then it’s a very bad general who gets
pocketed in a time like that.
Megson’s mile in 2.11 3-5 was a great perform-
ance, more especially as the race was unpaced. It was
over six seconds better than any previous Australian
cycling competition mile.
The ten-mile race proves what has often been
contended—viz., that Megson cannot stay long at a hot
pace. Megson drew off after eight laps, although ins con-
dition was good. Previously, when Parsons met Megson
in a ten-mile contest, Megson had to draw off because he
raced himself nearly blind. Pither (M.L.) and Lewis
(N.S.W.) were also badly spread-eagled by Parsons; he
lapped Pither once, and Lewis twice.
• * * •
Zimmerman is going to hold a carnival “on his
own” in Melbourne first or second Saturday in Nov.
Afterwards he will compete at the Nov. meeting of the
N.S.W. League, which has increased its total prize-money
to £5OO.
* • • *
In Zimmerman’s Adelaide interview, some pos-
sible explanation is cast upon ’Murkan records. Says
Zim.: “Some of the American tracks have straight
quarters, halves, and even miles, and when you get on
one of these, with a fair wind behind you, you can bet
you travel.”
* • * *
Another version. Zimmerman, writes a Bul-
letin correspondent, is a delicate, effeminate-looking
young fellow, and is the last person one would take for a
cyclist. He is tall and thin as a match, and, like Parkes
and Mark Twain, sadly needs a hair-cut. In his ordinary
clothes “ Zimmy ” could easily be mistaken for an at tenu-
ated Robert Spalding visiting the kawlinies in search of
health. As a matter of fact, the champion did lose over
101 b. on the voyage, and looked far from well on reaching
Largs Bay.
In racing-togs, Zim. shows to better advantage.
He has good shoulders, and is surprisingly muscular for
a man of his build, but his legs—ah, there we have it.
They are long and sinewy. From hip to knee Zim.'s legs
are extraordinarily long, whereas, from knee to ankle,
they are peculiarly short—in fact, his knees almost touch
the handles when he is going easy. Were Zimmerman a
new rider—say an amateur taking a turn on the track for
the first time—he would not be treated seriously. He
looks ridiculous on his machine-resemhles a giraffe on
wheels—and when he is going steady the spectator
momentarily expects to see him fall off from sheer weak-
ness. But when he spurts—the long knees bob quicker,
the Parkesian hair floats in the breeze, and Zim. is half-
round the track in a few seconds.
Zimmerman, according to some people, is con-
sumptive, and was quietly ordered to Australia for his
health. The narration of the rumor amused him greatly.
“I am out here for my health, yes. lam also studying
the health of the Nimrod Cycle Co„ in which I am
interested.” _ < •
“Zim” is a great advocate of banking cycle
tracks. He was shown the track at Adelaide Jubilee
grounds, which is banked higher than any other track in
Australia, and has been pronounced dangerous by many.
The American was not surprised. “ You ought to have
it banked several feet higher still,” he said. “You will
never lower world’s records till you do.”
Zimmerman weighed himself on leaving the
steamer and scaled list. 71b., He decided not to
race in Adelaide on October 12 or 19, but said he
might do a half-mile sprint on the 26th, and a meeting
has been arranged for that date.
* ♦ * *
The bicycle problem of to-day is to find an un-
pniictnrable pneumatic tyre. One patented has strips of
whalebone inserted between the air tube and the outer
casing. Another suggestion is the preventer, consisting
of a hand of steel with a serrated edge fastened between
tlie bicycle forks so as to skim the tyre as it revolves and
thus scrape off all foreign bodies.
Melb. cycling academies are rushed, at present,
by foolish virgins and dilatory matrons, who have post-
poned their initiation into wheel craft until the eleventh
hour. Private teachers with Dikes of their own are adver-
tised for daily, for “sassiety” means to pedal its way into
Lady Brassey’s affections or perish in the attempt. The
two “leading ladies” of Melbourne are pushed through
their paces daily in company with a host of inferior lights,
who follow tlie road regardless of age or weight.
* * * *
The bike-boom is a sort of bioyclone. Ib cer-
tainly is carrying everything before it.

Both Argus and Age have now started a
cycling column. * * *
Chicago has produced another new thing in
bikes. An ordinary safety has a crossbar where the
Saddle should be, with a seat at each end of it, and double
p-dals so that the riders sit abreast instead of tandem.

In Tasmania, the only association that makes
the chips fly is the United Axemen’s Association, This
combination of choppers is out with a £5OO programme
for Nov. 27. Axemen residing in any of the other provinces
are allowed in at half-entrance-fee, and competitors from
Canada or other foreign countries have the right of free
•utrv and a liberal travelling allowance.
* * ■ * *
Comparing the times of Australian interpro-
vincial eight-oar contests with those of (he English
University races, one would prima-facie conclude that
the pick of Vic. and N.S.W. oarsmen were laughably
inferior to the Oxford and Cambridge crews. But Aus-
tralian interprovincial races are rowed at a fixed hour on
a given date without reference to the state of the tide,
whereas in England the ’varsity crews await tlie full flow
when the Thames is running at ahnut 12 miles an hour
*' on its own,” and not until then they launch their boats.

Melb. obituary : Cox, of Moonee Valley. Nob
exactly the pioneer, but the first really successful ex-
ploller of the Melb. gate-money meetings. Incurred a
good deal of odium for thus following English precedent,
hut the V.A.T.U. came round to imitating, and the V.R.O.
is beginning.

* « * •
When Earl Rosebery and party were on a
Riverine station the boss promised them a bnekjumping
exhibition, and arranged with the well-known rider,
‘‘Sydney Bob,” to be on hand in the morning. Bob
appeared dressed for the occasion—new cords, red shirt,
and cabbage-tree. At the first buck he was over the
mare’s head, second, ditto, and the English party laughed
heartily. Away rushed Bob into the creek up to his
waist. Then he showed them in earnest how to sit a
“devil.” He had forgotten to wet his new pants.

George Giffen’s little brother Walter made 103
against the leading Adelaide junior club recently.

Donald Dinnie’s son, just turned 21, is perform-
ing hitherto-unprecedented feats of strength in the U.S.
and, inter alia, lifts a live pony above his head with one
arm. He is said to lie far better than the old man when
the latter was at his best.

Zimmerman says that Lewis, theN.S.W. cyclist,
is the best-built man he ever saw for racing, and is far
ahead of Megson in this respect. Zim. thinks that
Lewis’s mile record, 2min. Bsec., is a great performance,
and that, it properly paced, Lewisshould do a mile in 1.57.

Melb. will shortly be overrun with “wheels.”
3000 “bikes” are on the water, shipped by a Canadian
firm in view of the Brassey boom. Vic. consignees are
instructed to sell at £9 apiece.

Coroner Youl : “Starter Geo. Watson told me
years ago that when he saw ladies crossing the street
when he was driving near them, he pulled on one side,
closed his eyes, and said as much as he could recollect of a
short prayer."

In recognition of the services rendered by the
two Melb. hospitals during the last football season in
patching up disabled players, the Vic. Junior Football
Association, a numerous and prosperous body, has voted
these institutions a guinea each.
* * * *
Abe Willis, though well on to 40 years, is as
spry and wiry as ever, and can get all over any boxer, no
matter how big or how ugly. His go with “little Peter
Jackson ” at Foley’s, the other evening, was as full of fire
and excitement as a dog-fight. “Little Peter” can box
himself, but he thought it was raining brick-stuffed
boxing-gloves.

the race-scene coming on ?” The number of Jew's on the
stage in the early part of the play had made him think it
was a betting-ring.
