Recent statistics show that double parental
alcoholism invariably leads to drunkenness in the
olfspring, and frequently to fatal epilepsy ; also,
ep leptic drinking parents invariably have epileptic
children. Alcoholism in the parents also conduces
to phthisis in both parents and children. Further,
the drunken son of a drunken father is invariably
madly violent in his cups.
For rheumatism use Colemane and Sons
Enualypte Special Oil.*
“Scientific temperance” is now part of
the official curriculum of Tasmanian State
schools. What is “ scientific temperance?”
The writer remembers an Australian parson
who, at a mission feast of young folk,
alter a tea-fight, gave the audience some
“scientific temperance.” Holding an egg up
in one hand and a phial in another, the shep-
herd spake like this: “This is an egg. This is
alcohol. Now, the white of egg is albumen.”
With this he cracked the egg, dropping the con-
tents into a glass, and removing the yolk. Every-
body was interested, especially a reporter, sadly
and most obtrusively drunk. “This, then, is
albumen. Now, the brain of man is almost
entirely composed of albumen. I intend to show
you what effect alcohol has on albumen, and,
therefore, on the human brain.” “ Ho, ho,”
hiccoughed the inebriated reporter from just under
the platform. “ Will our friend there keep quiet,
please?” (Pours some alcohol on the white-
of-egg, which gets muddy, of course.) “ There,
that’s the state of a man’s brain when he
has taken one glass of spirits and is feeling
what he calls ‘merry.’” Applause. “Bosh!”
from the illuminated scribe. “ Will our friend
bear with me?” (Pours some more spirit on the
albumen, which thickens.) “ That’s the condition
of a man’s brain when he wants to lend everybody
money.” “Good man!” ejaculated the excited
reporter. “ ‘ Good man !’ says our friend. Well ”
—(pours on some more spirit, and albumen gets
more turbid) —“ that’s the condition of the
drinker’s brain after three glasses, when he wants
to quarrel with his best friend. „ “Rot!” from
the now infuriated press representative. “I’m
sorry if my illustration annoys our friend; he
evidently knows something about these things.”
“Yah!” from the reporter. (Parson pours on
more spirit, and explains, with interruptions,
until he gets to the sixth driblet, when the
albumen is all found coagulated.) “ And
that’s the condition of a man’s brain,” screams
the parson, triumphantly, “ when he’s drunk
six glasses and goes home and kicks his
wife and jumps on his children !” Sensation
and cheering. The wild reporter is on his legs,
howling—“l don’t mind fair argumensh, hie!
But shish ish dam humbug, an’ you—hie—know it.
You know it —know it —know it I you do (hie) !”
Parson (sarcastically) : “Perhaps our friend would
like to come up on the platform and debate the
subject? If so, if lie will keep quiet till I’ve con-
cluded my remarks, we shall, I’m sure, all be
pleased to give him a hearing.” “Will yer ?”
says the reporter, eagerly. “Oh, yes.” “ Itsher
wazsher!” And he actually kept still till the end
of the lecture. Then he staggered up the side
steps of the platform. There was tolerable silence,
only broken by laughter, as he made a drunken
bow, and then went to a platform back-door
where the “washing-up” of the tea-fight was
going on. “ You’d better shing a hymn—
hie—insher meantime ; I’ll be back dreckly,”
said he, and disappeared. “We will sing a
hymn,” said the chairman. “ I think our
friend has gone to wash himself.” (Loud laughter
at the humorous idea.) But the reporter, evi-
dently screwed up for the occasion, reappeared
with a kettle in his hand, and another egg. And
this was his lecture : “My de-ah bruzzers an*
shishters. Shiah ish hot water —hie. Shish is an
egg.” (Breaks it into a tumbler, and, after some
trouble, manages to scoop out the yolk). “ You’ve
heard all about —hie —sher human brain; also—hie
—’bout alcoll ! Well, shish ish sher shtate of sher
human brain after —hie—one doshe of—hie—hot
water !” (Fours in a few drops of hot water, and
the white of egg goes turbid.) Then, right on to
the bitter end of coagulation of albumen by heat,
mimicking the parson, till : “ And shat’s sher
shtate —hie—of the teetotallersh brain after shix
glasshes of hot water—hie—when he goesh home
to kick his poor wife !” Only he had to scream the
last part, because the leaders of the meeting had
got the girl at the harmonium to start that instru-
ment a-going.

SEND FOR TESTIMONIALS.
Tho old Melb. firm of W. H. Glen and
Co., music-sellers, died of “effluxion of time,”
the other week, and another W. H. Glen and Co.
reigns in its stead. This firm, built in accordance
with the will of the original Mr. Glen, consists of
his old partner, Mr. John Kemp, a new William
Glen, and three others. The new firm, in accord-
ance with ancient business custom, has swallowed
the entire stock, fixtures and book-debts of the old
firm. Also, it is fulfilling an ancient law of com-
mercial nature by selling things at a somewhat
alarming sacrifice for the next month or so. A
new firm, like a new monarch, loves to inaugurate
its career in a blaze of generosity. Even the book-
debts are granted a free pardon when there’s no
perceptible chance of turning them into cash.
Dear Bulletin, — A recent par. refers to
“the Edward Irving who did philosopher ihomas
Carlyle out of his best girl.” Now, Carlyle gob
that girl (Miss Jane Welsh); and Irving, there-
fore, instead of doing, was done. —J.R.I.
At the opening Dean-agitation meeting at
North Sydney, Parson Bennett-Anderson said :
“ I looked into George Dean’s eyes and I saw
that he was innocent.” The poor, deluded parson
went on to say that he had addressed many thou-
sands in England on behalf of Mrs. Maybrick. If
lie looked into her eyes he would certainly have
seen that she was innocent, too. Mrs. May brick s
eyes would convince almost anybody.
Subscribers to the Echo Farm (Sydney)
Home for Inebriates should obtain and study
■barrister Field’s report as arbitrator in the recent
dispute between Mr. Robert Hobson, late manager
of the home, and Mr. Courtenay Smith, the
“honorary director.” The Bulletin hopes yet
to see in Sydney an inebriates’ home, under intel-
ligent medical management and supervised by
Government. Pious laymen and laywomen of the
unscientific and unimaginative teetotal-faddist
type must inevitably botch such an enterprise.
Psalms aren’t an in variable antidote to the whisky-
habit, and many dipsomaniacs are far too high in
the scale of intelligence to find comfort in the com-
pany of the average parson and his hopelessly stupid
surroundings.

LONDON, Sept. 23.
Mr. H. Morris, of Wanganui, New Zealand, has passed
first in the examination for admission to the Indian Civil
Service. Mr. Morris was 1000 marks ahead of the next
candidate.

In the examination of candidates to fill vacancies in the
Indian Civil Service 16 Maoris from Wanganui, New
Zealand, have passed with first place honors.
The Maoris were 1000 marks ahead of the other candi-
dates.
Australian authors are lively. Rolf
Boldrewood has just published “ A Crooked
Stick,” Mary Gaunt (Mrs, Lindsay Miller) ‘‘A
Pointing Finger,” “Jim” Hogan “The Sister
Dominions,” and Ethel Turner “The Family of
Misrule.” Miss Turner also leads off Ward,
Lock, and Bowden’s new “Nautilus Series ” with
“ The Story of a Baby.” Louis Beoke has brought
out “His Native Wife,” and promises another
about Christmas. Also Henry Lawson, and
“ Jacky ” O’Hara are about to issue volumes of
verse 5 and “ Banjo” Paterson’s 1 Man from
Snowy River ”is published to-day. The greater
part of the edition (1000 copies) has been sold in
advance.

Michael Davifct thinks that the prospects
of Home Rule for Ireland were not seriously
dimmed by the result of the recent British elec-
tions. They couldn’t well be less bright than
when Rosebery ruled on sufferance.
Cornelius Herz, a French savant, claims
to have discovered a method of cabling upwards
of 100,000 words per minute, instead of 20, as at
present ; and declares that when his invention is
applied it will be possible to send 50 words from
London to Australia for so that letters will
be practically done away with.

Verses.” It will be a handsome book, and the
price will be ss. The original edition (which will
include some verses hitherto unprinted) will con-
sist of one thousand copies only. Post-free from
Bulletin office, ss. 6d.
One of Bellamy’s ideals has reached real-
isation at Budapest, in Hungary, where a tele-
phonic newspaper speaks to (5000 subscribers all
day long, and in the evening regales them with
current Parliamentary debates and opera music
brought to every man’s armchair.

A recent cheque-trouble shows how readily
Australians will accept a man as a “lord” if he
only has the front to assume a title. After all,
one can hardly blame the public for the plentiful-
ness of temporarily-successful impostors with
bogus titles. Titled people are now so common in
England that even if a man has regularly read the
English papers for 20 years he can never take up a
print without seeing the names of two or three
“noblemen ” of whom he had never heard before.
If G. Republican Dibbs never did another
useful thing, he would still deserve the gratitude
of humanity for the lift he has given Townsville.
Before Dibbs’s recent visit the inhabitants of that
township used to sit in a melancholy row on their
dfi4oo,ooo breakwater, and beckon “Dilly, dilly,
come and be killed ” to the ships which cautiously
kept three miles away in the bay, out of moat of
the danger from rocks and shallows. Before
Dibbs’s visit Townsville people hung down their
heads whenever Ross Creek was mentioned, and
hastily changed the unsavory topic. But now !
Dibbs has said the breakwater is better than that
of Colombo. Dibbs says continual dredging is a
trifle —“ why, we dredge Sydney harbor.” Dibbs
has planned plazas, and sea walls, and oriental
gardens. And now, if you whisper “Ross Creek”
to a Townsville man, he knocks you down. While
the rival port of Bowen howls disgustedly. Why
doesn’t Bowen invite Parkes up to boom Port
Denison ?
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods; 6, Toys; 7, Perfumery ; 8, Pictures; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornament Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half a million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half a mile
long."

Councillor Fleming is the Ishmael of
Brunswick (Vic.) corporation. The other day, a peti-
tion, signed by 32 ratepayers, was presented, ask-
ing the Mayor to convene a public meeting re the
sweating question, whereupon Fleming “ ventured
to assert that many of those who signed the peti-
tion had never ‘sweated ’ in their lives—they were
too lazy.” Eggs for Fleming at next election.

Bards of the Back-blocks.
PROSPECTOR AND FOSSICKER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Click, click, the hammer rings
On the drill with measured sway;
“ Quick, quick,” the miner sings
Till to-day is yesterday;
All the woodland whispers “ Hope,”
The rugged hills hurl back “Despair.”
A tell-tale shot—a length’ning rope—
A misty candle—“ Nothing there.”
“ Ha, ha, ha ! ” the jackass cries,
Watching from a withered bough,
“Gold’s a god in those things' eyes,
I must see a lizard now.”
QUERCUS.
POLYPHEMUS AND GALATEA.
(With apologies to the shade of Tennyson).
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Come out, O Miss, of yonder ’ornestead proud :
Wot I can’t get inside (the shearer sang)—
Inside the shoulder cold they’d give your Bill!
So cease to live within the ’ornestead, cease
Thy sunbeam-soapy smiles to waste on pa,
To stand the stare of Jackeroo Esquire ;
And come and mash me in the mallee, come
And mash me in the mallee, roll thou up
And join me on the snug selection, me,
Whose ’ands and land a “ bit ” will always raise,
Who never blues his cheque amid the vats,
But, ant-like, saveth all; so ne’er need walk
Like snails, and swaggies on their uppers borne.
Lor ! the fust time your ravin’ locks I seen
Down dropped my ’art, as dropped the fleece of
snow
That all about me ringer-cloven fell,
The tally-torrent from my dusky paws :
D’ye fuller? Come, O come, a peg come down,
Live with me in the mallee, let the wild
Bald-headed boss be wild (my troubles !) —leave
The crawling jackeroo to mop and swill,
And watch the smoke-wreaths from his cigarette
Fade, like ’is last remittance, from ’is sight:
So fade thou, too, and come where my love-tales
Await thee; feather pillers, my sweet-art,
I buys for thee ; thy shearer calls thee, I,
I and my pipe (and sweet as it’s bin found
Unto my lips, sweeter thy lips would be),
Come —or, hard-hurrying like a sheep just shorn,
Will issue droves from my tonsorial gums
Of really quite innumerable B’s.
PIERRE CASSEE.
AN EXTINCT VOLCANO.
(A Tale of a Public House Bar.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was tall, his skin was hard and brown and
rough;
His nose was large and red—the reason why
I think you’ll guess; his voice was coarse and
gruff,
His endless aim to “ wet the other eye.”
He spoke of cheques that he had earned and
smashed,
Of pounds as many men would speak of pence;
Of “boxing blokes” whom he had “licked ” and
“ lashed,”
Of scores whom he had killed in self-defence.
He’d seen the time when he had “ wiped the
floor ”
With two stout “ bobbies ” and a “ tracker
bloke.”
Then coolly asked if “ there were any more
Who’d like to have their blanky noses broke?”
Brave deeds of daring, too, he could recount
Of rough horse-riding on the wild Barcoo,
Where tearing outlaws by the score he’d mount
And stick to every one like patent glue.
At shearing, too, his “centuries ” he’d clipped ;
At fencing sunk his fifty holes in clay;
His twenty yards of muck he’d moved and
“shipped ”
Quite frequently within the eight-hours’ day.
He’d like to see the bloomy, crimson blank
Who’d dare to hint as he’d been spoutin’ lies—
He was so tall, and broad, and lean, and lank,
That no one cared upon the point to rise.
Just then a small and weazened-looking youth
Of twenty summers—maybe, somewhat more,
With manners quiet, nor at all uncouth,
Stepped to the bar from out the bar-room door.
He called for ale, and laid his sixpence down,
Was just about to quaff the sparkling beer,
When Rednose cried out, with an angry frown :
“Young man, we want no ‘Jimmy Woodsers’
here.”
The fire flashed fiercely from the youngster’s eyes,
When Rednose answered with a wry grimace,
Then both to his and our intense surprise
The beer was dashed full in his ugly face.
A lion seeking whom he may devour,
A tiger loosed from out his prison cage,
Wild knights of old entombed within the tower,
But scarce portray the angry “Long ’un’s ”
rage.
A living earthquake now whose mission dire
Is death ; he strides towards his puny foe—
An active, fierce, volcanic living tire,
Resolved to end the matter with one blow.
Alas ! that we should reck without our host
Or count our chicks before the eggs are hatched;
At least, when we aspire to “clear the coast,”
• We first should mind we are not over-matched.
As madly on the fiery meteor came
To “squelch” the youth with one almighty
blow ;
The latter, who had practised at the game,
Just stepped aside —then let his right hand go.
A gurgling noise, a groan, a “ sickening thud,”
A huge brown carcase laid upon the floor,
A red nose redder still with drops of blood,
A conquered drunken bully—nothing more.
JNO. C. HUMPHRIES.

“In Divorce!"
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They were three—and you might have
labelled them, in the jargon of the fortune-teller,
the fair man, the dark man, and the red woman.
But the combination had violated tradition. The
dark man was the traitor—not the fair; he was
the “ injured husband,” who, with one forgetful
hand still grasping the tail of the dead wallaby
slung over his shoulder, stood motionless in the
doorway and gazed at the other two.
For half-a-minute there was silence, more inten-
sified than broken by faint profundity of far-away
thunder that appropriately sounded—the thought
crossed Darkeuday swiftly—the sinister keynote
of the situation. Then, as he noted the direction
both of the woman’s eyes and of the man's, he
looked down in his turn at his own right hand,
and became conscious that his Winchester was in
it. “Ha!" he said —let the wallaby’s tail slide
gradually through his Angers, and slowly brought
the piece across his body.
Darn end ay had never been a man to be compre-
hended by the woman who had apparently now
been making “ other arrangements,” but instinct,
always acute in inverse ratio to intelligence,
warned her that in this frigid deliberateness of
action dwelt more danger than in any sign of
swiftly-moving passion.
“No, no!” she said. “For God’s sake, no
shooting ! That won’t mend matters.”
She was of the large and luscious type, with
bounteous curves, and the milky skin that mostly
goes with lush red hair. Handsome —more than
handsome!—in her way, but far less of Nature’s
mate for her husband than for the burly, curly,
black-eyed nincompoop at her elbow. As she had
apparently discovered.
Darkenday—he was a lean, brick-faced man
with a contemplative grey eye—laughed a little
under the hand which he took from the ride to
stroke his short, hay-colored beard. “ That’s
hard sense, anyhow—even if it does come from the
mouth of a—well, never mind !” he broke off,
drawing a stool across the hut-doorway and sitting
down upon it, with the ride across his knees and
a curious, placid inexorableness upon his face.
“ Calling names won’t mend matters, either—
that’s sense for sense ! And, now, the question
is—what do you mean to do—you and this—l said
I wouldn’t call names—this gay dog with the
nicely-pomaded hair. Scent on its handkerchief,
too, no doubt, amongst its other charms !”
The emphasis on the word “ dog ” went home.
Gotch—a bush-dandy of the drst water, with
parti-colored riding-trousers and a liberal native
impudence-recovered his grip of that quality.
Besides, he was three inches over and ten years
under this thin, dry man in the red shirt and
ancient cabbage-tree hat.
“Just you put your bloomin’ gun away, and
I’ll blanky well show you the kinder dog I am !
If you was the sorter bloke that was dt to have a
wife like Carrie here, you’d put your bloomin’
’ands up like a blanky man, instead of sittin’
there an’ talkin’ shoot.”
Darkenday, still stroking his beard, surveyed
him with an unmoved eye.
“ I said nothing about shooting. It was
‘ Carrie, here,’ who was so quick to understand
what she deserves —and you! According to the
common way of thinking, that is. But, fortu-
nately for the extremely precious pair of you, I’m
not of the common way of thinking.”
“ Put away your gun, then ! ” Gotch advanced
a step anxious, after his kind, to exhibit to the
female animal his brute superiority over a smaller
and weaker male.
“ And make another dog of myself ? ” Darken-
day said “ to contend for the favor of the —urn !
I said I wouldn’t call names, didn’t I ? ”
His eye, resting steadily upon the woman’s full-
davored charms, supplied well enough the “ miss-
ing word; ” but, too coarse-witted to feel the full
contemptuous weight of the undnished metaphor,
she merely forced herself to give look for look.
“ Harry Gotch is my man ! ” she said, dedantly.
“ That is what I was coming to ! ” Darkenday
answered, as a sudden blaze of lightning dlled the
doorway with a pale-blue background to his black
judicial dgure. “Ha ! ” and he turned his head,
“ it will be a suitable night for the new nuptials !”
“Nuptials?” echoed Mrs. Darkenday, not
assured of the meaning of the word.
“ Ay ! For your second wedding, you know !
with the man—or, is it the dog ? —of your choice ”
For the drst time Darkenday laughed outright,
with a sound dtly chorused by a sharp rattle of
close-at-hand thunder. “Harry Gotch is your
man, you say.” Here he sprang suddenly to his
feet. “ Then let Harry Gotch take you—and
go ! ”
“But”—from the woman. “Look here!”
from the one man. And then, with strident in-
sistance, from the other : “LetGotch take you, I
say ’’—another crash of thunder—“ and go ! ”
Silence, then, but for the big drops striking
sullenly upon the bark-sheets of the roof, till,
by the next eye-searching glare, the scarcely-
comprehending lovers saw that the owner of the
hut had crossed it with a rapid step. The door—
and the storm—were free to them.
“You have made a second choice, woman,”
Darkenday said, more slowly, “ as you had a per-
fect right to do. Therefore, /do not quarrel with
it —hut therefore, also, you must abide by it, as far
as lam concerned. Do you understand ? ”
Only the thunder answered, and after it came
Darkenday's voice again—level now and steady as
if he were reading an inscription.
“You have made, I say, a second choice. That
is your business! Mine is to take care that
Gotch's darling does not remain another night
under Darkenday’s roof! You shall have half-
an-hour ! ”
He took a wallaby-skin from the wall, deliber-
ately breeched the ride with it, and went forth
into the wildness of the night.
* * »
“ Harry! ”
“Well?”
“ Do you think he means it ?
“By God! doesn’t he? He means that
shootin’-iron to be in order when he comes back !”
“I,’ after a long pause, “I should know him
better than you do ! ”
You should ! ” with a ferocious blasphemy.
“You should!—but, by God, you didn’t!—else
you’d never have caught me in this ”
“Goon!” she said, and her voice had taken
the ring of contempt intolerable to a man whose
only criterion of manliness is a woman’s estimate
thereof. In which case there is always a screw
loose even in the animal courage with which it is
the pride of the brute-approaching human to
make, after all, an indifferent second to the bull-
terrier or the gamecock.
“ Look here, Carrie,” Gotch said presently,
with all the stiffening gone out of his voice, “if I
clear out now I reckon you’ll be able to patch it
up with him all right, won’t you V
Mrs. Darkenday’s laugh lashed him like a
whip.
“ Begone !” she cried, “ and save your cowardly
carcase ! That is what you are thinking of —not
of the woman who mistook you for a man !”
And Gotch slunk out into the rain.
• # •
“You are wet!”
“Pretty well —but the rifle isn’t! Where’s
your—friend ?”
Mrs. Darkenday, as she lighted the lamp, saw
something reassuring in her husband’s face.
“ He’s gone,” she said.
“For good?”
“ Faugh ! —yes ! Can you forgive me ?”
Darkenday stood his Winchester in a cornel
and began to pull off his wet boots before he said,
as quietly as if he were not quite sure what she
was talking about —“ Forgive you, Carrie ? What
for ?”
She came and put her hand upon his soaked
shoulder.
“ For not knowing a man from a mangy cur !’
“ Ah, well,” said Darkenday, fumbling forth
the pipe of peace, “ I daresay you thought he was
a lot more in your style than I am. But, if you’re
of a different opinion now, we’ll—well, we’ll go on
as before. ‘ Perfect accord shall never be ; but
less may oil the wheels of life ! ’ he muttered, as
he began to cut up tobacco.
Mrs. Darkenday heaved up her statuesque
shoulders in inarticulate protest against the
abstract. “ What did you mean,’ she said,
“ when you gave me half-an-hour to lie gone 1 ”
“ What I said ! It was a decree nisi, but we
won’t make it absolute.”
“But, look here, Tom,” she said, only half-
understanding. “ Tell me this. If you had found
us together when you came back, would you have
shot us ? ”
He looked up quickly at her over his shoulder.
“ Put yourself in my place !’ he said. “ Try to
be me for a moment. Would you ? ”
Her large red mouth flattened and paled.
“ Wouldn’t I! ’ she said, more to herself than to
him.
“Ah, well,” he said, “thats the gentler sex,
you see. I wouldn’t! ”
ALEX. MONTCOMERY.

“ The Baron de Worms has been offered
and has accepted a peerage.”— Cable.
This will not astonish the everyday folk
Who travel through life in the steerage,
They accept as a chestnut the cablegram joke
That Worms may be found in the peerage.
That flatulent monstrosity, the Imperial
Federation League (Melb.), has decided to start a
band. To be in keeping the band should be com-
posed wholly of wind instruments. The Bulle-
tin long believed that the League had suspected
itself to be a circus.

Farnham, over which he spent many thousands,
that he might render it a more worthy abode for
the future occupants of the historic See of Win-
chester.” The princely residence at Farnham !
“But the Son of Man hath not where to lay His
head.”
Editor Bulletin, The “ Pastoralists’
Union” and “ Govt. Labor Bureau ” are respon-
sible for much destitution amongst bona-tide
bushmen in this district during the present
season. The former sends men from Sydney,
armed with a P.U. ticket, on which is printed
“ Help those who help you ; ” and men presenting
these are taken on in preference to ordinary bush-
men every time. The latter gives free passes
to men who are prepared to work for whatever
wages offer. This being so, the ordinary bush-
hand, who hitherto was always sure of a job at
shearing-time, now walks about idle and almost
starving. The Sydney men, as soon as shearing**
over, return to the city to live on Govt, charity.—
W. Condobolin, N.S.W., l/10/’95.
The Sydney Sunday Times’ pen-lady dis-
courses on the congenial theme “ Are tights im-
moral ” :
I heard some clever people discussing the other night
the question of the immorality orotherwise of tights. Some
held that it is immodest and immoral for a handsome and
shapely young woman to display her charms in flesh-
colored apparel, and that those who go to the halls and
theatres where young ladies show their legs are equally
immoral and immodest. This almost unrestricted display
of lovely woman's attractiveness was said to corrupt the
minds of callow youths by its suggestiveness. We were a
party of ladies, and I must confess that I delicately intro-
duced the subject. The principal concern of most of iis
was for the men. I remarked that a girl had a perfect
right to wear tights if she thought fit.' That her lovely
legs were her own, a gift from the Creator, and that the
wearing of clothes was, after all, a mere conventionality.
Not that 1 believe in shattering conventionalities, but it
is well to remember their existence in considering sucG
questions. I don’t see anything in the wearing of tights
to corrupt men.
And then the lady goes on to discuss the necessity
for a large maternity home in Sydney.
Dear Bulletin, —“ H. J. M‘Cooey ” uses
his own experience as a standard of measuring the
possibilities of all created things, including the
vegetation of this continent. With a premature
war-whoop he attempted to scalp a poet for
locating bloodwood, myall, and pine trees in the
same region. Now, in Western Queensland, the
bloodwood (E. Oorymbosa), the myall (acacia pen-
dula), and the cypress pine (callitris robusta) are
friends and neighbours over a large area. With
a fine vein of self-deluded sarcasm, Mr. M‘Coney
anticipates that someone will now tell us that he
has seen the lyre-bird and the emu playing
together ! Let me whisper that I saw that impro-
bable scene on two occasions. The last was in the
drought of 1878. A lyre-bird (Menura Alberta)
had come down from the scrub on the range to a
small waterhole in a clump of gums (E. hcema-
stoma), and two emus were also there in search of
water. The menura was running round a gum-
tree and the astonished emus were following him
with heads down and necks stretched to the
extreme limit. I saw a similar scene on another,
occasion, but one is sufficient to knock out Mr,
M‘Gooey’s theory.—A. Mkston.
